Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Principle of Shubha and Ta'zir in Hudud
A legal-philosophical refutation of the objection to hudud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a hadd punishment is dropped upon the existence of any doubt (shubha) and replaced by a discretionary ta'zir punishment.

Core Idea
A single doubt drops the hadd; the judge imposes ta'zir at discretion.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa addresses a common criticism that hudud punishments are impracticable today. The author responds by emphasizing the built-in flexibility of Sharia: hudud are not absolute, but conditioned by strict proof and suspended by the mere presence of doubt (shubha), based on the prophetic command: 'Avert hudud by doubts.' When the hadd is dropped, it is replaced by a ta'zir punishment at the judge's discretion. This principle ensures that hudud are rarely applied, contrary to the misconception of rigidity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The claim is that hudud cannot be applied in modern times due to harshness or impracticality. The passage rebuts this by highlighting the conditions and flexibility.
The author frames the objection as 'in their view'.
2
Extract the Hanafi principle of shubha
The hadith 'ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات' is cited. The principle: any doubt (shubha) on the part of the criminal causes the hadd to be dropped. Hanafis interpret shubha broadly, including doubts in contract, act, or ownership.
Shubha is a preventer (mani) for the application of hadd.
3
Apply the rule: substitution by ta'zir
When a hadd is dropped due to shubha, it is not simply abandoned; it is replaced by a ta'zir punishment determined by the judge according to the circumstances.
This shows the continuity of punishment, just not the fixed hadd.
4
Connect to modern application
In contemporary contexts, this principle allows judges to avoid physical hudud whenever there is any uncertainty, substituting imprisonment or fines instead.
The example of theft in Saudi Arabia is given to show that when applied, hudud can be highly deterrent.

الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه

المشكلة الثانية : تطبيق الحدود.
هذه المشكلة هي (في نظرهم أنَّ بعض العقوبات البدنية المحدّدة شرعاً (الحدود) كعقوبة الزاني بالجلد أو الرجم وعقوبة السارق بقطع اليد أصبحت غير ممكنة التطبيق في هذا العصر وما استجد فيه من الملابسات. والجواب عن ذلك : أنَّ الحدود في الشريعة ولا سيما حد الزنى مشروطة بشرائط ضيّقة جداً في إثباتها وتطبيقها تجعلها نادرة التطبيق فهي تسقط أو تتبدل إلى عقوبة تعزير يقدرها القاضي بمجرد وجود شبهة لدى المجرم لقول الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم : (ادرؤو) الحدود بالشبهات.
ومن جهة أخرى يوجد في الشريعة باب واسع هو قاعدة الضرورات وما تسوّغه شرعاً من تدابير استثنائية موقوتة تشمل ترك الواجب وفعل الممنوع عندما توجد ضرورات ملجئة.
وعقوبات الحدود على الجرائم الاجتماعية هي خمس فقط وهي: حد الزنى والسرقة وقذف المحصنات من النساء، وشرب الخمر والحرابة وهي قطع الطريق للسلب والنهب) (۱) . أما ما سواها من عقوبات التعزير على جميع أصناف الجرائم فكله في نظام الإسلام مفوّض إلى الحكام بحسب مقتضيات الحال .
فإذا لوحظ أن تطبيق عقوبة الحدود الخمسة قد أصبح متعذراً في زمان أو مكان تُطَبَّق عندئذ عقوبة أخرى، ولا يوجب هذا ترك الشريعة جمعاء. على أن عقوبة السارق بقطع اليد لا ينبغي أن نتصور فيها عدداً من الأيدي مقطوعاً بعدد السارقين الذين يلوعون المجتمعات البشرية اليوم باستمرار، بل إنَّ يداً واحدة تكفي لمنع آلاف وإن مجرد تطبيق هذه العقوبة في المملكة العربية السعودية في بضع حوادث قد قطع دابر السرقات وأنتج أمناً داخلياً مذهلاً ومثالياً في العالم.
ولزميلنا الدكتور صبحي المحمصاني، وهو قاض جنائي في الأصل، بحث قيم حول هذه العقوبة جلّى فيه حقائق ذات عبرة في كتابه عن، (نظرية العقود والموجبات في الشريعة الإسلامية).
وإن يداً أثيمة تروّع المجتمع والمتاجر والبيوت باستمرار السرقة والسَّلب عن غير حاجة دافعة ولا تردعها عن التكرار العقوبات المطبقة اليوم، خيرٌ للمجتمع أن يخسرها ويطمئن على ما هو أثمن، ويفرض أنها ذهبت في حادث سيارة مثلاً! .
٤/٢١ - بهذه المناسبة نقول : إنَّ الشريعة الإسلامية قانون للحياة الإنسانية الصالحة كلي متكامل ومتلازم مترابط يمسك بعضه ببعض، ويسند
Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh

The second problem: Application of hudud.
This problem is (in their view that) some bodily punishments specified by Sharia (the hudud), such as the punishment of the fornicator by flogging or stoning and the punishment of the thief by cutting off the hand, have become impossible to apply in this age and its new circumstances. The answer to that is that the hudud in Sharia, especially the hadd of zina, are conditioned by very narrow conditions in their proof and application, making them rarely applied. They are thus dropped or changed to a ta'zir punishment estimated by the judge merely upon the existence of a doubt (shubha) on the part of the criminal, due to the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Avert hudud by doubts.'
On the other hand, there is in Sharia a wide chapter: the principle of necessities and what it permits legally of temporary exceptional measures, including abandoning the obligatory and doing the prohibited when compelling necessities exist.
The hudud punishments for social crimes are only five: the hadd of zina, theft, qadhf of chaste women, drinking wine, and hirabah, which is highway robbery for plunder. As for other punishments of ta'zir for all kinds of crimes, all of them in the Islamic system are delegated to rulers according to the exigencies of the situation.
If it is observed that the application of the five hudud punishments has become impossible in a time or place, then another punishment is applied, and this does not necessitate abandoning the entire Sharia. Moreover, for the punishment of the thief by cutting off the hand, one should not imagine a number of hands cut off equal to the number of thieves that today continuously plague human societies. Rather, one hand suffices to prevent thousands, and indeed the mere application of this punishment in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia in a few incidents has cut off the root of thefts and produced astonishing and ideal internal security in the world.
Our colleague Dr. Subhi al-Mahmasani, originally a criminal judge, has a valuable study on this punishment in which he elucidates instructive facts in his book 'The Theory of Contracts and Obligations in Islamic Law'.
A sinful hand that terrifies society, shops, and homes with constant theft and robbery without pressing need and is not deterred from repetition by punishments applied today – it is better for society to lose it and feel secure about what is more precious, and assume that it was lost in a car accident, for example!
4/21 - On this occasion we say: Islamic Sharia is a law for good human life, a complete, integrated, interdependent whole, holding each other, and supporting.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical discussion addressing a contemporary objection to the application of Sharia, specifically the claim that hudud punishments are impossible to implement today. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, responds by clarifying several core Hanafi principles. First, he emphasizes that hudud are not mechanically applied; they are conditioned by extremely strict evidentiary requirements (like four witnesses for zina) and are dropped in the presence of any doubt (shubha), based on the hadith 'Avert hudud by doubts.' This makes their actual application very rare. Second, he introduces the concept of ta'zir (discretionary punishment) as a substitute: when a hadd is unenforceable due to doubt or circumstances, the judge may impose a ta'zir punishment. Third, he invokes the principle of necessity (darurah), which allows temporary exceptions to normal rules. He then lists the five fixed hudud crimes and notes that all other crimes fall under ta'zir, which is flexible and entrusted to the ruler. He argues that if a particular hadd becomes impossible in a given time or place, another punishment (ta'zir) can be applied, and this does not mean abandoning the entire Sharia. He uses the example of the hand-cutting penalty for theft in Saudi Arabia to show its deterrent effect, suggesting that the extraordinary security achieved justifies the penalty. He also references Dr. Subhi al-Mahmasani's work. Finally, he concludes by emphasizing the integrated and holistic nature of Islamic Sharia as a complete system for human life.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-hudud): عقوبات مقدرة شرعاً على جرائم محددة، كالزنا والسرقة والقذف وشرب الخمر والحرابة
التعزير (al-ta'zir): عقوبة تقديرية يفرضها القاضي على الجرائم التي لا حد فيها، أو عند تعذر تطبيق الحد
الشبهة (al-shubha): الاحتمال الذي يمنع إقامة الحد، سواء كان شبهة في العقد أو الفعل أو الملك
قاعدة الضرورات (qa'idat al-darurat): القاعدة الفقهية التي تبيح المحظورات عند الضرورة، وتشمل ترك الواجب وفعل الممنوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what happens to a hadd punishment when a doubt (shubha) exists on the part of the criminal?
A) The hadd is still applied but with a reduced sentence.
B) The hadd is dropped and replaced with a ta'zir punishment at the judge's discretion.
C) The hadd is delayed until the doubt is resolved.
D) The hadd is applied exactly as prescribed regardless of doubt.

Key Takeaway
Any doubt (shubha) drops the hadd and triggers ta'zir, a flexible discretionary punishment.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

6 min
The Principle of Necessity (Darurah) and the Flexibility of Sharia
Continuation of the rebuttal, emphasizing the principle of necessity and the limited scope of hudud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of darurah to temporarily suspend hudud and explain how Sharia remains a complete, adaptable system.

Core Idea
Necessity permits temporary suspension of hudud; Sharia adapts via ta'zir.

Dr. al-Zarqa continues his rebuttal by invoking the wide chapter of the principle of necessities (qa'idat al-darurat). This principle permits exceptional measures, including abandoning obligatory acts and doing prohibited acts when compelled. In the context of hudud, if it becomes impossible to apply a hadd in a particular time or place, the ruler may apply a different punishment (ta'zir) instead. The author emphasizes that there are only five fixed hudud; all other crimes fall under ta'zir, which is flexible. This shows that Sharia is not rigid but a complete system capable of adapting to circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of darurah
The passage states that Sharia has a wide chapter of necessities that permits temporary exceptional measures, including abandoning the obligatory and doing the prohibited.
This is the well-known maxim 'الضرورات تبيح المحظورات'.
2
Apply darurah to hudud
If a hadd becomes impossible to apply in a given time or place, it can be replaced by another punishment (ta'zir) based on necessity.
This is not a permanent abolition but a temporary measure.
3
Recognize the limited scope of hudud
Only five crimes have fixed punishments (hudud). All other crimes are punished by ta'zir, which is discretionary.
The list: zina, theft, qadhf, drinking wine, hirabah.
4
Evaluate the example of hand-cutting
The author cites Saudi Arabia to show that when hudud are applied, they can be highly deterrent. The punishment, though severe, is effective and rarely needed.
This supports the idea that hudud are not impractical per se.

Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh

The second problem: Application of hudud.
This problem is (in their view that) some bodily punishments specified by Sharia (the hudud), such as the punishment of the fornicator by flogging or stoning and the punishment of the thief by cutting off the hand, have become impossible to apply in this age and its new circumstances. The answer to that is that the hudud in Sharia, especially the hadd of zina, are conditioned by very narrow conditions in their proof and application, making them rarely applied. They are thus dropped or changed to a ta'zir punishment estimated by the judge merely upon the existence of a doubt (shubha) on the part of the criminal, due to the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Avert hudud by doubts.'
On the other hand, there is in Sharia a wide chapter: the principle of necessities and what it permits legally of temporary exceptional measures, including abandoning the obligatory and doing the prohibited when compelling necessities exist.
The hudud punishments for social crimes are only five: the hadd of zina, theft, qadhf of chaste women, drinking wine, and hirabah, which is highway robbery for plunder. As for other punishments of ta'zir for all kinds of crimes, all of them in the Islamic system are delegated to rulers according to the exigencies of the situation.
If it is observed that the application of the five hudud punishments has become impossible in a time or place, then another punishment is applied, and this does not necessitate abandoning the entire Sharia. Moreover, for the punishment of the thief by cutting off the hand, one should not imagine a number of hands cut off equal to the number of thieves that today continuously plague human societies. Rather, one hand suffices to prevent thousands, and indeed the mere application of this punishment in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia in a few incidents has cut off the root of thefts and produced astonishing and ideal internal security in the world.
Our colleague Dr. Subhi al-Mahmasani, originally a criminal judge, has a valuable study on this punishment in which he elucidates instructive facts in his book 'The Theory of Contracts and Obligations in Islamic Law'.
A sinful hand that terrifies society, shops, and homes with constant theft and robbery without pressing need and is not deterred from repetition by punishments applied today – it is better for society to lose it and feel secure about what is more precious, and assume that it was lost in a car accident, for example!
4/21 - On this occasion we say: Islamic Sharia is a law for good human life, a complete, integrated, interdependent whole, holding each other, and supporting.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical discussion addressing a contemporary objection to the application of Sharia, specifically the claim that hudud punishments are impossible to implement today. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, responds by clarifying several core Hanafi principles. First, he emphasizes that hudud are not mechanically applied; they are conditioned by extremely strict evidentiary requirements (like four witnesses for zina) and are dropped in the presence of any doubt (shubha), based on the hadith 'Avert hudud by doubts.' This makes their actual application very rare. Second, he introduces the concept of ta'zir (discretionary punishment) as a substitute: when a hadd is unenforceable due to doubt or circumstances, the judge may impose a ta'zir punishment. Third, he invokes the principle of necessity (darurah), which allows temporary exceptions to normal rules. He then lists the five fixed hudud crimes and notes that all other crimes fall under ta'zir, which is flexible and entrusted to the ruler. He argues that if a particular hadd becomes impossible in a given time or place, another punishment (ta'zir) can be applied, and this does not mean abandoning the entire Sharia. He uses the example of the hand-cutting penalty for theft in Saudi Arabia to show its deterrent effect, suggesting that the extraordinary security achieved justifies the penalty. He also references Dr. Subhi al-Mahmasani's work. Finally, he concludes by emphasizing the integrated and holistic nature of Islamic Sharia as a complete system for human life.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-hudud): عقوبات مقدرة شرعاً على جرائم محددة، كالزنا والسرقة والقذف وشرب الخمر والحرابة
التعزير (al-ta'zir): عقوبة تقديرية يفرضها القاضي على الجرائم التي لا حد فيها، أو عند تعذر تطبيق الحد
الشبهة (al-shubha): الاحتمال الذي يمنع إقامة الحد، سواء كان شبهة في العقد أو الفعل أو الملك
قاعدة الضرورات (qa'idat al-darurat): القاعدة الفقهية التي تبيح المحظورات عند الضرورة، وتشمل ترك الواجب وفعل الممنوع

The Challenge of Interest vs. Islamic Law
Introduces the third contemporary problem: riba vs. modern finance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nature of the conflict between Sharia's absolute prohibition of riba and modern interest-based financial systems, and identify the core problem that Islamic economists and muftis must address.

Core Idea
Islam prohibits riba absolutely; modern economies run on it — resolving this tension is a central fiqh challenge.

This passage introduces a crucial contemporary issue: the apparent incompatibility between Islamic law's categorical prohibition of riba (interest) and the reality of modern finance, where interest-based transactions dominate. As a mufti or student of fiqh, understanding this conflict is the first step toward crafting solutions that uphold Sharia while allowing economic activity to function. We will explore the exact terms of the problem and its implications for iftaa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core prohibition
Locate the statement in the passage that declares the Islamic ruling on riba. Note the strength of the prohibition: 'categorically' and 'relentlessly'.
The passage uses 'يحرم ... تحريماً قطعياً' — indicating a definitive, undisputed ruling.
2
Recognize the modern context
Identify what the passage says about contemporary economic practices: 'many commercial and banking transactions are based on interest'.
Interest (fa'idah) is equated with prohibited riba, not a separate category.
3
Frame the problem
Understand the consequence the passage describes: applying Sharia 'leads to disruption' of these activities. This is the doubt (shubhah) that needs an answer.
The passage presents this as a perception of opponents, not necessarily the author's own conclusion.
4
Connect to the mufti's role
Consider how a mufti would address this conflict: by distinguishing between prohibited riba and permissible profit, and by facilitating alternative Islamic financial instruments.
The Hanafi school provides detailed rules on valid sales (e.g., murabahah, musharakah) as substitutes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشكلة الثالثة : نظام الفائدة الربوية .
يحرم الإسلام الربا تحريماً قطعياً ويحاربه دون هوادة. والمعاملات التجارية والمصرفية اليوم يقوم كثير منها على أساس الفائدة التي هي من الربا المحرم قطعاً. فتطبيق الشريعة في هذه المجالات يتنافي تنافياً مؤكداً مع نظام الفائدة، ويؤدي - في نظر من ثارت في ذهنه هذه الشبهة - إلى تعطيل كثير من الأعمال التجارية والمصرفية التي هي اليوم محور الحياة الاقتصادية .
The third problem: The interest-based system (riba). Islam categorically prohibits riba and fights it relentlessly. Today, many commercial and banking transactions are based on interest, which is categorically prohibited riba. Applying Sharia in these areas is in definite conflict with the interest system, and leads - in the view of those who have this doubt in their minds - to the disruption of many commercial and banking activities that are today the pivot of economic life.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage identifies the third contemporary problem in applying Sharia: the conflict between the absolute prohibition of riba (interest) and the modern interest-based economic system. The author acknowledges that many current financial transactions rely on interest, which creates a significant obstacle for implementing Islamic law. The concern, raised by those who question the feasibility of Islamic economics, is that eliminating interest would paralyze commercial and banking activities that are central to modern economies. This sets the stage for discussing potential solutions, such as Islamic banking alternatives or gradual reforms. The passage is introductory and does not provide a ruling; it frames the problem that must be addressed.

Key Terms
ربا (riba): Unlawful increase in a loan or sale transaction, specifically interest on money loans.
فائدة (fa'idah): Interest, considered a form of riba when predetermined on capital loans.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the conflict between Sharia and contemporary economic systems?
A) Sharia prohibits interest, but most modern transactions are based on it.
B) Sharia allows interest in banking but prohibits it in trade.
C) Sharia does not prohibit interest; the problem is cultural.
D) The conflict is only theoretical and has no practical implications.

Key Takeaway
The core problem of interest-based finance is that riba is categorically haram, yet it permeates modern economies; Islamic solutions must replace, not accommodate, interest.

Interdependence of Hudud and Social Obligations in Sharia Implementation
Discussing the necessary connection between hudud punishments and prior social duties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why hudud penalties cannot be implemented in isolation from prior social duties, and identify the key prerequisites for each hadd as discussed by al-Zarqa.

Core Idea
Hudud require prior social welfare, moral purification, and legal prevention.

This passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa challenges the reductionist view that implementing Sharia merely means applying hudud punishments. Instead, he argues for a holistic approach: each punishment is tied to a positive duty that must first be fulfilled by state and society. Without these prerequisites, hudud become distortions of justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three examples
Al-Zarqa gives examples: theft, adultery, and drinking. List them and their corresponding duties.
Use the passage: hadd al-sariqa, hadd al-zina, hadd shurb al-khamr.
2
State the linked duty for each
For theft: social solidarity and care for the poor. For adultery: moral purification and facilitating marriage. For drinking: banning manufacture and trade.
These are the 'wajibat' (duties) that must precede the punishment.
3
Consider the opposite scenario
Imagine society does the opposite: media encourages drinking, women are exploited, wealth disparity is huge. The author says hudud alone would be contradictory.
This critical refinement exposes the inconsistency.
4
Evaluate the conclusion
The author argues that hudud-only implementation is not implementation but distortion and deception.
Why? Because the maqasid (higher objectives) are violated.

الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه
بعضه بعضاً، فإذا أهمل بعض أجزائه يظهر الخلل والغرابة في البعض الآخر.
- فحد السرقة مثلاً مرتبط بواجب آخر هو الاهتمام بحال الفقراء وكفايتهم بحسن توزيع الثروة، وتحقيق التكافل والضمان الاجتماعيين.

- وحد شرب الخمر مرتبط بمنع صنعها وتجارتها والدعاية لها.
وهكذا . . . فهذه الارتباطات بين الزواجر والواجبات شروط لا بد منها لتطبيق عقوبات الحدود الشرعية.

Fourth Chapter: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh
[These rulings] are interconnected; if some parts are neglected, defect and oddity appear in other parts.
- For example, the punishment for theft is linked to another duty: caring for the poor and sufficing them through proper distribution of wealth, achieving social solidarity and security.
- The punishment for adultery is linked to purifying society from means of temptation and arousal of sexual instincts, educating people about religion, inclining them to piety towards Allah, and facilitating marriage.
- The punishment for drinking wine is linked to preventing its manufacture, trade, and advertisement.
And so on... These connections between prohibitory punishments and duties are indispensable conditions for applying the prescribed punishments.
But if these duties in the state and society are neglected, rather if the reality of the prevailing system is the opposite—various media are employed to advertise types of wines and compare them; women's and girls' exposure and display of their charms is the symbol of progress and civilization; the prevailing economic system allows vast disparity in wealth and wages such that large deprived classes exist alongside sated ones concerned only with their pleasures stuffed with sins and amassing unlawful gain—then how can applying the hudud alone be considered the manifestation of implementing Sharia? Is this implementation or distortion, deception and contradiction?!!
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical argument about the holistic nature of Sharia implementation. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, asserts that hudud punishments (such as those for theft, adultery, and drinking) are not standalone penalties but are intimately connected to positive social duties (واجبات) that must be fulfilled first. For the hadd of theft, society must ensure economic justice and welfare for the poor. For the hadd of adultery, societal measures to reduce temptation and facilitate marriage are prerequisites. For the hadd of drinking, banning production and trade of alcohol is necessary. If these duties are ignored—or worse, if society promotes the opposite—then applying only the hudud is contradictory and misrepresents Sharia. This is a critical refinement of the common simplistic understanding that implementing Sharia means merely enforcing penal codes. The author uses rational argument (talil) to emphasize that the maqasid (higher objectives) of Sharia require a comprehensive approach.

Key Terms
حد السرقة (hadd al-sariqa): The prescribed punishment for theft, which is amputation of the hand under certain conditions.
الزواجر والواجبات (al-zawajir wa al-wajibat): Prohibitory punishments and positive duties, respectively; the two categories of legal obligations that together form the comprehensive system of Sharia.
التكافل والضمان الاجتماعيين (al-takāful wa al-ḍamān al-ijtimā‘iyyayn): Social solidarity and social security; essential prior duties for the just application of hudud.

Key Takeaway
Hudud penalties are not standalone; they require prior fulfillment of social and moral duties, otherwise implementation becomes distortion.

The Riba Problem and Islamic Alternatives in Contemporary Finance
Refuting the claim that Sharia cannot accommodate modern finance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Islamic law provides alternatives to interest-based finance and apply the principle that prohibition does not lead to economic paralysis.

Core Idea
Islamic law's prohibition of riba does not cripple the economy; fiqh provides valid contracts for all modern financial needs.

This passage tackles a common objection to implementing Sharia: that eliminating interest would destroy modern finance. The author, a renowned Hanafi jurist, responds by highlighting the rich tradition of permissible financial contracts in Islamic law, which can be adapted to contemporary needs. This lesson explores the legal-philosophical grounds for Islamic alternatives to riba.

Guided Path
1
Pinpoint the objection
Identify the claim that the author is refuting: that applying Sharia by eliminating riba would paralyze commercial and banking activities that are central to modern economies.
The objection is based on the assumption that no permissible alternatives exist.
2
Analyze the author's rebuttal
The author asserts that Islamic Sharia has permitted many financial contracts (e.g., mudarabah, musharakah, murabahah) and has principles that allow deriving new contracts. These can fulfill all necessary transactions without riba.
The rebuttal is a rational argument (dalil aqli) based on the existence and flexibility of Islamic contracts.
3
Recognize the evidence cited
The author refers to hundreds of modern research works and conferences in Islamic economics. This shows that his claim is not theoretical but based on contemporary scholarship and practice.
This strengthens the argument by indicating that the alternative is well-researched and viable.
4
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Consider a modern need like home financing. Instead of a conventional mortgage (riba), one can use a murabahah (cost-plus sale) or diminishing musharakah (partnership). The principle: the prohibition of a specific contract does not prohibit the underlying need; permissible alternatives must be found within Sharia.
This demonstrates the operational relevance of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشكلة الثالثة : نظام الفائدة الربوية .
يحرم الإسلام الربا تحريماً قطعياً ويحاربه دون هوادة. والمعاملات التجارية والمصرفية اليوم يقوم كثير منها على أساس الفائدة التي هي من الربا المحرم قطعاً. فتطبيق الشريعة في هذه المجالات يتنافي تنافياً مؤكداً مع نظام الفائدة، ويؤدي - في نظر من ثارت في ذهنه هذه الشبهة - إلى تعطيل كثير من الأعمال التجارية والمصرفية التي هي اليوم محور الحياة الاقتصادية.
والجواب : إنَّ الشريعة الإسلامية، إلى جانب تحريمها الربا والفائدة منه كما أسلفنا قد أباحت كثيراً من عقود المعاملات المالية التي فصلها الفقه، وقرر فيها قواعد وأحكاماً تسمح باستنباط عقود جديدة تنسجم مع قواعد الشريعة وتلبي الحاجاتِ المتجددة، ويمكن بذلك إنجاز سائر المعاملات التجارية والمصرفية المناسبة للحياة الاقتصادية المعاصرة دون اللجوء إلى الربا المحرم وأخصُّ بالذكر تمويل المشروعات والأعمال التجارية والصناعية ومشروعات الحكومة ومشترياتها، ومشتريات المستهلكين وكذلك يمكن حشد المدخرات من الجمهور واستثمارها، وإجراء عمليات التمويل الدولي، كل ذلك بعقود صحيحة فقهاً وخالية من الربا، وعلى أسس تجارية وليست خيرية تبرعية).
وما قررناه آنفاً هر المحصلة العامة لمئات من البحوث والكتب الحديثة المتخصصة، والمفصلة كتبها اقتصاديون ومصرفيون وفقهاء، وعُقِدَت لها عشرات المؤتمرات المتخصصة والندوات العلمية، خلال السنوات الثلاثين الماضية، في مجال ما صار يعرف بالاقتصاد الإسلامي.
لهذا يمكننا اليوم أن نرى أن التخلص من نظام الفائدة الربوية وتشغيل الجهاز المصرفي دون اللجوء إلى الربا (لمن يريد ذلك)، هو أمر ممكن التحقيق إذا أُعِدَّت له العُدَّة، ولا ينبغي أن يتخذ ذريعة لعدم تطبيق الشريعة. بل قد نوهت بعض الدراسات بالمزايا الاقتصادية لأساليب التمويل الإسلامي.
The Third Problem: The Usurious Interest System.
Islam prohibits riba absolutely and combats it relentlessly. Many commercial and banking transactions today are based on interest, which is definitively prohibited riba. So applying Sharia in these fields conflicts decisively with the interest system, and leads – in the view of those who entertain this doubt – to paralysis of many commercial and banking activities that today are the axis of economic life.
The answer: Verily, Islamic Sharia, alongside its prohibition of riba and interest as we have stated, has permitted many financial transaction contracts detailed by fiqh, and has established rules and rulings that allow deriving new contracts that harmonize with Sharia rules and meet renewed needs. Thus, all suitable commercial and banking transactions for contemporary economic life can be accomplished without resorting to prohibited riba. I specifically mention the financing of projects, commercial and industrial enterprises, government projects and purchases, consumer purchases, as well as mobilizing savings from the public and investing them, and conducting international finance operations – all of this with contracts that are legally valid (fiqhan) and free from riba, and on commercial, not charitable or gratuitous, bases.
What we have stated above is the general outcome of hundreds of specialized and detailed modern research works and books written by economists, bankers, and jurists, and dozens of specialized conferences and scientific seminars held over the past thirty years in the field of what has become known as Islamic economics.
Therefore, today we can see that freeing oneself from the usurious interest system and operating the banking system without resorting to riba (for those who want that) is something achievable if proper preparations are made, and should not be taken as an excuse for not applying Sharia. Rather, some studies have highlighted the economic advantages of Islamic financing methods.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage addresses the third challenge to implementing Sharia in modern times: the pervasiveness of interest-based (riba) finance. The author first states the absolute prohibition of riba in Islam. He then acknowledges the doubt that eliminating interest would cripple modern economies. The response is a legal-philosophical argument that Islamic law provides ample permissible contracts (such as mudarabah, musharakah, murabahah, etc.) that can fulfill all contemporary financial needs without riba. Al-Zarqa emphasizes that this is not mere theory but the result of extensive modern scholarship and conferences in Islamic economics. The key point is that Riba-free banking is feasible and should not be an excuse to postpone Sharia implementation. The argument is rational (dalil aqli) based on the existence of alternatives. This passage is a critical refinement of the claim that Sharia cannot accommodate modern finance.

Key Terms
الربا (al-ribā): Usury or interest, categorically prohibited in Islam.
الفائدة (al-fā'idah): Interest, considered a form of riba al-nasi'ah, prevalent in modern banking.
الاقتصاد الإسلامي (al-iqtiṣād al-islāmī): Islamic economics, a field that develops financial systems compliant with Sharia.

B) Sharia permits many contracts that can replace interest-based transactions.

Key Takeaway
The absolute prohibition of riba does not incapacitate the economy; Islamic law provides a robust set of permissible contracts that can serve all modern financial needs while staying true to Sharia.

Riba and Islamic Finance: Achieving Interest-Free Banking
Response to the challenge of riba in modern finance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on riba and explain how commercial contracts can replace interest-based financing.

Core Idea
Islamic law prohibits riba but permits adaptable commercial contracts, making interest-free banking possible.

In this lesson, we examine the first contemporary challenge: the issue of riba in modern finance. Mustafa al-Zarqa argues that while riba is strictly prohibited, Islamic law provides a wealth of permissible contracts that can be used for modern banking and financing without resorting to interest. This is not a theoretical claim but is supported by extensive modern scholarship and practical experience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem and the Islamic response
The problem is the widespread use of interest (riba/fā'ida) in modern finance. The Islamic response is a clear prohibition of riba but permission for many other commercial contracts.
Riba is understood broadly as any fixed, predetermined interest.
2
List the types of permissible transactions
The author mentions financing projects, businesses, government purchases, consumer purchases, mobilizing savings, and international financing—all achievable through contracts like murabaha, mudaraba, musharaka, and ijara.
These contracts are based on trade, profit-sharing, or leasing, not lending at interest.
3
Note the evidence from modern scholarship
Hundreds of books, research, and conferences on Islamic economics have demonstrated the feasibility of interest-free finance. The author cites these as supporting the ability to eliminate riba.
This shows that the claim is not merely theoretical but practically grounded.
4
Conclude that interest-free banking is possible
The author asserts that with proper preparation, the banking system can operate without interest. Economic advantages are noted.
The goal is to show that riba is not a necessary component of modern finance.

٢٨٦
Chapter (21): Contemporary problems facing the application of Sharia. The answer: Indeed Islamic law, alongside its prohibition of riba and interest from it as we previously stated, has permitted many financial transaction contracts that fiqh has detailed, and established in them rules and rulings that allow deriving new contracts that harmonize with the rules of Sharia and meet renewed needs. And thereby all commercial and banking transactions appropriate for contemporary economic life can be accomplished without resorting to forbidden riba. I especially mention financing projects, commercial and industrial businesses, government projects and purchases, and consumer purchases. Also, savings from the public can be mobilized and invested, and international financing operations can be conducted, all with contracts that are valid in fiqh and free from riba, and on commercial bases, not charitable donations. What we have stated above is the general outcome of hundreds of specialized modern books and detailed research written by economists, bankers, and jurists, and for which dozens of specialized conferences and scientific seminars have been held over the past thirty years in the field of what is now known as Islamic economics. Hence today we can see that getting rid of the usurious interest system and operating the banking system without resorting to riba (for whoever desires that) is possible to achieve if prepared for it, and it should not be taken as a pretext for not applying Sharia. Rather, some studies have pointed out the economic advantages of Islamic financing methods in mitigating economic fluctuations and crises and in limiting the concentration of wealth. And Islamic banks applying these methods of dealing and financing have been established in various countries. We have appended an appendix to this chapter containing additional details on this topic. (6/1) The fourth problem: Novel matters. Indeed some legal situations today are based on the existence of modern economic situations that had no parallel in the economic and jurisprudential past in Islamic life, such as today's limited liability joint-stock companies (Anonymes) and insurance contracts (Assurance), and the important rulings they contain that laws have regulated, and other such situations. The answer to that: The legal principles in Islamic jurisprudence have capacity, flexibility, and amazing ability for inclusion and derivation. The original texts of these rulings and principles in the Quran and Prophetic hadith can be limited to a few tens of pages, and they were sufficient and adequate for there to be derived from them during the early Islamic centuries a great jurisprudence like the encompassing sea, and independent legal schools and important theories from which a jurisprudential library in Islam was formed reaching thousands of volumes, and every ruling in it is supported by its evidence from those texts by way of generality or analogizing new incidents to incidents covered by the text, or by way of istihsan (juristic preference) or istislah (public interest). So all economic situations today, if they are necessary, beneficial, and achieve public interest, new rulings can be derived for them on the bases of Islamic jurisprudence and its theories, just as the earlier jurists did, and those new rulings after their derivation.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of two contemporary challenges to the application of Sharia: the issue of interest-based finance and the emergence of novel legal institutions. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, presents the Islamic legal response. For the first issue, he asserts that Islamic law prohibits riba but permits many contracts that can be adapted for modern banking and finance without resorting to interest. These contracts are based on commercial principles, not charity. He cites the extensive modern literature and conferences on Islamic economics, arguing that interest-free banking is achievable. For the second issue, he addresses novel situations like joint-stock companies and insurance. He emphasizes that Islamic legal principles are broad and flexible, allowing for the derivation of new rulings through methods such as qiyas (analogy), istihsan, and istislah. The original sources (Qur'an and Sunnah) are concise, yet they have generated a vast body of fiqh. Therefore, any beneficial new situation can be accommodated within Islamic jurisprudence by jurists using established methodologies.

Key Terms
الربا (al-ribā): Any increase in a loan or exchange that is legally prohibited; specifically refers to usury or interest.
الفائدة (al-fā’idah): Interest charged on loans, considered a form of riba and thus prohibited.
الاقتصاد الإسلامي (al-iqtiṣād al-islāmī): The field of economics based on Islamic principles, avoiding riba and promoting ethical finance.

Key Takeaway
Riba is banned, but Islam provides ample commercial alternatives for modern finance.

Novel Matters: The Flexibility of Fiqh to Accommodate New Situations
Response to the challenge of novel legal institutions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Hanafi fiqh derives rulings for novel situations using qiyas, istihsan, and istislah.

Core Idea
Fiqh is not static; its principles are flexible enough to generate rulings for entirely new economic and legal institutions.

This lesson tackles the second contemporary challenge: novel legal and economic institutions like joint-stock companies and insurance that have no direct precedent in classical fiqh. Mustafa al-Zarqa argues that Islamic jurisprudence has inherent flexibility and capacity for derivation. The original sources (Qur'an and Sunnah) are concise, yet they have generated a vast and comprehensive body of fiqh through methods such as qiyas (analogy), istihsan (juristic preference), and istislah (public interest). Thus, any beneficial new situation can be accommodated.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the challenge: novel institutions without direct precedent
The author mentions limited liability companies and insurance contracts as examples of modern situations not found in classical fiqh.
This challenge is often used as an argument against the applicability of Islamic law.
2
Identify the Islamic response: principles of flexibility and capacity
The response emphasizes that the legal principles of fiqh have 's'a, muruna, qabiliyya' (capacity, flexibility, and ability) for inclusion and derivation.
This is a foundational attitude: fiqh can grow.
3
List the methods of derivation mentioned
The author explicitly mentions: qiyas (analogizing new incidents to those covered by text), istihsan (juristic preference), and istislah (public interest).
These are classical usul tools.
4
Apply the methods to a new situation
For example, to derive the ruling for insurance: analogize to existing contracts like mudaraba or mutual assistance, or use istislah if it serves a necessary public interest.
The result must not contradict a clear nass.
5
Conclude that Islamic law can handle modern developments
If the situation is necessary, beneficial, and achieves public interest, new rulings can be derived. This is exactly what earlier jurists did.
The message is one of continuity and adaptability.

Chapter (21): Contemporary problems facing the application of Sharia. The answer: Indeed Islamic law, alongside its prohibition of riba and interest from it as we previously stated, has permitted many financial transaction contracts that fiqh has detailed, and established in them rules and rulings that allow deriving new contracts that harmonize with the rules of Sharia and meet renewed needs. And thereby all commercial and banking transactions appropriate for contemporary economic life can be accomplished without resorting to forbidden riba. I especially mention financing projects, commercial and industrial businesses, government projects and purchases, and consumer purchases. Also, savings from the public can be mobilized and invested, and international financing operations can be conducted, all with contracts that are valid in fiqh and free from riba, and on commercial bases, not charitable donations. What we have stated above is the general outcome of hundreds of specialized modern books and detailed research written by economists, bankers, and jurists, and for which dozens of specialized conferences and scientific seminars have been held over the past thirty years in the field of what is now known as Islamic economics. Hence today we can see that getting rid of the usurious interest system and operating the banking system without resorting to riba (for whoever desires that) is possible to achieve if prepared for it, and it should not be taken as a pretext for not applying Sharia. Rather, some studies have pointed out the economic advantages of Islamic financing methods in mitigating economic fluctuations and crises and in limiting the concentration of wealth. And Islamic banks applying these methods of dealing and financing have been established in various countries. We have appended an appendix to this chapter containing additional details on this topic. (6/1) The fourth problem: Novel matters. Indeed some legal situations today are based on the existence of modern economic situations that had no parallel in the economic and jurisprudential past in Islamic life, such as today's limited liability joint-stock companies (Anonymes) and insurance contracts (Assurance), and the important rulings they contain that laws have regulated, and other such situations. The answer to that: The legal principles in Islamic jurisprudence have capacity, flexibility, and amazing ability for inclusion and derivation. The original texts of these rulings and principles in the Quran and Prophetic hadith can be limited to a few tens of pages, and they were sufficient and adequate for there to be derived from them during the early Islamic centuries a great jurisprudence like the encompassing sea, and independent legal schools and important theories from which a jurisprudential library in Islam was formed reaching thousands of volumes, and every ruling in it is supported by its evidence from those texts by way of generality or analogizing new incidents to incidents covered by the text, or by way of istihsan (juristic preference) or istislah (public interest). So all economic situations today, if they are necessary, beneficial, and achieve public interest, new rulings can be derived for them on the bases of Islamic jurisprudence and its theories, just as the earlier jurists did, and those new rulings after their derivation.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of two contemporary challenges to the application of Sharia: the issue of interest-based finance and the emergence of novel legal institutions. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, presents the Islamic legal response. For the first issue, he asserts that Islamic law prohibits riba but permits many contracts that can be adapted for modern banking and finance without resorting to interest. These contracts are based on commercial principles, not charity. He cites the extensive modern literature and conferences on Islamic economics, arguing that interest-free banking is achievable. For the second issue, he addresses novel situations like joint-stock companies and insurance. He emphasizes that Islamic legal principles are broad and flexible, allowing for the derivation of new rulings through methods such as qiyas (analogy), istihsan, and istislah. The original sources (Qur'an and Sunnah) are concise, yet they have generated a vast body of fiqh. Therefore, any beneficial new situation can be accommodated within Islamic jurisprudence by jurists using established methodologies.

Key Terms
الربا (al-ribā): Any increase in a loan or exchange that is legally prohibited; specifically refers to usury or interest.
الفائدة (al-fā’idah): Interest charged on loans, considered a form of riba and thus prohibited.
الاقتصاد الإسلامي (al-iqtiṣād al-islāmī): The field of economics based on Islamic principles, avoiding riba and promoting ethical finance.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning used to derive rulings for new cases by comparing them to established cases with a shared effective cause (illa).
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method of departing from a clear analogy in favor of a more equitable result based on a stronger evidence or public interest.
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): Consideration of public interest; deriving rulings based on general welfare when no specific text or qiyas exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Qiyas, istihsan, and istislah
C) Following the majority of scholars
D) Rejecting all new situations as invalid

Key Takeaway
Fiqh's flexibility allows it to generate rulings for novel situations using established methods like qiyas, istihsan, and istislah.

Fiqh Flexibility for Novel Economic Issues
Explaining the flexibility of Islamic fiqh in addressing novel economic issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principles of istihsan and istislah to evaluate the permissibility of modern financial contracts (e.g., insurance) using the Hanafi framework.

Core Idea
Islamic fiqh is inherently flexible; new rulings can be derived for novel economic situations using istihsan and istislah.

A common misconception is that Islamic fiqh is rigid and cannot accommodate new economic realities like insurance or joint-stock companies. This passage, from Mustafa al-Zarqa's work, demonstrates the opposite: Hanafi methodology, through tools like istihsan (juristic preference) and istislah (public interest), allows for dynamic adaptation. We will examine how the principles of fiqh can be extended to novel issues, using the example of insurance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Recognize that modern economic situations, such as insurance and limited liability companies, have no direct precedent in classical fiqh. This creates a challenge for deriving rulings.
The passage calls these 'al-umur al-mustajiddah' (novel matters).
2
Understand the Hanafi Response
Note that the principles of Islamic fiqh are broad and flexible. The primary sources (Quran and Sunnah) are compact but sufficient for deriving a vast body of fiqh through methods like qiyas (analogy), istihsan, and istislah.
The author emphasizes 'al-mabadi' al-huquqiyyah' (legal principles) have 'wass'ah wa murunah' (breadth and flexibility).
3
Recognize the Tools of Derivation
Identify the specific methods: qiyas (analogy to existing cases), istihsan (juristic preference for a ruling more suitable to the situation), and istislah (consideration of public interest). These are the mechanisms that allow fiqh to expand.
Istihsan is a distinctive Hanafi source, often used when qiyas leads to a harsh result.
4
See the Example: Insurance
Observe how the general opinion on insurance shifted from impermissibility (until the 1960s) to permissibility for cooperative insurance. This shift was achieved through ijtihad using the above tools.
The author himself was a pioneer in this research, presenting at the 1961 Damascus conference.
5
Apply the Method
Now you can apply this reasoning to other novel economic issues: identify the need, use the appropriate method (qiyas, istihsan, istislah), and derive a ruling that aligns with maqasid al-shari'ah.
The goal is to produce rulings that meet public interest without contradicting core principles.

Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh. Benefit: ... free from in alleviating economic fluctuations and crises and in limiting the concentration of wealth... Islamic banks in various countries have applied these methods of dealing and financing... We have devoted an appendix to this chapter with additional details on this subject. 6/1 - The Fourth Problem: Novel Matters. Indeed, some legal situations today are based on the existence of modern economic conditions that had no parallel in the economic and jurisprudential past in Islamic life, such as today's limited liability joint-stock companies (Anonymes) and insurance contracts (Assurance) and the important rulings in them regulated by laws, and other situations. The answer to that: The legal principles in Islamic fiqh have vastness, flexibility, and wonderful capacity for assimilation and derivation. The original texts of these rulings and principles in the Quran and Prophetic hadith can be limited to a few tens of pages, and they were sufficient and adequate for there to be derived from them during the early Islamic eras a great fiqh like the encompassing sea, and ijtihad-based legal schools and important theories that formed a jurisprudential library in Islam reaching thousands of volumes, each ruling in it supported by its evidence from those texts by way of implication or analogy of new incidents to incidents addressed by the text, or by way of istihsan or istislah. So all economic situations today, if they are necessary, beneficial, and achieve public interest, new rulings can be derived for them on the foundations of Islamic fiqh and its theories, as the earlier jurists did, and those new rulings after their derivation and construction on the rules of Islamic fiqh become attached to it and become part of it, and its theories become a reference for judicial ijtihad and legal interpretation in them in the manner by which Islamic fiqh itself expanded. Among the contemporary examples of that are insurance contracts, which was the general impression among jurists until the 1960s that they are not permissible, then modern jurisprudential research began around them since then, and the general outcome of them was: A - The emergence of agreement in opinion among contemporary scholars and fiqh councils that insurance on property against risks, and insurance against liability for fault, if it is cooperative insurance, is permissible fiqhan. Also, many scholars and fiqh bodies approved the 'Takaful' formula to replace savings life insurance (technically called: simple mixed insurance). B - The establishment of some insurance and reinsurance companies on Islamic foundations under the supervision of Shariah boards. * (1) I was the first researcher on insurance in general in its three forms: property insurance, insurance against liability for wrongful act, and life insurance (in the manner free from usurious benefits), and I concluded its permissibility in all of them as long as the contract between the insured and the insurance company does not contain conditions unacceptable Shariah. I detailed that in a research I presented to the Islamic Fiqh Week conference held at the University of Damascus in 1961 AD, and it was published then in a book titled (The Insurance Contract 'al-Sukara?' and the Position of Islamic Shariah towards it). I expanded the evidence and discussed opposing views. Then I expanded it and added another research on the subject and published it in a new book titled (The Insurance System - Its Reality and the Shariah Opinion on it), published by Al-Risala Foundation.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on the ability of Islamic fiqh to address modern economic issues. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by identifying the problem: many contemporary legal situations, such as joint-stock companies and insurance contracts, have no direct precedent in classical Islamic jurisprudence. He then provides the answer: the principles of Islamic fiqh are inherently broad, flexible, and capable of assimilating new issues. The primary sources (Quran and Sunnah) are compact but sufficient, as demonstrated by the vast jurisprudential literature derived from them through methods like analogy (qiyas), istihsan, and istislah. Any new economic situation that is necessary and beneficial can have rulings derived on the basis of Islamic fiqh principles, and those rulings become part of the fiqh corpus. The author then gives a concrete example: insurance contracts. Up to the 1960s, the general view was that insurance was impermissible, but subsequent research led to a consensus that cooperative insurance (ta'min ta'awuni) is permissible, and takaful was developed as an alternative to life insurance. The author notes his own pioneering role in this research, having presented a paper in 1961 and later books. This passage demonstrates the dynamism of Hanafi-inspired fiqh in adapting to new realities, using the tools of ijtihad and maslaha.

Key Terms
التأمين (at-ta'meen): Insurance contracts, including property insurance, liability insurance, and life insurance.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A method of juristic preference used to derive rulings that are more suitable for the situation, even if contrary to a primary analogy.
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): Considering public interest (maslaha) as a basis for ruling when no explicit text or analogy exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They were unanimously permissible.
B) They were generally considered impermissible.
C) They were considered obligatory.
D) There was no opinion on them.

Key Takeaway
Islamic fiqh has built-in flexibility through istihsan and istislah, allowing novel economic issues like insurance to be accommodated by deriving new rulings that serve public interest without violating Shariah principles.

Integrating Contemporary Problems into Fiqh: The Case of Insurance
Methodology for adapting contemporary issues via qawa'id

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of building contemporary issues on qawa'id to evaluate the permissibility of a modern contract.

Core Idea
Islamic law expands through ijtihad on its qawa'id: cooperative insurance shows how a once-impermissible contract became permissible.

This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm demonstrates how contemporary problems become part of fiqh by being built upon its fundamental principles (qawa'id). Using insurance as a case study, al-Zarqa illustrates the evolution of fatwa from general prohibition to conditional permissibility through rigorous scholarly research and ijtihad. The method is essential for any mufti dealing with novel contracts in modern finance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem and initial stance
First, recognize the novel issue: insurance contracts were not explicitly addressed in classical fiqh. The initial general impression among jurists was that they are impermissible due to perceived gharar (uncertainty) and riba (interest).
2
Apply the principles of fiqh
Next, the issue is built upon the qawa'id of Islamic law, such as the permissibility of cooperative contracts (tabarru'), the principle that contracts are permissible unless prohibited (al-asi fi al-mu'amalat al-ibahah), and the consideration of custom ('urf) and necessity (darurah).
3
Examine the evidence and scholarly consensus
Modern researchers, including al-Zarqa, analyzed the terms of insurance contracts and distinguished between types. They found that cooperative insurance (ta'min ta'awuni) and takaful avoid gharar and riba, leading to a consensus among contemporary scholars and fiqh councils that such forms are permissible.
4
Identify the conditions for permissibility
The permissibility is conditional: the contract must be cooperative in nature, free from prohibited elements like interest, and must not contain unfair terms. This shows how ijtihad refines the ruling.
5
Observe the practical outcome
As a result, Islamic insurance and reinsurance companies were established under Sharia supervision, providing a practical example of how fiqh adapts to modern needs.

الفصل (۲۱) مشكلات عصرية يواجهها تطبيق الشريعة
وبنائها على قواعد الفقه الإسلامي تلحق به وتصبح جزءاً منه، وتصبح نظریاته مرجعاً للاجتهاد القضائي والتفسير القانوني فيها بالطريقة التي تضخم بها الفقه الإسلامي نفسه.
ومن الأمثلة العصرية على ذلك عقود التأمين التي كان الانطباع العام لدى الفقهاء حتى الستينيات أنها لا تجوز ثم بدأت حولها البحوث الفقهية الحديثة منذ ذلك الحين(۱) ، وكانت المحصلة العامة لها هي:

* (١) كنت أول الباحثين في التأمين بوجه عام بوجه عام في صوره الثلاث التأمين على الممتلكات، والتأمين من المسؤولية عن الفعل الخطأ، والتأمين على الحياة (بالطريقة الخالية من الفوائد الربوية وانتهيت إلى جوازه فيها كلها ما لم يتضمن العقد الذي يعقد بين المستأمن وشركة التأمين شروطاً غير مقبولة شرعاً. وقد فصلت القول في ذلك في بحث قدمته إلى مؤتمر أسبوع الفقه الإسلامي الذي عُقد بجامعة دمشق سنة ١٩٦١ م، ونُشِر إذ ذاك في كتاب بعنوان (عقد التأمين السوكرة» وموقف الشريعة الإسلامية منه) . وقد بسطت الأدلة وناقشت الآراء المخالفة. ثم وسعته وأضفت إليه بحثاً آخر في الموضوع ونشرته في كتاب جديد بعنوان (نظام التأمين - حقيقته والرأي الشرعي فيه)، نشرته مؤسسة الرسالة
Chapter (21): Contemporary problems facing the application of Sharia. And building them on the principles of Islamic jurisprudence attaches them to it and makes them part of it, and its theories become a reference for judicial ijtihad and legal interpretation therein in the way that Islamic jurisprudence itself expanded. Among the contemporary examples of that are insurance contracts, which were generally perceived by jurists until the 1960s as impermissible, then modern juristic research began around them since then (1), and their general outcome was: (a) Emergence of consensus among contemporary scholars and juristic councils that insurance on property against risks, and insurance against liability for error, if it is cooperative insurance, is permissible in fiqh. Also many scholars and juristic bodies approved the 'Takaful' formula to replace savings life insurance (technically called simple mixed insurance). (b) Establishment of some insurance and reinsurance companies on Islamic foundations under the supervision of Sharia boards. * (1) I was among the first researchers on insurance in general in its three forms: property insurance, liability insurance for wrongful acts, and life insurance (in a manner free from riba-based benefits), and I concluded that all of them are permissible as long as the contract between the insured and the insurance company does not include conditions unacceptable in Sharia. I detailed that in a research paper presented to the Islamic Jurisprudence Week conference held at Damascus University in 1961 CE, then published in a book titled 'The Insurance Contract (?) and the Position of Islamic Sharia towards It.' I expanded it and added another research on the subject and published it in a new book titled 'The Insurance System: Its Reality and the Legal Opinion on It,' published by Al-Risala Foundation.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm presents the author’s methodology for handling contemporary legal issues within the framework of Islamic jurisprudence. It is a legal-philosophical discussion. The author asserts that new problems, when built upon the principles (qawa‘id) of fiqh, become absorbed into the legal system and expand it. He gives the example of insurance contracts, which were widely considered impermissible until modern research led to a consensus on the permissibility of cooperative insurance and takaful. The author emphasizes his own pioneering role in this research, noting that all three forms of insurance (property, liability, life) are permissible if they avoid riba and unacceptable conditions. The passage demonstrates how ijtihad operates on contemporary issues, using the qawa‘id to derive rulings, and highlights the evolution of fatwa over time. For the Hanafi madhhab, this aligns with the principle that custom (‘urf) and necessity (darurah) can influence rulings, and that the mufti must consider the realities of his time.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد القضائي (al-ijtihad al-qada'i): Judicial ijtihad: the process of deriving rulings by judges using the principles of fiqh for new cases.
التأمين التعاوني (al-ta'min al-ta'awuni): Cooperative insurance: a form of mutual insurance based on shared risk and cooperation, deemed permissible by contemporary scholars.
تكافل (takaful): A Sharia-compliant alternative to conventional insurance, based on mutual guarantee.

D) It is permissible only for property insurance.

Key Takeaway
Islamic law has the built-in capacity to address novel problems through ijtihad on its principles, as demonstrated by the permissibility of cooperative insurance.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Critique of the Classical Definition of Muhsan in Zina Punishment
Modern critical refinement of a classical hadd definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the classical Hanafi definition of muhsan, explain the author's critique based on the illa of betrayal with a current spouse, and evaluate the contemporary reinterpretation of rajm as ta'zir.

Core Idea
A muhsan's current spouse makes the betrayal greater; without this condition, the rationale for stoning is undermined.

This passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa challenges a cornerstone of the hadd punishment for zina: the definition of muhsan. Classical jurists defined it simply as one who has ever married and consummated, but al-Zarqa argues that this ignores the underlying wisdom (illa) that a currently married person who commits zina commits a greater betrayal. By critically examining the Quranic usage and the rationale, he proposes a refined definition that better aligns with the severity of the punishment. He also notes a contemporary view that stoning might be ta'ziri rather than a fixed hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classical Hanafi definition of muhsan
Recall that classical Hanafi fiqh defines muhsan as a free, adult, Muslim person who has had valid marriage and consummation, regardless of current marital status. This is the baseline that al-Zarqa critiques.
2
Understand the author's critique: missing the illa
Al-Zarqa argues that without requiring a spouse at the time of zina, the rationale for a harsher punishment—betrayal of a current spouse who could satisfy desires—is lost. This is the core of his methodological critique.
The illa (cause) is central to Hanafi usul; here it is used to question a traditional ruling.
3
Compare with Quranic usage of muhsan
Al-Zarqa points out that in Surah al-Nisa, 'muhsana' means a woman with a husband, and in Surah al-Nur, it means chaste or free. He argues consistency should require a current spouse for the zina punishment context.
4
Examine the alternative view: rajm as ta'zir
Some contemporary scholars classify stoning as a ta'zir (discretionary) punishment, not a fixed hadd, meaning it can be applied by the ruler when there are aggravating circumstances. This would reduce the fixed punishment to flogging for all fornicators.
This view is not mainstream Hanafi, but it shows the consequences of redefining muhsan.
5
Evaluate the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a person married, divorced, then committed zina as a single, classical law would stone them, but al-Zarqa's definition would only flog them. The contemporary ta'zir view would also shift stoning from a divine fixed right to a state discretion.
This highlights the real-world impact of the definitional dispute.

First Appendix to Chapter Twenty-One
Regarding the Punishment for the Crime of Zina
7/21 - The punishment for the crime of zina is that the fornicator and the adulteress be struck one hundred [?] – this is what the Quranic text came with: 'The adulteress and the fornicator – flog each one of them with one hundred lashes' (Surah al-Nur: 2/24).
Surah
And it came in the established Sunnah that this applies to the virgin fornicator (i.e., one who has not married). As for the muhsan (married person), his punishment is stoning with stones until death, which is among the harshest punishments due to the corruption caused by zina in society – mixing and loss of lineages and the rights dependent on lineage, financial and otherwise. It is a theft of honor that destroys families, causes bloodshed, corrupts morals, turns friendships into enmities, causes loss of trust and honesty, and may lead to other crimes. There is no doubt that these consequences are more severe in the case of the married traitor than in the case of the virgin. Therefore, the noble prophetic hadith came with a stricter punishment for the muhsan fornicator than the punishment for the virgin.
And it is necessary to observe two very important matters in this context, namely:
8/21 - First: That the earlier scholars interpreted 'muhsan' as one who had actually married and had intercourse in a valid Islamic marriage before committing zina, and they did not stipulate that he had a spouse at the time of his zina that would suffice him from this heinous crime, and no interpretation of 'muhsan' came from the prophetic hadith.
But I see that this interpretation has wasted the aspect that justifies the severer punishment for the muhsan over the virgin, and at the same time it contradicts the meaning of 'muhsan' as it came in the Great Quran in the statement of prohibited women, i.e., those whom it is forbidden for a man to marry, such as a man's daughter or sister, for example. For it came in Surah al-Nisa in the enumeration of prohibited women: 'And [forbidden to you are] the muhsanat among women...' meaning it is forbidden for a man to marry a muhsana woman. And the scholars of the Ummah – exegetes, hadith scholars, and jurists – have agreed that what is meant by 'muhsana' here is a woman with a husband, so a man may not under any circumstances marry a married woman, for it is not permissible for a woman to have two husbands!! Rather it is even forbidden by consensus based on another Quranic text to marry a woman during the waiting period from a previous marriage that has ended by divorce or death of the husband.
And it cannot be denied that 'ihsan' has another meaning in language that refers to a person who is chaste and not promiscuous, and the Quran also came with it in establishing the hadd of qadhf against one who accuses a muhsana woman of zina, as in His saying: 'And those who accuse muhsanat [chaste women] and then do not bring four witnesses – flog them...' and His saying: 'Indeed, those who accuse the muhsanat, the unaware believing women, are cursed...' (Surah al-Nur 23 and 24).
And it also came in the Quran using 'muhsana' in the sense of a free woman (not a slave woman).
So what seems to me to be the most appropriate and correct interpretation of 'ihsan' in the punishment of zina is that 'muhsan' – and it came in the prophetic hadith with the wording 'thayyib' (non-virgin) – is the person who has a spouse that suffices him from zina but he commits zina, for this is a greater crime and more deserving of a severe deterrent punishment, as long as no other interpretation has come from the Lawgiver?! However, there are contemporary scholars who hold that the punishment of stoning for the muhsan is ta'ziri (discretionary) and not among the hudud (fixed punishments), so it is delegated to the ruler when there are aggravating reasons for it, and upon that is carried what came in the established Sunnah of implementing the punishment of stoning. As for the original textual punishment for the fornicator, which is the hadd, it is flogging for the muhsan thayyib and others, as came in the Great Quran.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a modern jurist's critical analysis of the classical Hanafi (and generally Sunni) definition of 'muhsan' for the purpose of the zina punishment. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by affirming the Quranic 100 lashes for zina and the sunna that this applies to virgins, while the married (muhsan) are stoned. He then explains the rationale for the harsher punishment due to the greater harm caused by a married person's infidelity.

He then identifies two crucial points. The first is that classical scholars defined muhsan simply as one who has had valid marriage and consummation, regardless of whether he had a spouse at the time of the zina. Al-Zarqa criticizes this definition because it ignores the underlying reason (illa) for the severity: that the person had a spouse who could satisfy his desires, making his sin a greater betrayal. Moreover, he argues that this definition contradicts the Quranic usage of the word 'muhsan' in other contexts, where it refers to a woman with a husband (Surah al-Nisa) or a chaste person (Surah al-Nur). He also notes that in Surah al-Nur, 'muhsana' can mean a free woman.

Therefore, al-Zarqa proposes that the soundest interpretation is that 'muhsan' is equivalent to 'thayyib' (non-virgin) as in the hadith, but specifically one who had a spouse at the time of zina who could suffice him. This aligns with the rationale. He also mentions that some contemporary scholars consider the stoning punishment to be ta'ziri (discretionary) rather than a fixed hadd, leaving it to the ruler's discretion for aggravated cases, while the fixed hadd remains flogging. This is a modern critical refinement that questions the traditional understanding of the hadd punishment.

Key Terms
إحصان (ihsan): Legal term for the status of being muhsan; defined by classical jurists as having been married and consummated, but the author argues it should also require having a spouse at the time of zina.
محصن (muhsan): The person subject to the stoning punishment; classically one who has had valid marriage and consummation, but the author proposes it means one who has a spouse at the time of zina.
رجم (rajm): Stoning to death; the punishment for a muhsan fornicator according to established Sunnah. Author notes some contemporary scholars consider it ta'ziri not hadd.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment left to the judge/ruler; some contemporary scholars apply this to stoning for muhsan based on circumstances.
آية المحصنات (ayat al-muhsanat): The verse in Surah al-Nisa (4:24) that uses muhsanat to mean women with husbands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa, what is the main problem with the classical definition of muhsan?
A) It requires a current spouse, which is too strict.
B) It does not require a current spouse, thus missing the rationale for the harsher punishment.
C) It contradicts the hadith that says 'the thayyib is stoned'.
D) It includes slaves, which is unjust.

Key Takeaway
The definition of muhsan in zina punishment is not settled; understanding the underlying rationale (illa) is essential for correct application, though classical Hanafi remains the operative position.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Meanings of Ihsan and Its Role in Zina Punishment
Analyzing Quranic uses of muhsan and procedural difficulties in zina punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the different Quranic meanings of muhsan and explain how the definition of muhsan affects the punishment for zina, including the hadd vs ta'zir debate.

Core Idea
Muhsan means married in zina punishment; procedural conditions make stoning nearly inapplicable.

The term muhsan appears in several Quranic contexts with distinct meanings: married woman, chaste woman, and free woman. This lesson unpacks these meanings and focuses on the definition relevant to zina punishment. We then explore the rigorous proof requirements for the hadd punishment and the resulting shift to ta'zir, highlighting a contemporary scholarly debate on whether stoning is a fixed hadd or discretionary punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify Quranic uses of muhsan
List the three distinct meanings of muhsan in the Quran as presented by al-Zarqa: (1) married woman in prohibitions on marriage, (2) chaste woman in the context of qadhf, (3) free woman. Recognize that the same root word takes different legal meanings depending on context.
This step is essential to avoid conflating definitions across different rulings.
2
Define muhsan for zina punishment
Al-Zarqa argues that the correct definition for zina is the person who has a spouse that suffices them from zina, equivalent to thayyib (non-virgin) as per hadith. This definition makes the crime more severe and warrants stoning (rajm) according to classical Hanafi fiqh.
Note that other schools may have slight variations (e.g., including slave women).
3
Analyze the procedural hurdles for proving zina
The passage explains that zina can only be proven by confession repeated four times in separate sittings, or by testimony of four eyewitnesses—a near impossibility. If conditions fail, the punishment shifts from hadd to ta'zir due to doubt (shubha).
Remember the maxim: 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat' (hudud are warded off by doubts).
4
Evaluate the hadd vs ta'zir debate for stoning
Al-Zarqa presents the classical Hanafi view that stoning is a fixed hadd, but also notes contemporary scholars who consider it ta'zir delegated to the ruler. The practical consequence is whether stoning can be applied today as a mandatory punishment or only at the ruler's discretion.
This step requires you to weigh the evidence: mutawatir sunnah vs. Quranic primacy of flogging.
