الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه
المحصن عن البكر، وهو في الوقت نفسه مخالف لمعنى المحصن الذي وَرَد به القرآن العظيم في بيان المحرمات من النساء، أي اللاتي يحرم على الرجل الزواج بهن كبنت الرجل وأخته مثلاً. فقد جاء في سورة النساء في تعداد المحرمات قوله تعالى: وَالْمُحْصَنَتُ مِنَ النِّسَاء ... أي يحرم على الرجل أن يتزوج امرأة محصنة. وقد أجمع علماء الأمة من مفسرين و محدثين وفقهاء على أن المراد بالمحصنة هنا ذات الزوج، فلا يجوز الرجل بحال من الأحوال أن يتزوج امرأة متزوجة، إذ لا يجوز أن يجتمع على امرأة زوجان!! بل حتى يحرم بالإجماع بناء على نص قرآني آخر أن يتزوج هي في العدة من زواج سابق قد انحل بطلاق أو وفاة الزوج.

ولا ينكر أن للإحصان في اللغة معنى آخر يعبر به عن الشخص المصون غير المستهتر، وقد ورد به القرآن أيضاً في إيجاب حد القذف على من يتّهم امرأة محصنة بالزنى، وذلك في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالَّذِينَ يَرْمُونَ الْمُحْصَنَتِ ثُمَّ لَمْ يَأْتُوا بِأَرْبَعَةِ شُهَدَاءَ فَأَجَلِدُوهُمْ ... وقوله : وإِنَّ الَّذِينَ يَرْمُونَ الْمُحْصَنَتِ الْغَفِلَاتِ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ لُعِنُوا ...) (سورة النور ٢٣ و ٢٤).
وقد جاء أيضاً في القرآن استعمال المحصنة بمعنى الحرة (غير الرقيقة المملوكة) .

فالذي يبدو لي أن الأوْجَه والأصح في تفسير الإحصان في عقوبة الزنى أنَّ المحصن (وقد ورد في الحديث النبوي بلفظ التيب) هو الشخص الذي يكون له زوج يغنيه عن الزنى فيزني فإن هذا يكون أعظم جرماً واستحقاقاً لتشديد العقوبة الرادعة، ما دام لم يرد عن الشارع تفسير آخر؟! على أن هناك من العلماء العصريين من يرى أنَّ عقوبة الرجم للمحصن تعزيرية وليست من الحدود فهي مفوّضة إلى ولي الأمر عند وجود أسباب مشدّدة لها، وعلى ذلك يحمل ما ورد في السنة النبوية الثابتة من تطبيق عقوبة الرجم، أما العقوبة الأصلية النصية للزاني، وهي الحد ، فهي الجلد للمحصن الثيب ،وغيره كما جاء في القرآن العظيم.

ملحق أول بالفصل (۲۱) عقوبة جريمة الزنى

۹/۲۱ - الثاني : أنه لا يمكن في الواقع إثبات جريمة الزنى إلا بإقرار الزاني على نفسه، لأن إثبات الزنى بالشهادة يتطلب معاينة من أربعة شهود عدول (ثقات)، ورؤية تكاد تكون مستحيلة. وإن إقرار الزاني الموجب للحد أن يتكرر منه يجب أربع مرات في أربعة مجالس. فإذا لم تتوافر هذه الشرائط العسيرة في إثبات الزنى لأجل تطبيق عقوبة الحد (جلداً أو رجماً) تنتقل العقوبة إلى التعزير بالنوع والقدر الذي يراه الحاكم بحسب الأحوال، وذلك لحصول الشبهة في إثبات الجريمة، والحدود تدراً بالشبهات بنص الحديث النبوي.
ويلحظ أيضاً أن إقرار الزاني بالزنى، إذا رجع بعده المقر عن إقراره يسقط الحد برجوعه وتنتقل العقوبة إلى التعزير بما يكفي للزجر. وفي حالة الرجم إذا كان الزاني ثيباً محصناً يعتبر هروبه من مكان الرجم كالرجوع عن الإقرار تمتنع معه متابعة الرجم، وتنتقل عقوبته إلى التعزير.

في ضوء كل ذلك من الأحكام المقررة في السنة والفقه يتبين أن رجم الزاني المحصن هو في الشريعة عقوبة للتخويف أكثر منها للتطبيق لكي يعلم الزاني المحصن الذي له زوجة تغنيه ماذا يستحق من العقاب. ولا يكاد يمكن تطبيق عقوبة الرجم على زان إلا بإرادته حينما يقر على نفسه ويُصِرُّ . وأن الحالات التي وردت في السنة النبوية كلها من هذا القبيل.
Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Jurisprudence. The muhsan (married) as opposed to the virgin, and it is at the same time contrary to the meaning of muhsan that came in the Great Quran in the statement of prohibited women, i.e., those whom it is forbidden for a man to marry, such as a man's daughter and his sister for example. For it came in Surat an-Nisa' in the enumeration of prohibited women: 'And married women from among the women...' meaning it is forbidden for a man to marry a married woman. And the scholars of the ummah from exegetes, hadith specialists, and jurists have agreed that what is meant by muhsan here is a woman with a husband, so it is not permissible for a man under any circumstances to marry a married woman, since it is not permitted for two husbands to be together on one woman!! Indeed, it is even forbidden by consensus based on another Quranic text for him to marry her during the 'idda from a previous marriage that has been dissolved by divorce or the death of the husband. And it is not denied that ihsan in language has another meaning expressing a person who is chaste, not dissolute, and the Quran has also come with it in imposing the hadd of qadhf on one who accuses a chaste woman of zina, that is in His saying: 'And those who accuse chaste women then do not bring four witnesses - flog them...' and His saying: 'Indeed, those who accuse chaste, believing women unaware are cursed...' (Surat an-Nur 23, 24). And it has also come in the Quran the usage of muhsana to mean free (not a slave). So it appears to me that the most correct and sound interpretation of ihsan in the punishment of zina is that the muhsan (and it has come in the prophetic hadith with the word thayyib) is the person who has a spouse that suffices him from zina, and he commits zina, then this is a greater crime and more deserving of a severe deterrent punishment, as long as no other interpretation has come from the Lawgiver?! However, there are contemporary scholars who hold that the punishment of stoning for the muhsan is disciplinary (ta'ziri) and not from the hudud, so it is delegated to the ruler when there are aggravating circumstances, and upon that is based what is confirmed in the Sunnah of applying the punishment of stoning. As for the original textual punishment for the zani, which is the hadd, it is flogging for the married thayyib and others as comes in the Great Quran. First Appendix to Chapter (21) Punishment of the Crime of Zina. 9/21 - Second: It is not possible in reality to prove the crime of zina except by the confession of the zani himself, because proving zina by testimony requires the seeing by four upright (trustworthy) witnesses, a sighting that is almost impossible. And the confession of the zani that necessitates the hadd must be repeated by him four times in four sittings. So if these difficult conditions for proving zina for applying the hadd punishment (flogging or stoning) are not met, the punishment moves to ta'zir in type and amount as the ruler sees fit according to circumstances, due to the presence of doubt in proving the crime, and the hudud are warded off by doubts according to the text of the prophetic hadith. It is also noted that if the confessor to zina retracts his confession afterwards, the hadd falls due to his retraction and the punishment moves to ta'zir sufficient for deterrence. And in the case of stoning, if the zani is a married muhsan, his escape from the place of stoning is considered like retraction of confession, so the stoning is discontinued and his punishment moves to ta'zir. In light of all these established rulings in the Sunnah and jurisprudence, it becomes clear that the stoning of the muhsan zani is, in the Sharia, a punishment more for deterrence than for application, so that the muhsan zani who has a wife that suffices him knows what punishment he deserves. And it is hardly possible to apply the punishment of stoning to a zani except by his will when he confesses and persists. And the cases that have come in the prophetic Sunnah are all of this nature.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly analysis by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa on the concept of ihsan (muhsan) in Islamic criminal law, specifically in relation to the punishment for zina (unlawful sexual intercourse). The author begins by distinguishing the different Quranic usages of the term muhsan: (1) in the context of marriage prohibitions, muhsana means a married woman; (2) in the context of qadhf (false accusation), muhsana means a chaste woman; (3) it can also mean a free woman. He then argues that for the punishment of zina, the correct interpretation of muhsan is the one who has a spouse that suffices him from zina—this is the person who is thayyib (non-virgin) as per hadith. He notes that some contemporary scholars view stoning as a ta'zir (discretionary) punishment rather than a fixed hadd, thus putting it under the ruler's discretion. The second part of the passage discusses the procedural difficulties of proving zina: it requires either confession repeated four times or the testimony of four eyewitnesses, which is nearly impossible. If these conditions are not met, the punishment shifts from hadd to ta'zir. Additionally, if the confessor retracts, the hadd is dropped. He concludes that stoning is more of a deterrent than an applicable punishment, and its historical applications in the Sunnah occurred only when the offender confessed and persisted.

Key Terms
المحصن (al-muhsan): In this passage, muhsan is defined as a person who has a spouse that suffices them from zina, equivalent to thayyib (non-virgin), and thus liable to stoning if he commits zina.
الحد (al-hadd): A fixed, inviolable punishment prescribed by Allah; for zina, it is flogging for the non-muhsan and stoning for the muhsan according to classical fiqh.
التعزير (al-ta'zir): A discretionary punishment administered by the ruler or judge for crimes that do not have a fixed hadd, or when the conditions for hadd are not met.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Confession of the crime; for zina, it must be made four times in four separate sittings to establish the hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for a confession of zina to lead to a hadd punishment?
A) A single confession in one sitting
B) Confession repeated four times in four separate sittings
C) Confession accompanied by two witnesses
D) Confession made in front of the ruler

Key Takeaway
The punishment for zina depends on the definition of muhsan, and the procedural hurdles are so stringent that the hadd is rarely applied, often shifting to ta'zir.

The Principle of Necessity in Resolving the Riba Conflict
Exploring the shariah-based resolution for the riba-modern banking conflict via the necessity principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'al-darurat tubih al-mahdhurat' to evaluate whether temporary acceptance of interest-based transactions is permissible in Hanafi fiqh, and identify the conditions and limitations of such necessity.

Core Idea
Necessity can justify temporary deviation from riba prohibition, but only within strict Hanafi parameters.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi addresses the apparent conflict between the absolute prohibition of riba and the reality of modern banking. The author proposes that the principle of necessity (darurah) or need (hajah) can provide temporary relief until an Islamic economic system is established. In this lesson, we will examine the Hanafi framework of darurah and its application to financial transactions, a vital skill for muftis navigating contemporary economic life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Recognize the core conflict: Islam prohibits riba absolutely, yet modern financial systems rely on interest. The text presents this as a practical dilemma requiring a shariah-based solution.
2
Locate the Proposed Solution
The author suggests two avenues: reliance on darurah/need with temporary exceptions, or redefining the exact scope of prohibited riba by examining pre-Islamic practices. Focus on the first avenue for this lesson.
The second avenue involves a different method (tahqeeq al-manat) which is not covered here.
3
Define the Hanafi Qawida
State the maxim: الضرورات تبيح المحظورات (Necessities make prohibitions permissible). This is a major legal maxim (qa'ida kubra) with broad scope but strict conditions.
Hanafi usul requires that darurah be real, imminent, and no lawful alternative exists.
4
Apply the Qawida to the Case
Consider a scenario: a Muslim must engage in an interest-based bank account for safekeeping due to lack of Islamic banking options. Assess if this qualifies as darurah. The text suggests temporary permissibility until an Islamic system is established.
Darurah is evaluated case by case; collective need (hajah) may also justify permissibility per some Hanafi scholars.
5
Identify Limitations and Istithna'at
Explain that darurah is limited by its cause and duration. Once the necessity ends, the prohibition returns. Also, the maxim does not override explicit prohibitions that are inherently harmful (e.g., major sins).
Hanafis also require that the matter does not harm others or violate foundational principles.

ربا
التجارية والمصرفية اليوم كلها تقوم على أساس الفائدة التي هي جزئي تحدد نسبته القوانين فتطبيق النظرية الإسلامية في تحريم الربا يتنافى تنافياً ظاهراً مع نظام الفائدة والجواب: أن هذه المشكلة يمكن حلها في مبادىء الشريعة بطرق عديدة: إما بالاستناد إلى قاعدة الضرورات أو الحاجات والتدابير الاستثنائية الموقوتة إلى أن يقام في المجتمع الإسلامي نظام اقتصادي متجانس يغني الناس عن الالتجاء إلى نظام الفائدة وإما بالرجوع إلى تحديد الحالة الربوية التي كان عليها العرب وجاءت الشريعة بمنعها،
Second Appendix to Chapter Twenty-One: The Problem of the Usury-Based Interest System. Introduction: I have rewritten this subject in this new version now a complete rewrite to remove, first, confusion that occurred in the minds of some readers from some expressions in previous editions, and to add, second, some important economic and legal facts indicated by research that appeared during the forty years that passed after writing the previous version of this subject in the early 1950s, and also indicated to some extent by the activities of Islamic financial institutions since their inception in the mid-1970s. 11/21 - The previous version of this subject in past editions of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi: The Third Problem: The system of interest in commercial transactions and banking operations. Islam prohibits riba absolutely and fights it relentlessly, while today commercial and banking transactions are all based on interest, which is a part determined by laws. Thus applying the Islamic theory of prohibiting riba apparently conflicts with the interest system. The answer: This problem can be resolved in the principles of shariah in many ways: either by relying on the principle of necessities or needs and temporary exceptional measures until an integrated Islamic economic system is established that makes people independent from resorting to the interest system, or by going back to determining the riba situation that the Arabs had and which shariah came to prohibit.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a preface and revision of an earlier appendix on the problem of interest (riba) in modern banking. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that he has rewritten the topic to correct misunderstandings and add recent research and the experience of Islamic financial institutions over forty years. He then reproduces the earlier formulation: that Islamic law strictly prohibits riba, while modern financial systems rely on interest (considered a form of riba). The apparent conflict can be resolved using several shariah principles. First, the principle of necessity (الضرورات) or need (الحاجات) may allow temporary exceptions until an Islamic economic system is established. Second, one may re-examine the specific riba practices of the pre-Islamic Arabs that the shariah prohibited, implying that not all interest may be prohibited. The author indicates that multiple shariah-compliant solutions exist within the legal framework.

Key Terms
ربا (riba): Usury or interest; any excess in a loan or exchange without proper countervalue, prohibited in Islam.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity that permits normally prohibited acts under certain conditions.
حاجة (hajah): Need; a lesser degree of necessity that may permit some relaxations but not complete prohibition lifting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle explained in this lesson, when is it permissible to engage in an interest-based transaction due to necessity?
A) Whenever the Muslim believes it is necessary for his financial comfort.
B) Only in case of life-threatening necessity or severe widespread need, with no halal alternative, and with the intention to return to compliance.
C) Never, because riba is absolutely forbidden under all circumstances.
D) Only for government bonds, as they are considered public welfare.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-darurat tubih al-mahdhurat' offers a temporary and conditional escape from the riba prohibition in modern banking, but only under genuine and verifiable necessity.

Legal Stratagems for Riba: The Hanafi View on Bay' al-Mu'amala
Author presents Hanafi-based solutions for riba in banking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the later Hanafi position on bay' al-mu'amala and evaluate its contemporary application as a solution to riba in banking.

Core Idea
Later Hanafis permit the ruler to fix a profit rate on loans via bay' al-mu'amala, a conditional stratagem, not a blanket endorsement of interest.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa presents two creative solutions to the problem of riba in modern banking. The second solution draws on the rulings of later Hanafi jurists who allowed the ruler to set a profit rate on loans through an ancillary sale contract, known as bay' al-mu'amala. This lesson unpacks the conditions and limits of that opinion, and distinguishes it from outright permissibility of interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two additional solutions
From the passage, note the two ideas al-Zarqa presented at the Princeton conference: nationalization of banks and the later Hanafi opinion on bay' al-mu'amala.
The author lists five solutions in total; focus on these two as they are detailed.
2
Examine the later Hanafi opinion
Understand the ruling: the ruler may determine a profit (fā’ida) on loans via an ancillary contract, called bay' al-mu'amala, due to need (hāja). This is considered permissible by later Hanafis (mutakhkhirūn).
The primary source cited is Ibn Abidin's Radd al-Muhtar, Kitab al-Buyu'.
3
Analyze the conditions
Identify the conditions: the ruler must set the rate; the loan must be structured as a sale (e.g., the ruler sells a commodity on credit and buys it back for cash at a lower price); the interest is capped at the ruler's specified percentage; people cannot exceed it.
This is a legal stratagem (hīla) that requires genuine trade underlying the transaction.
4
Differentiate from general permissibility
Al-Zarqa explicitly states he never held the opinion that bank interest is generally permissible. The bay' al-mu'amala is a limited concession under state authority, not a personal right to charge interest.
Many readers misinterpreted al-Zarqa's presentation as his own fatwa.
5
Evaluate contemporary applicability
Assess whether modern Islamic banking practices like tawarruq or organized murabaha can be analogized to bay' al-mu'amala. Consider the role of the state and the condition of actual trade.
The hila is only valid if it avoids riba al-fadl and riba al-nasi'ah through genuine sale.

الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه حيث كان المرابون يتحكمون كما يشاءون بالفقير المحتاج إلى القرض لإقامة أود حياته. ومن هذه الدراسة وفي ضوئها يعرف عندئذ ما يجوز وما يمتنع شرعاً من الشؤون المصرفية وأعمال المصارف. ولا ننسى أن تحريم الربا في الإسلام لا يمكن أن يعتبر بعدم صلاحه لحياة اقتصادية عصرية فإننا نرى الآن نظاماً اقتصادياً متطرقاً يهدد العالم أجمع بقوته وهو يقوم على منع الرأسمالية والربا (١)
١٢/٢١ - وكنت في عام ١٩٥٣ م دُعيت إلى تقديم بحث في مؤتمر الثقافة الإسلامية في جامعة برنستون في أمريكا بعنوان «الشريعة الإسلامية وحقوق الأسرة فيها (۲)، واستحسنتُ فيما بعد أن أجعله فصلاً أول في المدخل الفقهي لأنه يلخص الأسس التي يقوم عليها النظام القانوني في الشريعة بجميع فروعه وكان البحث المذكور يشتمل على قسم عن المشكلات العصرية التي تواجهها الشريعة ومنها مشكلة نظام الفائدة، ويتضمن فكرتين إضافيتين طرحتهما للنقاش، ضمن الحلول القابلة للنقاش حول الفائدة، لكنني لم أجدهما مناسبتين للإدراج في المدخل الفقهي في طبعاته السابقة، وهما:
۱ - تأميم المصارف لحساب الدولة فينتفى عندئذ معنى الربا من الفائدة الجزئية التي تُؤخذ عن القرض إذ تعود عندئذ إلى خزينة الدولة لمصلحة المجموع وينتفي محذور تمركز رؤوس الأموال في أيدي فريق من المرابين .

1 - قاعدة الضرورة.
٢ - إقامة نظام اقتصادي يغني الناس عن الفائدة .
- دراسة متعمقة للحالة الربوية التي كان عليها العرب عند ظهور الإسلام، أو بتعبير آخر ربا الجاهلية، حيث يظن بعض الباحثين أن الربا فيها قد كان على قروض استهلاكية وليس على قروض استثمارية كفوائد البنوك اليوم.
٤ - تأميم المصارف لحساب الدولة.
٥ - متابعة متأخري الحنفية في رأيهم الآنف الذكر.
وقد ظنَّ بعض القراء مما سبق بيانه، ظناً خاطئاً منه، أنني أقول بإباحة الفوائد المصرفية عموماً، أو بإباحتها في القروض الاستثمارية دون الاستهلاكية والحقيقة أنَّ هذا لم يكن لي برأي في يوم من الأيام، وإنما عرضته مطروحاً للنقاش.
•
١٣/٢١ - نظرتي الحاضرة إلى الحلول الخمسة المذكورة:
كنت حين كتبت ما سبق على شعور بعظيم الحاجة إلى تمحيص هذه الحلول المتصوَّرة التي يفكر بكل منها بعض المعاصرين جميع
Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh where usurers used to control as they wished over the poor needy person for a loan to sustain his life. And from this study and in its light one then knows what is permissible and what is prohibited legally from banking affairs and operations of banks. And we do not forget that the prohibition of riba in Islam cannot be considered as its unsuitability for a modern economic life, for we now see an extreme economic system threatening the whole world with its power, and it is based on preventing capitalism and riba (1). 12/21 - And in 1953 CE I was invited to present a paper at the Conference of Islamic Culture at Princeton University in America entitled 'Islamic Law and Family Rights in It' (2), and I later approved to make it a first chapter in the Fiqh Introduction because it summarizes the foundations upon which the legal system in the Shariah is based in all its branches. That paper included a section on contemporary problems facing the Shariah, including the problem of the interest system, and contained two additional ideas that I put forward for discussion, among the discussable solutions regarding interest, but I did not find them suitable for inclusion in the Fiqh Introduction in its previous editions, and they are: 1 - Nationalization of banks on behalf of the state, then the meaning of riba would disappear from the partial interest taken on the loan since it would then return to the state treasury for the benefit of the public, and the danger of concentration of capital in the hands of a group of usurers would disappear. 2 - The opinion of the later jurists of the Hanafi school who stipulated the permissibility of the ruler determining a financial benefit for loans concluded with an ancillary contract due to need, and they said that then it is not legally permissible for people to exceed in the interest on loans the specified percentage set by the ruler. And they called this measure: 'bay' al-mu'amala', and the consensus of the jurists in the school was on its permissibility (see section 13/15 above, and the book Radd al-Muhtar by Ibn Abidin, vol. 4, book of sales, section on bay' al-mu'amala). From the foregoing it results that what I wrote earlier in the early 1950s in this Fiqh Introduction and in the Princeton paper was written when five solutions for the problem of usurious interest were circulating in the minds of those concerned, since banks, upon which the economic wheel in the countries depends, do not deal except on its basis, and they are: 1 - The principle of necessity. 2 - Establishing an economic system that makes people independent of interest. 3 - An in-depth study of the usurious situation that the Arabs were upon at the advent of Islam, or in other words the riba of Jahiliyyah, where some researchers think that riba at that time was on consumption loans and not on investment loans like bank interest today. 4 - Nationalization of banks on behalf of the state. 5 - Following the later Hanafis in their aforementioned opinion. And some readers, from what was previously explained, mistakenly thought that I say that bank interests are generally permissible, or that they are permissible in investment loans without consumption loans. The fact is that this was never my opinion at any time, but rather I presented it for discussion. • 13/21 - My present view on the five mentioned solutions: When I wrote the above, I was aware of the great need to scrutinize these imagined solutions, each of which some contemporaries are thinking about.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical discussion by the author Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa on the contemporary problem of riba (interest) in banking. He begins by stating that Islamic prohibition of riba is not incompatible with modern economic life; rather, it offers a viable alternative. He then references his 1953 paper at Princeton University, which included two additional solutions for the interest problem that he did not include in earlier editions of this book. The first solution is the nationalization of banks so that interest payments go to the state treasury rather than private individuals, thus removing the exploitative element of riba. The second solution is drawn from later Hanafi jurists (mutakhkhirin) who permitted the ruler (sultan) to fix a rate of profit on loans via an ancillary contract, called 'bay' al-mu'amala' (transaction sale). The author notes that this measure is considered permissible by consensus within the Hanafi school. He then lists five solutions that were circulating among scholars: (1) the principle of necessity (darurah), (2) establishing an alternative economic system, (3) reexamining the nature of pre-Islamic riba as consumption-based versus investment-based (today's bank interest), (4) nationalizing banks, and (5) following the later Hanafi opinion on bay' al-mu'amala. The author clarifies that he is not endorsing any of these as his own fatwa; he simply presented them for scholarly discussion. This passage demonstrates the Hanafi approach to confronting modern financial challenges through ijtihad within the school's principles.

Key Terms
ربا (ribā): Usury or interest prohibited in Islamic law; here refers to any excess in a loan transaction.
بيع المعاملة (bay‘ al-mu‘āmala): A legal stratagem (hila) where the ruler fixes a profit rate on loans via a supplementary sale contract, considered permissible by later Hanafis.
فائدة (fā’ida): Interest or profit on loans; in this passage, synonymous with riba in the context of banking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many solutions for the problem of usurious interest were circulating in the 1950s, as mentioned by al-Zarqa?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi opinion on bay' al-mu'amala is a conditional legal stratagem, not a general license for interest, and must be applied under state authority and genuine trade.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Refining Fatwa: The Mufti's Self-Correction on Bank Interest
Author reevaluates his earlier discussion of five solutions to riba-based bank interest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa hierarchy for evaluating proposed solutions to bank interest, identifying which solutions are viable under Hanafi usul and which are refuted by definitive texts.

Core Idea
A mufti must continuously refine his position as evidence emerges; bank interest is riba by definitive texts.

In this passage, the distinguished Hanafi jurist Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa revisits his earlier tentative discussion of five solutions to the problem of bank interest. He clarifies that his original presentation was an invitation to debate, not a personal endorsement. Now, after reviewing definitive evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah and the consensus of contemporary scholars, he affirms that bank interest (الفائدة المصرفية) is unequivocally riba, prohibited by categorical texts. This lesson examines the five proposed solutions, the author's current evaluation of each, and the vital process of critical refinement that every mufti must practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Proposed Solutions
Read the passage carefully and list the five solutions that were circulating in the early 1950s: the rule of necessity, an interest-free economy, restricting riba to consumption loans, nationalizing banks, and following the later Hanafi opinion on bay' al-mu'amalah.
These solutions reflect common attempts to address bank interest without declaring it outright riba.
2
Distinguish Presentation from Endorsement
Note the author's explicit statement that his earlier discussion was not his personal opinion but a presentation for debate. This is a crucial adab for scholars: even when exploring possibilities, one must clearly demarcate tentative hypotheses from established positions.
Many readers mistakenly assumed he endorsed permissibility; he corrects this misimpression.
3
Recognize the Author's Final Position
Identify the author's current conclusion: bank interest is 'from the very core of riba prohibited by the definitive texts of the Book and Sunnah.' He cites subsequent writings by trustworthy scholars that dispelled doubts and provided irrefutable evidence.
The key term here is 'النصوص القطعية' (definitive texts), which in Hanafi usul indicate certain knowledge that cannot be overridden by mere argument.
4
Evaluate Each Solution in Light of the Final Position
For each of the five solutions, state whether the author now accepts it as a valid basis for permitting bank interest. Only the rule of necessity is acknowledged as theoretically possible, but he emphasizes that being compelled to *take* riba is extremely difficult to imagine. The other four are effectively set aside as either impractical, refuted, or inapplicable.
This step demonstrates how a mufti applies the hierarchy: definitive textual evidence (nusus qat'iyya) takes precedence over analogical reasoning or legal stratagems.

But it is very difficult to imagine being compelled to take riba. 15/21 - As for the second solution, establishing an economic system that makes people independent of riba, this, with our firm belief in its possibility, our knowledge of its modality was not clear in the early 1950s, but it has become, praise be to God, clear today after research, studies, and application in the field of Islamic economics. 16/21 - The aim of mentioning the third solution was to call for scrutiny of the idea that the loans prevalent among the Arabs when Islam came prohibiting riba were consumption loans for the needy to maintain the necessities of life.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a supplementary discussion (ملحق) where the author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, revisits his earlier writings on the problem of bank interest and riba. He first summarizes five solutions that had been proposed: (1) invoking the rule of necessity, (2) establishing an interest-free economy, (3) restricting the prohibition to pre-Islamic 'riba al-jahiliyyah' (which was on consumption loans), (4) nationalizing banks, and (5) adopting the later Hanafi opinion that permits 'bay' al-mu'amalah' (a sale transaction that circumvents interest). He clarifies that his earlier presentation of these ideas was not an endorsement but an invitation to debate. He then states his current view (after further scholarly discussion) that bank interest is unequivocally riba prohibited by definitive Qur'anic and prophetic texts. He evaluates each solution: Necessity can theoretically permit paying riba, but being compelled to *take* it is hard to imagine. The second solution (Islamic economics) was unclear then but now well-developed. The third solution (distinguishing consumption vs. investment loans) is not elaborated fully here but was intended to prompt scrutiny. The author's tone is one of scholarly integrity: he acknowledges earlier tentative views and now affirms the definitive prohibition, showing how a mufti should refine his position in light of evidence.

Key Terms
الربا (al-ribā): Usury or interest, specifically bank interest which the author now affirms is prohibited by definitive texts.
الفوائد المصرفية (al-fawāʾid al-maṣrifiyyah): Bank interest, which the author concludes is the same as the prohibited riba.
قاعدة الضرورة (qāʿidat al-ḍarūrah): The legal maxim 'Necessity permits the prohibited', which can apply to taking riba only under strict conditions.
بيع المعاملة (bayʿ al-muʿāmalah): A legal stratagem (hila) involving a sale transaction to avoid riba, considered permissible by Hanafi jurists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Bank interest is riba prohibited by definitive texts; prior proposed solutions do not change this, and a mufti must rely on categorical evidence over speculative opinions.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Evaluating the Rule of Necessity (Qāʿidat al-Ḍarūra) as a Solution to Riba
Critique of applying the necessity maxim to riba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the application of qāʿidat al-ḍarūra to riba and identify its legal conditions and limitations.

Core Idea
Necessity may permit paying riba but not taking it, and its conditions are so strict that it is practically inapplicable.

The rule of necessity (qāʿidat al-ḍarūra) allows prohibited acts under extreme duress. Dr. al-Zarqa examines whether this maxim can justify dealing with bank interest. He concludes that while theoretically possible, the conditions are nearly impossible to fulfill for taking riba.

Guided Path
1
State the generic rule
Recall the Hanafi maxim: 'al-ḍarūra tubīḥu al-maḥẓūrāt' (necessity permits the prohibited). Note its conditions: the harm must be certain, imminent, and unavoidable.
2
Identify the proposed application
Some researchers proposed that people or institutions compelled by economic circumstances may use riba-based banking out of necessity.
3
Analyze the author's critique
Al-Zarqa distinguishes between paying riba (as a borrower) and taking riba (as a lender). He argues that while paying may be necessary in dire straits, taking riba is almost never compelled.
The difficulty lies in imagining a situation where one is forced to LEND at interest.
4
Determine the scope
Even if necessity applies, it only permits the minimum required to remove the harm; it does not legalize riba as a normal practice.
The maxim 'al-ḍarūra tuqaddar bi-qadarihā' applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أما الخل الأول، وهو قاعدة الضرورة، فيمكن أن يحتاج إليه في أي زمان أو مكان فرد أو جماعة وهو حين تتحقق شروطه الشرعية يبيح دفع الربا. لكن من العسير جداً تصور الاضطرار إلى أخذ الربا . ١٥/٢١ - وأما الحل الثاني وهو إقامة نظام اقتصادي يغني الناس عن الربا، فهذا مع إيماننا الراسخ بإمكانه، فإنَّ معرفتنا بكيفيته لم تكن واضحة في مطلع الخمسينيات، لكنها أصبحت بحمد الله جلية اليوم بعدما تم من بحوث ودراسات وتطبيق في مجال الاقتصاد الإسلامي. ٢١/ ١٦ - وكان الهدف من ذكر الحل الثالث هو الدعوة إلى تمحيص الفكرة القائلة بأن القروض التي كانت شائعة بين العرب حين جاء الإسلام بتحريم الربا كانت قروضاً استهلاكية للمحتاجين لإقامة أود العيش ولم تكن قروضاً لأغراض التجارة والاستثمار وأن أخذ الربا عليها هو المقصود بربا الجاهلية الذي حرمه الإسلام. وكان بعض المفكرين العصريين قد أكدوا على هذه الفكرة والتمسوا فيها مخرجاً للقول بأنَّ القروض للتمويل الاستثماري والإنتاجي تكون بمنأى عن التحريم وانتهوا من ذلك إلى تحليل الفوائد المصرفية . وقد مخص هذه الفكرة عدد من الباحثين فلم يجدوها صحيحة تاريخياً، بل أوردوا أدلة وقرائن كثيرة مقنعة لا تترك مجالاً للشك بأن القروض الاستثمارية كانت معروفة ومنتشرة في المجتمع العربي وقت البعثة (۱) وهي التي كانت تموّل رحلتي الشتاء والصيف التجاريتين لقريش. ۱۷/۲۱ - وفي شأن الحل الرابع وهو تأميم المصارف لحساب الدولة، قد بينت بحوث الاقتصاديين المسلمين أنه لا ينتظر من التأميم في ذاته، أن يحول دون الظلم الناجم عن الربا أو دون تمركز رؤوس الأموال. ذلك أنَّ المصارف هي في الحقيقة وسيط يعمل في أموال المدخرين فيقدمها للمستثمرين فتستوي في ذلك المصارف الخاصة والمملوكة للدولة، بلا فرق أن عائد الوساطة يذهب في الأخيرة إلى خزانة الدولة. فإذا كانت العلاقة التمويلية التي تتم فيها الوساطة بين المدخرين والمستثمرين هي علاقة ربوية، فإنَّ حقيقة الربا ومحاذيره لا ينتفيان ولو كان الوسيط هو الدولة . ويقال مثل ذلك عن تمركز رؤوس الأموال، فالبحوث الاقتصادية تشير إلى أن التمويل الربوي يميل بصورة عفوية إلى الانسياب نحو الأغنياء في (۱) انظر عرضاً شاملاً وخلاصة شافية في هذا الموضوع في بحث د. رفيق المصري : ربا القرض وأدلة تحريمه جدة مركز أبحاث الاقتصاد الإسلامي بجامعة الملك عبد العزيز ١٤١٠ هـ (طبعة ٢). ٢٩٧
Chapter Four: Deriving Contemporary Codification from Fiqh and reviewing historical evidence about the state of riba in the Jahiliyyah and the legal evidence for the final ruling that the Book and Sunnah established on the issue of riba, and whether banking interest enters into it, then reaching the sound juristic opinion on excluding what must be excluded from these conceivable solutions, and establishing the final ruling on these banking interests which it is not permissible to conceive of otherwise in light of the definitive textual indications. Afterwards, writings and responses from trustworthy contemporary scholars appeared that dispelled the fog and proved with evidence that admits no doubt: that banking interests dealt with by banks are the very essence of riba prohibited by definitive texts from the Book and Sunnah. However, it is useful for me to state my current view on the five solutions I mentioned earlier, among which researchers' thinking oscillated: 14/21 - As for the first solution, which is the rule of necessity (qāʿidat al-ḍarūra), it may be needed at any time or place, by an individual or a group, and when its legal conditions are fulfilled it permits paying riba. But it is very difficult to imagine being compelled to take riba. 15/21 - As for the second solution, which is establishing an economic system that makes people independent of riba, despite our firm belief in its possibility, our knowledge of its modality was not clear at the beginning of the 1950s, but it has become, praise be to God, clear today after research, studies, and application in the field of Islamic economics. 16/21 - The purpose of mentioning the third solution was to call for scrutiny of the idea that the loans that were common among the Arabs when Islam came prohibiting riba were consumption loans for the needy to support their livelihood, and were not loans for trade and investment, and that taking riba on them is what is meant by the riba of Jahiliyyah that Islam prohibited. Some contemporary thinkers had insisted on this idea and sought in it a way out to say that loans for investment and productive financing are immune from prohibition, and they concluded from that to legalize banking interest. A number of researchers examined this idea and found it historically incorrect; rather, they provided many convincing evidences and indications leaving no room for doubt that investment loans were known and widespread in Arab society at the time of the mission (1), and they were the ones financing the winter and summer trade journeys of Quraysh. 17/21 - Regarding the fourth solution, which is nationalizing banks for the state account, the research of Muslim economists has shown that nationalization in itself is not expected to prevent the injustice resulting from riba or prevent the concentration of capital. That is because banks are in fact an intermediary that works with the funds of savers and presents them to investors, and in this regard private banks and state-owned banks are equal, with no difference that the return of the intermediary goes in the latter to the state treasury. So if the financing relationship in which intermediation occurs between savers and investors is a usurious relationship, then the reality of riba and its harms are not eliminated even if the intermediary is the state. The same is said regarding the concentration of capital; economic research indicates that usurious financing tends spontaneously to flow towards the rich. (1) See a comprehensive presentation and a sufficient summary on this topic in the research of Dr. Rafiq al-Masri: Riba al-Qard and the Evidence for Its Prohibition, Jeddah, Center for Research in Islamic Economics, King Abdulaziz University, 1410 AH (2nd edition). 297
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents a contemporary legal analysis of the riba problem by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa. It falls under legal philosophy as it discusses the methodology of deriving rulings in modern contexts. The author first reaffirms that banking interest is definitively prohibited riba, citing scholarly consensus. He then evaluates five solutions previously proposed by researchers: (1) the rule of necessity, which theoretically allows riba under dire necessity but is practically inconceivable for taking riba; (2) establishing an Islamic economic system, which is now possible with modern research; (3) the historical claim that pre-Islamic loans were only for consumption, which is refuted by evidence that investment loans existed; (4) nationalization of banks, which does not eliminate the usurious nature of the relationship; and (5) the fifth solution is mentioned in the text but not elaborated in these pages (the author ends with an evaluation of the fourth). The commentary emphasizes that a mufti must critically assess proposed solutions against definitive texts and empirical evidence, avoiding superficial fixes.

Key Terms
قاعدة الضرورة (qāʿidat al-ḍarūra): The legal maxim that necessity permits prohibited acts when certain conditions are met.
ربا (ribā): Prohibited excess or interest on loans.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Dr. al-Zarqa, why is it 'very difficult' to be compelled to take riba (i.e., receive interest)?
A) Because taking interest is always optional.
B) Because one can always choose to lend without interest.
C) Because the lender is not in a state of necessity; it is the borrower who may be in need.
D) Because taking riba incurs severe sin.

Key Takeaway
The rule of necessity cannot be used to justify the widespread practice of riba; it is an exceptional, limited relief for severe individual need only.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner

Evaluating the Islamic Economic System as an Alternative to Riba
Recognition of the potential and progress of an Islamic economic system.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the feasibility and current status of an Islamic economic system as a solution to the riba problem.

Core Idea
An Islamic economic system is possible and now clearer, but in the 1950s its modality was not well understood.

The second proposed solution was to establish a complete Islamic economic system free of interest. Dr. al-Zarqa affirms its possibility but notes historical lack of clarity. He then points out that, decades later, substantial progress has been made.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proposal
Some argued that the best long-term solution is to replace the riba-based system with a comprehensive Islamic economic system.
2
Identify the author's assessment at the time
In the early 1950s, the author states that although the possibility was firmly believed, the 'how' was not clear.
3
Note the update
By the time of writing, research and applications in Islamic economics have clarified the modality, making the solution more concrete.

Source Text

الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه واستعراض الأدلة التاريخية عن حالة ربا الجاهلية والأدلة الشرعية على الحكم النهائي الذي قرّره الكتاب والسنة في قضية الربا وهل تدخل فيه الفوائد المصرفية، ثم الوصول إلى الرأي الفقهي السديد في استبعاد ما يجب استبعاده من هذه الحلول المتصوّرة، وتقرير الحكم النهائي في هذه الفوائد المصرفية الذي لا يجوز تصور خلافه في ضوء دلالات النصوص القطعية . وقد ظهرت بعد ذلك كتابات وردود من العلماء المعاصرين الثقات بددت الضباب وأثبتت بالأدلة التي لا تقبل الارتياب : أن الفوائد المصرفية التي تتعامل بها البنوك هي من صميم الربا المحرّم بالنصوص القطعية من الكتاب والسنة . على أنَّ من المفيد أن أبين نظرتي الحاضرة إلى الحلول الخمسة التي ذكرتها آنفاً، وكان تفكير الباحثين يتردد بينها: ١٤/٢١ - Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
7 min
Evaluating the Historical Claim about Pre-Islamic Loans (Consumption vs. Investment)
Historical refutation of the claim that only consumption loans were ribawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the historical claim that pre-Islamic riba only involved consumption loans and not investment loans, using the evidence provided in the passage.

Core Idea
Investment loans existed in pre-Islamic Arabia and were included in the riba prohibition, refuting the attempt to exempt modern bank interest.

A third solution argued that the prohibited riba of Jahiliyyah only applied to consumption loans for the needy, and thus modern investment loans should be allowed. Dr. al-Zarqa and other researchers refute this by showing that investment loans were common in pre-Islamic society.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proposition
Some modern thinkers claimed that pre-Islamic loans were only for consumption (urgent personal needs), so the prohibition was limited to those. They argued investment loans are different and should be halal.
2
Review the historical evidence
The author states that researchers found convincing evidence that investment loans (e.g., financing trade caravans of Quraysh) were widespread before Islam.
3
Connect to the ruling
Since investment loans were known and practiced, they fall under the same prohibition as consumption loans. Therefore, modern bank interest (often for investment) is also riba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه واستعراض الأدلة التاريخية عن حالة ربا الجاهلية والأدلة الشرعية على الحكم النهائي الذي قرّره الكتاب والسنة في قضية الربا وهل تدخل فيه الفوائد المصرفية، ثم الوصول إلى الرأي الفقهي السديد في استبعاد ما يجب استبعاده من هذه الحلول المتصوّرة، وتقرير الحكم النهائي في هذه الفوائد المصرفية الذي لا يجوز تصور خلافه في ضوء دلالات النصوص القطعية . وقد ظهرت بعد ذلك كتابات وردود من العلماء المعاصرين الثقات بددت الضباب وأثبتت بالأدلة التي لا تقبل الارتياب : أن الفوائد المصرفية التي تتعامل بها البنوك هي من صميم الربا المحرّم بالنصوص القطعية من الكتاب والسنة . على أنَّ من المفيد أن أبين نظرتي الحاضرة إلى الحلول الخمسة التي ذكرتها آنفاً، وكان تفكير الباحثين يتردد بينها: ١٤/٢١ - أما الخل الأول، وهو قاعدة الضرورة، فيمكن أن يحتاج إليه في أي زمان أو مكان فرد أو جماعة وهو حين تتحقق شروطه الشرعية يبيح دفع الربا. لكن من العسير جداً تصور الاضطرار إلى أخذ الربا . ١٥/٢١ - وأما الحل الثاني وهو إقامة نظام اقتصادي يغني الناس عن الربا، فهذا مع إيماننا الراسخ بإمكانه، فإنَّ معرفتنا بكيفيته لم تكن واضحة في مطلع الخمسينيات، لكنها أصبحت بحمد الله جلية اليوم بعدما تم من بحوث ودراسات وتطبيق في مجال الاقتصاد الإسلامي. ٢١/ ١٦ - وكان الهدف من ذكر الحل الثالث هو الدعوة إلى تمحيص الفكرة القائلة بأن القروض التي كانت شائعة بين العرب حين جاء الإسلام بتحريم الربا كانت قروضاً استهلاكية للمحتاجين لإقامة أود العيش ولم تكن قروضاً لأغراض التجارة والاستثمار وأن أخذ الربا عليها هو المقصود بربا الجاهلية الذي حرمه الإسلام. وكان بعض المفكرين العصريين قد أكدوا على هذه الفكرة والتمسوا فيها مخرجاً للقول بأنَّ القروض للتمويل الاستثماري والإنتاجي تكون بمنأى عن التحريم وانتهوا من ذلك إلى تحليل الفوائد المصرفية . وقد مخص هذه الفكرة عدد من الباحثين فلم يجدوها صحيحة تاريخياً، بل أوردوا أدلة وقرائن كثيرة مقنعة لا تترك مجالاً للشك بأن القروض الاستثمارية كانت معروفة ومنتشرة في المجتمع العربي وقت البعثة (۱) وهي التي كانت تموّل رحلتي الشتاء والصيف التجاريتين لقريش. ۱۷/۲۱ - وفي شأن الحل الرابع وهو تأميم المصارف لحساب الدولة، قد بينت بحوث الاقتصاديين المسلمين أنه لا ينتظر من التأميم في ذاته، أن يحول دون الظلم الناجم عن الربا أو دون تمركز رؤوس الأموال. ذلك أنَّ المصارف هي في الحقيقة وسيط يعمل في أموال المدخرين فيقدمها للمستثمرين فتستوي في ذلك المصارف الخاصة والمملوكة للدولة، بلا فرق أن عائد الوساطة يذهب في الأخيرة إلى خزانة الدولة. فإذا كانت العلاقة التمويلية التي تتم فيها الوساطة بين المدخرين والمستثمرين هي علاقة ربوية، فإنَّ حقيقة الربا ومحاذيره لا ينتفيان ولو كان الوسيط هو الدولة . ويقال مثل ذلك عن تمركز رؤوس الأموال، فالبحوث الاقتصادية تشير إلى أن التمويل الربوي يميل بصورة عفوية إلى الانسياب نحو الأغنياء في (۱) انظر عرضاً شاملاً وخلاصة شافية في هذا الموضوع في بحث د. رفيق المصري : ربا القرض وأدلة تحريمه جدة مركز أبحاث الاقتصاد الإسلامي بجامعة الملك عبد العزيز ١٤١٠ هـ (طبعة ٢). ٢٩٧
Chapter Four: Deriving Contemporary Codification from Fiqh and reviewing historical evidence about the state of riba in the Jahiliyyah and the legal evidence for the final ruling that the Book and Sunnah established on the issue of riba, and whether banking interest enters into it, then reaching the sound juristic opinion on excluding what must be excluded from these conceivable solutions, and establishing the final ruling on these banking interests which it is not permissible to conceive of otherwise in light of the definitive textual indications. Afterwards, writings and responses from trustworthy contemporary scholars appeared that dispelled the fog and proved with evidence that admits no doubt: that banking interests dealt with by banks are the very essence of riba prohibited by definitive texts from the Book and Sunnah. However, it is useful for me to state my current view on the five solutions I mentioned earlier, among which researchers' thinking oscillated: 14/21 - As for the first solution, which is the rule of necessity (qāʿidat al-ḍarūra), it may be needed at any time or place, by an individual or a group, and when its legal conditions are fulfilled it permits paying riba. But it is very difficult to imagine being compelled to take riba. 15/21 - As for the second solution, which is establishing an economic system that makes people independent of riba, despite our firm belief in its possibility, our knowledge of its modality was not clear at the beginning of the 1950s, but it has become, praise be to God, clear today after research, studies, and application in the field of Islamic economics. 16/21 - The purpose of mentioning the third solution was to call for scrutiny of the idea that the loans that were common among the Arabs when Islam came prohibiting riba were consumption loans for the needy to support their livelihood, and were not loans for trade and investment, and that taking riba on them is what is meant by the riba of Jahiliyyah that Islam prohibited. Some contemporary thinkers had insisted on this idea and sought in it a way out to say that loans for investment and productive financing are immune from prohibition, and they concluded from that to legalize banking interest. A number of researchers examined this idea and found it historically incorrect; rather, they provided many convincing evidences and indications leaving no room for doubt that investment loans were known and widespread in Arab society at the time of the mission (1), and they were the ones financing the winter and summer trade journeys of Quraysh. 17/21 - Regarding the fourth solution, which is nationalizing banks for the state account, the research of Muslim economists has shown that nationalization in itself is not expected to prevent the injustice resulting from riba or prevent the concentration of capital. That is because banks are in fact an intermediary that works with the funds of savers and presents them to investors, and in this regard private banks and state-owned banks are equal, with no difference that the return of the intermediary goes in the latter to the state treasury. So if the financing relationship in which intermediation occurs between savers and investors is a usurious relationship, then the reality of riba and its harms are not eliminated even if the intermediary is the state. The same is said regarding the concentration of capital; economic research indicates that usurious financing tends spontaneously to flow towards the rich. (1) See a comprehensive presentation and a sufficient summary on this topic in the research of Dr. Rafiq al-Masri: Riba al-Qard and the Evidence for Its Prohibition, Jeddah, Center for Research in Islamic Economics, King Abdulaziz University, 1410 AH (2nd edition). 297
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents a contemporary legal analysis of the riba problem by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa. It falls under legal philosophy as it discusses the methodology of deriving rulings in modern contexts. The author first reaffirms that banking interest is definitively prohibited riba, citing scholarly consensus. He then evaluates five solutions previously proposed by researchers: (1) the rule of necessity, which theoretically allows riba under dire necessity but is practically inconceivable for taking riba; (2) establishing an Islamic economic system, which is now possible with modern research; (3) the historical claim that pre-Islamic loans were only for consumption, which is refuted by evidence that investment loans existed; (4) nationalization of banks, which does not eliminate the usurious nature of the relationship; and (5) the fifth solution is mentioned in the text but not elaborated in these pages (the author ends with an evaluation of the fourth). The commentary emphasizes that a mufti must critically assess proposed solutions against definitive texts and empirical evidence, avoiding superficial fixes.

Key Terms
القروض الاستهلاكية (al-qurūḍ al-istihlākiyya): Loans taken for personal consumption needs.
القروض الاستثمارية (al-qurūḍ al-istithmāriyya): Loans used for investment or productive purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What historical evidence does the author cite to refute the claim that investment loans did not exist in pre-Islamic Arabia?
A) The Quran mentions trade caravans.
B) The winter and summer trade journeys of Quraysh were financed by loans.
C) Hadiths about trade partnerships.
D) The author does not provide specific evidence.

Key Takeaway
Historical evidence confirms that investment loans existed in pre-Islamic Arabia and were included in the riba prohibition, so modern bank interest on any loan is prohibited.

Evaluating the Nationalization of Banks as a Solution to Riba
Critical analysis of nationalization as a solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the claim that nationalizing banks can eliminate the harms of riba, using the author's critique.

Core Idea
Nationalizing banks does not eliminate riba; it only changes who receives the interest, not the prohibited nature of the transaction.

A fourth solution proposed nationalizing banks to place them under state control. Dr. al-Zarqa critiques this by arguing that banks are mere intermediaries; the underlying interest-based relationship between savers and investors remains unchanged, whether the intermediary is private or state-owned.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proposal
Proponents of nationalization argue that state-owned banks would not exploit people; the state takes the interest instead of private owners.
2
Identify the author's analytical lens
Al-Zarqa analyzes the function of a bank: it is an intermediary that channels funds from savers to investors, charging and paying interest.
3
Apply the critique
Changing the ownership does not change the nature of the transaction. The usurious relationship between saver and investor persists. The state merely replaces private shareholders as the beneficiary of the interest margin.
4
Consider the concentration of wealth
The author adds that research shows riba-based finance tends to flow to the rich, concentrating capital, regardless of bank ownership.

Source Text

Refuting the Historical Claim About Investment Loans
Refutes modern claim that investment loans were not riba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the historical argument that investment loans were not riba and identify its implications for contemporary fatwa on banking interest.

Core Idea
Pre-Islamic riba financed trade, not just consumption — modern attempts to exempt investment loans from riba are historically unfounded.

Some modern thinkers argue that Quranic riba only prohibited consumption loans, leaving investment loans permissible. Mustafa al-Zarqa, a leading Hanafi jurist, refutes this by citing historical evidence that pre-Islamic riba was precisely used for commercial financing. This lesson examines the historical claim and its implications for iftaa on modern banking.

Guided Path
1
Understand the modern claim
Identify the assertion that investment loans fall outside the prohibition of riba because they are for productive purposes.
This claim is often used to justify banking interest.
2
Examine the historical evidence
Read the passage's argument that pre-Islamic riba (riba al-jahiliyyah) financed the winter and summer trade journeys of Quraysh.
This evidence directly contradicts the modern claim.
3
Analyze the flaw in the historical claim
The flaw is that modern thinkers assumed riba was only for consumption, ignoring historical records showing its widespread commercial use.
The author cites 'convincing proofs and indications' from researchers.
4
Derive the fatwa implication
Conclude that all interest-bearing loans, regardless of purpose, fall under the prohibition of riba unless transformed into a valid Islamic contract.
This is the established Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملحق ثاني بالفصل (۲۱) مشكلة الفائدة الربوية
ولم تكن قروضاً لأغراض التجارة والاستثمار وأن أخذ الربا عليها هو المقصود بربا الجاهلية الذي حرمه الإسلام.
وكان بعض المفكرين العصريين قد أكدوا على هذه الفكرة والتمسوا فيها مخرجاً للقول بأنَّ القروض للتمويل الاستثماري والإنتاجي تكون بمنأى عن التحريم وانتهوا من ذلك إلى تحليل الفوائد المصرفية .
وقد مخص هذه الفكرة عدد من الباحثين فلم يجدوها صحيحة تاريخياً، بل أوردوا أدلة وقرائن كثيرة مقنعة لا تترك مجالاً للشك بأن القروض الاستثمارية كانت معروفة ومنتشرة في المجتمع العربي وقت البعثة (۱) وهي التي كانت تموّل رحلتي الشتاء والصيف التجاريتين لقريش.
Second appendix to chapter (21): The problem of usurious interest. They were not loans for trade and investment purposes, and taking interest on them is what is meant by the riba of Jahiliyyah that Islam prohibited. Some modern thinkers had emphasized this idea and sought in it an exit to say that loans for investment and productive financing are free from prohibition, and they concluded from that to permit banking interest. This idea was examined by a number of researchers and they did not find it historically correct; rather they brought many convincing proofs and indications leaving no room for doubt that investment loans were known and widespread in Arab society at the time of the mission, and they were what financed the winter and summer trade journeys of Quraysh.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
Passage type: Historical and legal refutation of a modern attempt to differentiate between consumption loans (riba) and investment loans (permissible profit). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, a leading Hanafi jurist, rejects the claim that the Quranic prohibition of riba only covers consumption loans. He cites historical research showing that pre-Islamic riba (riba al-jahiliyyah) was precisely used for commercial and investment purposes, such as financing the Quraysh trade caravans. Therefore, the argument that modern bank interest is not riba because it is for investment is historically unfounded. This is a critical point for muftis addressing contemporary financial transactions.

Key Terms
ربا الجاهلية (riba al-jahiliyyah): Pre-Islamic practice of lending with a conditional increase upon default or maturity, typically for trade financing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Government spending

Key Takeaway

Nationalization Does Not Remove Riba: Substance Over Form in Islamic Finance
Argues that nationalization alone fails to remove riba's harms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the legal effect depends on the substance (haqiqa) of the contract, not the identity of the intermediary.

Core Idea
Riba is defined by the contract's terms, not the intermediary's ownership.

The idea that nationalizing banks could solve the riba problem seems plausible to some. But Hanafi jurist Mustafa al-Zarqa shows that the actual prohibited element—the interest-based relationship between saver and investor—remains unchanged. This lesson examines his rational argument and its implications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed solution
The passage critiques the idea that nationalizing banks for the state's account would eliminate the harms of riba. First, identify what the 'fourth solution' is.
The solution is ta'mim al-masarif (nationalization of banks).
2
Extract the core objection
The author argues that banks are intermediaries; changing ownership does not alter the underlying financing relationship between savers and investors. If that relationship is ribawi, the prohibition remains.
Focus on the phrase 'حقيقة الربا ومحاذيره لا ينتفيان'.
3
Understand the economic consequence
Riba-based finance tends to concentrate wealth among the rich. Nationalization does not change this tendency because the contract type (riba) remains.
The passage mentions 'تمركز رؤوس الأموال' (concentration of capital).
4
Conclude: Islamic rulings are substance-based
The Hanafi approach evaluates contracts by their true nature (haqiqa), not by superficial changes. Nationalization is a change in form, not substance.
This aligns with the qawida: 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ والمباني'.

۱۷/۲۱ - وفي شأن الحل الرابع وهو تأميم المصارف لحساب الدولة، قد بينت بحوث الاقتصاديين المسلمين أنه لا ينتظر من التأميم في ذاته، أن يحول دون الظلم الناجم عن الربا أو دون تمركز رؤوس الأموال. ذلك أنَّ المصارف هي في الحقيقة وسيط يعمل في أموال المدخرين فيقدمها للمستثمرين فتستوي في ذلك المصارف الخاصة والمملوكة للدولة، بلا فرق أن عائد الوساطة يذهب في الأخيرة إلى خزانة الدولة. فإذا كانت العلاقة التمويلية التي تتم فيها الوساطة بين المدخرين والمستثمرين هي علاقة ربوية، فإنَّ حقيقة الربا ومحاذيره لا ينتفيان ولو كان الوسيط هو الدولة.
ويقال مثل ذلك عن تمركز رؤوس الأموال، فالبحوث الاقتصادية تشير إلى أن التمويل الربوي يميل بصورة عفوية إلى الانسياب نحو الأغنياء في المجتمع، فيزيدهم قوة مالية وغنى ويحرم الفقراء من التمويل فتزداد بين الأغنياء والفقراء الهوة اتساعاً. وهذا لا يزول بمجرد تأميم الدولة للمصارف.
17/21 - Regarding the fourth solution, which is the nationalization of banks for the state's account, research by Muslim economists has shown that nationalization in itself is not expected to prevent the injustice resulting from riba or the concentration of capital. That is because banks are in fact an intermediary working with the savings of depositors, providing them to investors, and in this respect private and state-owned banks are equal, with no difference except that the return of intermediation goes in the latter case to the state treasury. If the financing relationship in which the intermediation occurs between savers and investors is a riba-based relationship, then the reality of riba and its harms are not eliminated even if the intermediary is the state. The same is said about the concentration of capital; economic research indicates that riba-based finance tends spontaneously to flow towards the rich in society, increasing their financial power and wealth, and depriving the poor of financing, thus widening the gap between rich and poor. This does not disappear simply by the state's nationalization of banks.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
Passage type: Critical analysis of a proposed solution to the riba problem. The author, a Hanafi jurist, argues that nationalizing banks does not change the underlying riba contract between depositor and borrower. The intermediary's ownership (private or state) is irrelevant; the harm of riba—unjust enrichment and wealth concentration—remains. This demonstrates that Islamic legal rulings are based on the substance of the transaction (haqiqa) not form. For a mufti, this warns against superficial solutions that change labels but not the prohibited element.

Key Terms
تأميم (ta'mim): Transfer of private banks to state ownership.
ربا (riba): Usury or interest; prohibited in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does nationalization not solve the riba problem?
A) State-owned banks are more efficient
B) The essential riba relationship between saver and investor remains
C) Private banks are less harmful
D) Nationalization increases wealth concentration

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of a contract depends on its substance, not the identity of the intermediary.

Renewing Civil Law from Islamic Jurisprudence: Al-Zarqa's Call for Broad Ijtihad
Arguing for a new civil code based on broad Islamic fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state al-Zarqa's argument for broadening the basis of civil codification beyond the Hanafi school and explain the practical benefits he claims.

Core Idea
Islamic fiqh from all schools can surpass modern codes; we must draw from it entirely.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa presents a passionate argument for renewing the Ottoman Majalla by incorporating the full spectrum of Islamic juridical learning. He contrasts the narrow Hanafi base of the Majalla with the richness of the entire Islamic fiqh tradition, which he believes can produce a superior civil code. This lesson examines his reasoning and its implications for contemporary legal reform and Iftaa.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Context
Identify that the Ottoman Majalla is based exclusively on Hanafi fiqh. Al-Zarqa calls for a renewal that goes beyond this single school.
The Majalla was a landmark codification but limited to one madhhab.
2
Grasp the Argument for Breadth
Al-Zarqa argues that using all ijtihadat (from all schools) provides principles and rulings that accommodate all legislative needs, present and future. This yields a code superior to any existing one.
He uses the phrase 'fiqh Islami bi ma'nahu al-wasi' (Islamic jurisprudence in its broad sense).
3
Note the Critique of Foreign Codification
He criticizes the Syrian coup government for adopting the Egyptian Civil Code (which was based on French law), cutting ties with Islamic heritage. He warns of instability and confusion from foreign sources unfamiliar to local judges.
This is a key argument: foreign codes cause practical problems in application.
4
Recognize the Pragmatic Benefit
Deriving from Islamic sources prevents floundering in limitless problems because judges can refer to known Islamic schools for cases not covered by explicit texts.
Islamic sources are 'close to us and easily accessible'.
5
See the Example: Article 64
Al-Zarqa gives an example: Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Civil Procedure validated contractual conditions that were previously considered invalid under the Hanafi view adopted by the Majalla. He notes that this principle of respecting conditions is already present in Islamic ijtihadat.
This shows that broader fiqh can resolve issues that the narrow Hanafi approach could not.

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical argument from Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. He calls for a renewal of the Ottoman Majalla (the Hanafi-based civil code) by drawing on the entire spectrum of Islamic jurisprudence, not limited to the Hanafi school. He argues that such a comprehensive Islamic civil code would surpass modern codes in meeting legislative needs. Finally, he begins to illustrate with an example from Ottoman procedure law (Article 64) which validated contractual conditions that were previously considered invalid under the Majalla's fiqh, showing that Islamic fiqh had already addressed such principles.

اجتهادات إسلامية (ijtihadat Islamiyyah): Legal interpretations and rulings derived by scholars from various schools.

Deriving Modern Law from Islamic Jurisprudence: The Case of Contractual Conditions
Legal philosophy exposition on deriving modern codes from Islamic jurisprudence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' provides an Islamic basis for validating contractual conditions in modern codification, and apply this derivation method to similar cases.

Core Idea
The hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' validates contractual conditions, aligning with modern legal theory.

In this lesson, we examine how modern codification can draw from Islamic jurisprudence. The author uses the example of Article 64 of the Ottoman Code, which validates contractual conditions—a rule some thought was a modern European innovation. However, this rule is deeply rooted in the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' and ancient Arab custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: foreign legislation causing confusion
Recognize the author's argument that deriving law from Islamic sources prevents the disorder that arises from using foreign legislation unfamiliar to judges and society.
2
Examine the example: Article 64 on contractual conditions
Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Civil Procedure introduced the rule that contractual conditions are valid and do not invalidate the contract, eliminating the earlier view that some conditions were invalidating.
This Article was considered a modern advancement by its drafters.
3
Trace the Islamic basis: hadith and custom
The author identifies the hadith 'المسلمون عند شروطهم' (Muslims are bound by their conditions) and the pre-Islamic proverb 'الشرط أملك' as the foundational proofs. These show that the rule is deeply Islamic, not a foreign import.
The hadith is widely accepted across madhhabs.
4
Connect to modern codification
Note that the same principle is now enshrined in Article 148 of the Syrian Civil Code: 'العقد شريعة المتعاقدين' (the contract is the law of the contracting parties). This demonstrates how classical Islamic jurisprudence anticipated modern legal theories.
5
Understand the derivation process
Reflect on how the author uses this example to advocate for deriving modern laws from Islamic ijtihad schools rather than foreign sources, showing that Islamic jurisprudence is capable of meeting contemporary legislative needs.
The derivation (istimdad) is a conscious, scholarly process.

Chapter Four: Derivation of modern codification from Islamic jurisprudence, along with adding new temporal legal situations and extracting them on its foundations and principles. This derivation - besides reviving a noble heritage of ours - prevents confusion in limitless problems of application disorder if the law is derived from foreign legislation. Events not explicitly covered by texts, which occur frequently, must then have their rulings derived from well-known Islamic sources in the ijtihad schools from which derivation occurs, instead of resorting to foreign sources unknown in our environment and unintelligible to our judges' understandings and the methods they have adopted. Egypt, which formerly derived its civil law from French legislation for political reasons, today - since scientific maturity has awakened it - a cry from the lawyers themselves calls for laying down a new civil law derived from Islamic jurisprudence. 22/2 - So that the extent of Islamic ijtihad's ability to meet modern legislative needs becomes clear to us, we bring examples of the most important things that laws in our Syrian country have brought, and we see what in Islamic ijtihad corresponds to them: 1 - Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Civil Procedure came with a rule that the drafters of this law considered a new advance by which they approached modern European legislation. It is the rule of considering contractual conditions in contracts and agreements and eliminating invalidity due to conditions that were considered invalidating of the contract according to the legal view adopted by the Majalla. What Article 64 mentioned from this aspect agrees with the school of Ibn Shubruma, which the Majalla's committee itself referred to in its explanatory memorandum as above, and with other schools that see respect for contractual conditions and validity of contracts with them, and in this they follow the saying of the Messenger of God, peace be upon him: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions.' And it agrees with the ancient Arab custom before Islam, and the famous proverb: 'The condition is more deserving, whether against you or for you.' This is exactly the modern legal theory that a person's contract is his law. Article 148 of our new civil law established it, saying: 'The contract is the law of the contracting parties.' We will see its details in its place in the discussion of contract invalidity (section 57/3 and V/Ov).
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage from the fourth chapter of al-Zarqa's work constitutes a legal philosophy discussion on the derivation of modern codification from Islamic jurisprudence. The author argues that drawing from Islamic legal heritage prevents the chaos that arises when importing foreign legislation. He presents the first example: Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Civil Procedure, which validates contractual conditions. The author notes that this rule is not a modern European innovation; it agrees with the school of Ibn Shubrumah (a Hanbali jurist) and other schools, and is supported by the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions.' Furthermore, this aligns with pre-Islamic Arab custom and the famous proverb. This is precisely the modern legal principle 'the contract is the law of the parties,' now codified in Article 148 of the Syrian Civil Code. The author provides a comparative perspective, showing that Islamic jurisprudence already contained this principle centuries before modern European legislation.

Key Terms
استمداد (istimdad): The process of deriving or drawing legal rules from Islamic jurisprudence for modern codification.
تقنين (taqnin): Codification; the act of compiling legal rules into a formal code.
شروط عقدية (shurut 'aqdiyya): Contractual conditions; stipulations made by parties within a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A Qur'anic verse on fulfilling contracts
B) The hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions'
C) The practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs
D) A legal maxim from the Hanafi school

Key Takeaway
Islamic jurisprudence, through the hadith 'Muslims are bound by their conditions', provides a strong foundation for the modern principle that contracts are the law of the parties, enabling the derivation of contemporary legal codes from classical sources.

Respect for Contractual Conditions: Ibn Shubruma's View and Modern Law
Comparing Hanafi reluctance to enforce conditions with Ibn Shubruma's view favoring them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on contractual conditions, explain Ibn Shubruma's opposing view, and evaluate the modern legislative adoption.

Core Idea
The contract is the law of the parties — an ancient principle rooted in hadith, Arab custom, and modern codes.

This passage explores a key tension in Islamic contract law: how strictly should contractual conditions be enforced? The Hanafi school traditionally restricted conditions that deviated from default contract rules. However, the view of Ibn Shubruma, supported by the hadith 'المسلمون عند شروطهم' and pre-Islamic Arab custom, favored broader enforceability. This lesson examines the evidence, the dispute, and how modern codification (e.g., Article 148 of the Civil Code) adopted the wider principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
What exactly is being contested between the Hanafi school and Ibn Shubruma? The enforceability of contractual conditions that alter default rules. The Hanafi school restricts such conditions; Ibn Shubruma holds they are valid.
2
Examine the proof for Ibn Shubruma's view
What is the primary dalil? The hadith 'المسلمون عند شروطهم' and the pre-Islamic Arab custom as expressed in the proverb 'الشرط أملك عليك أم لك'.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? Under the Hanafi view, many contracts with conditions become fasid or give an option; under Ibn Shubruma's view, they are valid unless the condition is haram.
4
Trace the adoption in modern law
How did this view enter modern legislation? The Majallah committee referenced Ibn Shubruma's view, and Article 148 of the Civil Code states 'العقد شريعة المتعاقدين'.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Is there a Hanafi counter-argument? The Hanafi school relies on other principles (e.g., 'الصلح جائز') and a narrower reading of the hadith. The passage does not elaborate, but the tarjih shifted in modern times.

Usurper's Liability for Benefits: Hanafi View and Modern Reform
Comparing Hanafi rule that usurper does not guarantee benefits with modern law requiring compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on usurper's liability for benefits and apply the concept of daman al-manafi' in modern fatwa contexts.

Core Idea
The usurper does not guarantee benefits in Hanafi fiqh, but modern law adopts the Shafi'i view for public interest.

This passage from al-Zarqa's work examines a key difference between classical Hanafi fiqh and modern codification. The Hanafi school exempts a usurper from paying for the use of stolen property, while the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools impose liability. Understanding this dispute helps you see how muftis navigate between established madhhab positions and contemporary maslaha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
Read the passage and note the Hanafi rule: the usurper is not liable for the benefits of the usurped property, only for the value if it perishes.
The passage states this is according to the Majallah and Hanafi ijtihad.
2
Understand the Hanafi rationale
The Hanafi argument is that the guarantee of the principal includes the benefits; therefore, no separate compensation for use is required.
This is based on the idea that benefits are inherent to the property.
3
Contrast with Shafi'i/Hanbali view
The opposing schools hold that benefits are separate from the principal and must be compensated if enjoyed without right.
This leads to liability for ujr al-mithl (rental equivalent).
4
Analyze the modern reform
The author notes that modern Syrian law amended the Hanafi rule, imposing guarantee of the wage of the like, aligning with Shafi'i/Hanbali. The reform is justified by maslaha (public interest).
This shows that muftis and lawmakers may depart from the dominant madhhab view for societal benefit.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, the usurper returns the property with no payment for use; under Shafi'i/Hanbali, he must pay rent for the period of use.
This difference has significant implications in modern legal contexts.
