And the laws in our country have amended this ruling, so they imposed the guarantee of the wage of the like of this usurped property on the one who usurps it and then causes it to be idle or derives benefit from it without a contract, and in this amendment is benefit and correctness, as we will explain in the second part of this book. And this agrees with the Shafi'i and Hanbali ijtihads.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi position regarding a usurper's liability for the usufruct of stolen property. According to Hanafi fiqh, a usurper (ghasib) is only liable for the value of the item if it is destroyed, but not for the benefits he enjoyed from it (e.g., using a car without permission). The logic is that the guarantee of the principal covers the benefits implicitly. However, modern Syrian law amended this rule, making the usurper liable for the rental equivalent (ujr al-mithl) for the period of use, aligning with the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools. The author approves this reform. This illustrates how modern codification departs from the dominant Hanafi view when it serves public interest (maslaha).

Key Terms
غاصب (ghasib): A person who wrongfully takes possession of another's property
ضمان منافع (daman manafi'): Liability for the benefits or usufruct of property
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): The fair market rental value of a property

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not hold the usurper liable for benefits, but modern law aligns with the Shafi'i/Hanbali view for practical justice.

Dissolution of Lease upon Death: Hanafi vs. Modern Law
Comparing Hanafi rule that lease terminates with death of a party with modern non-dissolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on dissolution of lease upon death, explain the underlying reasoning, and apply the ruling to a fatwa scenario involving the death of a contracting party.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, a lease dissolves automatically upon death because the benefit is not inheritable.

The death of a party in a lease contract raises a critical question: does the contract continue or end? The Hanafi school has a clear ruling: the lease dissolves automatically. This lesson examines that ruling, contrasts it with the Shafi'i and modern legal approaches, and explores the practical implications for a mufti advising heirs or executors.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the lease (ijarah) is dissolved (munfasikh) upon the death of either the lessor or lessee.
The source explicitly says 'أوجب انفساخ الإجارة بموت أحد العاقدين'.
2
Contrast with the opposing view
The Shafi'i school and modern rent laws hold that the lease continues and binds the heirs.
The author notes modern laws follow the Shafi'i ijtihad.
3
Analyze the underlying reasoning
Hanafi scholars base the dissolution on the nature of manfa'ah (benefit) as something that is created anew and cannot be inherited.
This is a key difference in legal theory about the object of the contract.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi practice, heirs must renegotiate a new lease; in Shafi'i/modern law, the lease continues.
This affects the rights and obligations of the heirs.
5
Apply to a fatwa scenario
Consider a case where a lessor dies during the lease term. A mufti should explain the Hanafi ruling and advise on the interaction with civil law.
This step bridges classical fiqh and contemporary practice.

وهذا موافق للاجتهاد الشافعي .

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of death on a lease contract (ijarah). In Hanafi fiqh, the lease contract is considered a contract that is dissolved (munfasikh) upon the death of either the lessor or lessee, because the contract is considered personal and not inherited. However, modern rent laws in many Arab countries (and the Shafi'i school) hold that the lease continues after death and binds the heirs. The Ottoman Majallah did not explicitly rule on this, but modern legislation adopted the Shafi'i view for stability.

Key Terms

In Hanafi fiqh, a lease contract dissolves automatically upon the death of either party, a ruling rooted in the non-inheritable nature of usufruct (manfa'ah).

Possession in Mortgage: Hanafi vs. Maliki and Modern Law
Comparing Hanafi requirement of possession in mortgage with Maliki and modern non-requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi requirement of possession (qabd) in a mortgage, compare it with the Maliki and modern legal approach, and apply the relevant legal maxims in fatwa.

Core Idea
Possession is necessary for a binding mortgage in Hanafi; modern registration and Maliki view reject this requirement.

This passage examines the classical Hanafi position that a mortgage requires physical possession for it to become binding. It contrasts this with the Maliki school, which does not require possession for validity or bindingness, and shows how modern real estate laws have replaced possession with registration. Understanding this ikhtilāf is crucial for a mufti dealing with mortgages in contemporary contexts, as it affects the mortgagee’s liability and the contract’s enforceability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
State that in Hanafi fiqh, possession (qabd) is required for the mortgage contract to be binding (lāzim) and for the mortgagee to be liable for destruction.
The hadīth ‘ar-rahn maqbūḍ’ is the primary evidence.
2
Identify the Maliki position
Maliki school does not require possession; the contract is valid and binding without it, as registration or other means suffice.
Maliki view is based on the purpose of security, not physical control.
3
Understand the modern adaptation
Modern real estate laws substitute physical possession with registration in the land registry, which serves the same function of notice and security.
This is a practical necessity in modern legal systems.
4
Synthesize the fatwa hierarchy
Determine the operative position for fatwa today: the mortgage is valid without possession if registered, and the mortgagee is not liable for loss without possession.
The mufta bihi integrates Maliki validity and Shafi‘i non-liability.

This passage discusses the requirement of possession (qabd) for a mortgage (rahn). In Hanafi fiqh, possession is a condition for the mortgage to be binding (lazim) and also for the mortgagee's liability; if the property is not possessed, the mortgagee is not liable for its destruction. The Maliki school does not require possession for validity. Modern real estate laws have replaced physical possession with registration in the land registry, which achieves the same purpose. Additionally, the mortgagee is not liable for damage without possession, which agrees with Shafi'i view that the mortgagee is not a guarantor of the property.

Neighbor's Pre-emption: Hanafi, Shafi'i, and Modern Codification
Comparing Hanafi recognition of neighbor's pre-emption with Shafi'i rejection and modern abolition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate how modern codification selects between madhhab positions using the abolition of neighbor's pre-emption as a case study.

Core Idea
Hanafi neighbor pre-emption was abolished in Syria following Shafi'i opinion—a shift from one madhhab to another via legislation.

This passage examines a concrete example of how modern legal codes navigate between classical madhhab differences. The Syrian law abolished the Hanafi position on neighbor pre-emption and adopted the Shafi'i view, while also modifying procedural time limits per Maliki opinions. Understanding such trans-madhhab borrowing is essential for the contemporary mufti who advises on civil law matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original Hanafi rule
Recall that the Hanafi school recognizes shuf'at al-jiwar (neighbor pre-emption) based on hadith and custom.
This is the rule that the Majallah had adopted.
2
Recognize the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'i school restricts pre-emption to co-owners, rejecting adjacency as a basis.
Their reasoning is that pre-emption only applies where mixing properties causes harm.
3
See how modern law chose
Syrian law abolished neighbor pre-emption, aligning with the Shafi'i view.
The passage explicitly says the abolition 'agrees with the Shafi'i ijtihad'.
4
Analyze additional modifications
The time limits for claiming pre-emption were also adjusted according to Maliki opinions.
This shows selective borrowing from multiple schools.
5
Reflect on implications for fatwa
Consider how a mufti in such a jurisdiction should advise: acknowledging both the fiqh rule and the current law.
The mufti must balance classical rulings with state legislation.

Commentary
This passage discusses the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah). The Hanafi madhhab recognizes pre-emption for neighbors (shuf'at al-jiwar), meaning a neighbor has the right to purchase adjoining property when it is sold. The Majallah had adopted this view. However, modern Syrian law abolished this right, following the Shafi'i school which only grants pre-emption to co-owners (shuf'at al-shirkah). Additionally, the time periods for claiming pre-emption were amended according to Maliki views. The footnote notes that Syrian law went even further by completely abolishing all pre-emption rights, whereas Egyptian law retained it. This shows the flexibility of modern codification in selecting from different schools.

Key Takeaway

Ta'zir: Islamic Discretionary Punishment vs. Modern Fixed Penalties
Comparing Islamic ta'zir with modern fixed penalties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of ta'zir and distinguish it from fixed penalties in modern codes, and identify the implications for fatwa-giving (iftaa).

Core Idea
Ta'zir grants the judge discretion; modern law predetermines penalties — a shift from judicial ijtihad to legislative rigidity.

This passage compares the Islamic fiqhi principle of ta'zir with modern penal codes. It highlights that while both systems avoid the fixed divine punishments of hudud, they differ fundamentally in who sets the penalty: the judge (in ta'zir) versus the legislature (in modern codes). Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis when assessing the compatibility of modern laws with Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two systems compared
The passage juxtaposes Islamic ta'zir (discretionary punishment for non-hudud crimes) with modern penal codes that predefine penalties.
The phrase 'في معظم أحكامه' indicates the comparison is general, not absolute.
2
Note the common ground
Both systems agree that punishments for crimes outside hudud and qisas are not divinely fixed. Ta'zir is the Islamic framework for these crimes.
3
Identify the key difference
In Islamic ta'zir, the judge determines the punishment based on circumstances and the offender's status. In modern codes, the law stipulates the penalty in advance, leaving little or no judicial discretion.
The phrase 'فالقانون قد حدد لكل جريمة عقوبتها سلفاً' explicitly states the predetermination.
4
Consider the legal operation
Ta'zir is an act of ijtihad by the judge; modern fixed penalties reflect legislative ijtihad (collective determination of appropriate punishment).
This shift has implications for the mufti: fixed penalties can be seen as a form of ta'zir if enacted by a legitimate authority.
5
Reflect on the fatwa implication
When a mufti encounters a modern penal code, he must recognize that fixed penalties are not inherently un-Islamic. They represent a different model of ta'zir, provided they stay within shari'ah limits (e.g., not exceeding the gravity of the crime).
The mufti should advise judges to apply fixed penalties unless they cause clear injustice, in which case they may seek modification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ - قانون الجزاء في معظم أحكامه موافق للمبدأ الفقهي في تفويض عقوبة ما سوى الحدود إلى رأي الحاكم وهي المسماة بعقوبة «التعزير» فالقانون قد حدد لكل جريمة عقوبتها سلفاً.
6. The penal code, in most of its provisions, agrees with the fiqhi principle of delegating the punishment for other than hudud to the opinion of the judge, which is called the punishment of 'ta'zir', except that the law has predetermined the punishment for each crime in advance.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage discusses the principle of ta'zir (discretionary punishment) in Islamic law. Ta'zir refers to punishments for crimes that are not hadd (fixed) or qisas, and the judge has discretion in determining the type and severity. Modern penal codes, while aligning with the concept of ta'zir in that they do not prescribe fixed scriptural penalties, differ in that they fix the punishment for each crime beforehand, leaving less discretion to the judge. The author notes that modern law agrees 'in most of its provisions' with the fiqhi principle, but with the significant difference of predetermined penalties. This reflects the evolution from judicial discretion to legislative determination for consistency and legal certainty.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for crimes other than hudud
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed punishments prescribed by divine law (e.g., for theft, adultery)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between the Islamic ta'zir system and modern penal codes as described?
A) Ta'zir is for hudud crimes
B) Modern penal codes give judges more discretion
C) Ta'zir leaves punishment to judge's discretion; modern law fixes it in advance
D) There is no difference

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir's discretionary nature is fundamental, but modern fixed penalties represent a legislative determination of ta'zir, which is not inherently contradictory to Hanafi fiqh.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

How Modern Codification Draws from Islamic Fiqh: A Hanafi Usul Perspective
Describes how modern laws align with Islamic fiqh and the fruits of codification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the process by which modern legal codes derive provisions from Islamic fiqh and identify the specific madhhab positions adopted in key areas such as lease, mortgage, pre-emption, and penal law.

Core Idea
Modern codification selects from diverse madhhab rulings, demonstrating fiqh's adaptability and the relevance of ijtihad.

This lesson explores how contemporary legal codification (taqnin) has drawn upon the rich heritage of Islamic jurisprudence. Mustafa al-Zarqa provides concrete examples where modern laws align with the ijtihad of different madhhabs, revealing that Islamic fiqh is not a monolithic relic but a dynamic source capable of informing modern legislation. The culmination of this effort is the Jordanian civil code of 1976, the first modern code entirely based on Islamic fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples
Scan the passage for the four specific areas where modern codes adopted rulings from specific madhhabs: lease dissolution, mortgage possession, pre-emption, and penal discretion.
2
Link each example to its madhhab source
For each, note which madhhab's ijtihad was followed and why the choice was made (e.g., Shafi'i for lease non-dissolution, Maliki for mortgage without possession).
3
Understand the significance
Consider how these choices resolve practical issues and reflect the flexibility of fiqh.
4
Examine the Jordanian code
Note how it synthesized from multiple schools and replaced the Mejelle, using explanatory memoranda to link each article to its fiqh source.
5
Reflect on the methodology
Recognize that the process involves tarjih (preference) among madhhabs, akin to the work of a mufti selecting the operative opinion.

Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Jurisprudence. 3 - Hanafi ijtihad required the dissolution of lease upon death of either contractor, and the Mejelle is silent on this, and lease laws ruled non-dissolution upon death. This agrees with Shafi'i ijtihad. 4 - Real estate laws dispensed with requiring possession in mortgaged real estate, satisfied by placing a mortgage note on its page in the land registry. This agrees with Maliki ijtihad in not requiring possession for validity or bindingness of the mortgage contract. As previously mentioned (F 8/16). Then consequently, mortgaged real estate without possession is not guaranteed by the mortgagee in case of loss by law, and this also agrees with Shafi'i ijtihad in non-guarantee of the mortgaged property. 5 - Our real estate law number 1/3339 abolished the pre-emption of neighbors that the Mejelle approved, and this abolition agrees with Shafi'i ijtihad which does not recognize pre-emption for a neighbor but restricts it to the partner. Also that real estate law amended the periods set by the Mejelle for claiming pre-emption, so if the pre-emptor fails to claim within them, his pre-emption lapses. And those new periods agree with transmitted views in the Maliki school. 6 - The penal law in most of its rulings agrees with the juristic principle of delegating punishment other than hudud to the judge's opinion, called 'ta'zir' punishment. The law prescribes punishment for each crime in advance. (1) Note: This law was later integrated into the new civil code and became part of it, and the mentioned civil code completely abolished the right of pre-emption in Syria!! while its original, the Egyptian law, retained it for its benefit. Chapter 22: Civil Laws and the Virtue of Their Construction from Jurisprudence. And this determination is within the authority of rulers by Sharia. These are few examples among many; and following up on this subject is endless; and they inform of the reality we want to know, which is that Islamic ijtihads as a whole constitute a wise legal principle or a reasonable theory. 3/22 - Some Fruits of Codification from Jurisprudence. A team of senior jurists and Sharia scholars arose in Egypt since the new Egyptian civil code was drafted in the 1940s, which was of foreign origin. They called for its derivation from Islamic jurisprudence with its various schools, and they formulated the contract theory contained therein entirely as a new formulation incorporating the same legal provisions derived from Islamic fiqh schools, with each article referenced to the fiqh source from which it was derived, thus proving the possibility of creating the most modern contemporary laws from Islamic fiqh. And these scientific efforts and their likes in other countries began, accompanied by strong popular demands, and began to bear fruit in some Arab countries, where the first modern civil code in its style, arrangement, and classification was born, derived from Islamic fiqh with its various schools, in the Hashemite Kingdom of Jordan by a committee of fiqh and law experts, with explanatory memorandum accompanying its provisions article by article, linking them to their sources from the fiqh of the schools, or supporting them by analogical ijtihad or derivation based on the principle of maslaha and mursalah interests. That law was officially issued in 1976 CE, and replaced the Mejelle which had been in force in Jordan until that date. The issuance of this law broke the barrier of difficulties that had hindered the realization of this idea since the middle of this century. The diversity of juristic schools and the flexibility of Sharia texts were the greatest help in selecting appropriate rulings suitable for the needs of the age. The United Arab Emirates proceeded to adopt it completely and issue it.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of how modern codification (taqnin) has drawn from Islamic jurisprudence, illustrating the practical application of fiqh in contemporary legislation. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents several examples where modern laws align with the ijtihad of different madhhabs, showing that Islamic legal thought provides flexible and sound principles. The passage then highlights the successful crystallization of this idea in the Jordanian civil code of 1976, which was entirely based on Islamic jurisprudence and replaced the Ottoman Mejelle. This development is presented as a breakthrough, demonstrating that Islamic fiqh is not only relevant but also capable of producing modern, comprehensive legal codes. The commentary emphasizes the methodological realism: earlier attempts in Egypt showed the possibility, and Jordan actualized it. The author praises the flexibility of fiqh and the role of ikhtilaf (disagreement) in enabling selection of suitable rulings.

Key Terms
تقنين (taqnin): The process of codifying law into a formal legal code; deriving legal provisions from authoritative sources and arranging them systematically.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning by a qualified jurist to derive rulings from primary sources.
شفعة (shuf'a): Right of pre-emption; the right to purchase a property before it is sold to a third party, typically for co-owners and neighbors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Islamic fiqh, through its diverse schools, provides a sufficient and adaptable source for modern codification, as demonstrated by the Jordanian civil code and various national laws.

International Recognition of Islamic Law's Vitality
Historical document affirming Islamic law's vitality by international jurists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three-point resolution of the 1938 Hague Congress of Comparative Law and explain its significance for the Hanafi tradition's claim to modernity and universality.

Core Idea
In 1938, international jurists declared Islamic law alive, evolving, and independent—a historic validation of fiqh's adaptability.

This passage presents a legal-philosophical milestone: the 1938 Hague Congress of Comparative Law, which invited Al-Azhar and, after hearing presentations on Islamic criminal and civil responsibility, formally recognized Islamic Sharia as a source of general legislation. This recognition counters the orientalist claim that Islamic law is static or derivative. For the Hanafi tradition, which prides itself on flexibility through istihsan and 'urf, this resolution affirms that fiqh can meet modern legal needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the event and its legal significance
Locate the date, venue, and participants of the congress. Note that it is a comparative law conference focusing on legal sources.
The congress was in The Hague, 1938, a major center of international law.
2
Extract the three-point resolution
Read the Arabic text and translation to list the three resolutions exactly as stated: (1) Islamic Sharia as a source of general legislation, (2) it is alive and capable of development, (3) it is an independent legal system not derived from others.
These are direct quotes, not paraphrases.
3
Analyze the implications for the Hanafi school
Consider how these points align with Hanafi usul: the school's use of istihsan and 'urf demonstrates 'alive and capable of development'; its rejection of Roman law influence supports 'self-standing'.
Hanafi jurists have long argued that Islamic law is original and not borrowed.
4
Connect to the mufti's role today
A mufti can cite this international recognition to argue for the relevance of Islamic law in modern legislation, e.g., in drafting contracts or penal codes.
This is an external validation, not a legal proof (dalil), but it supports istidlal.
5
Reflect on the ongoing recognition
Note the mention of a subsequent International Lawyers' Conference resolution, indicating continued engagement.
The author uses this to show that recognition is not an isolated event.

Appendix to Chapter Twenty-Two: Some Resolutions of International Law Conferences and Testimony of Pillars of Law on the Merits of Islamic Jurisprudence. 6/22 - Indeed, the deeply rooted today in the science of law from the men of Europe have come to recognize that Islamic legislation is among the foremost suitable sources to meet the needs of modern legislation. This is declared by the resolution of the Congress of Comparative Law held in The Hague in Jumada al-Akhira 1356 AH = August 1938 AD, when Al-Azhar was invited to it, and it was represented by two delegates from senior scholars presenting on criminal responsibility and civil responsibility in Islam and on denying any alleged relationship between Roman law and Islamic Sharia. This congress consequently recorded its historic decision important for European lawmakers. It stated: 1. Considering Islamic Sharia as a source of general legislation. 2. Commentary
This appendix is a legal-philosophical note presenting evidence of international recognition of Islamic law. The author cites the 1938 Hague Congress of Comparative Law, which invited Al-Azhar. Two senior scholars presented on criminal and civil responsibility in Islam, and refuted any link between Roman law and Islamic Sharia. The congress then passed a historic three-point resolution: recognizing Islamic Sharia as a source of general legislation, affirming it is alive and capable of development, and declaring it an independent legal system not borrowed from others. This shows that even Western jurists acknowledged the vitality and originality of Islamic law. The author also mentions a later resolution by the International Lawyers' Conference in The Hague, confirming ongoing recognition.

Key Terms
التشريع الإسلامي (al-tashri' al-islami): Islamic law/legislation, encompassing all branches of fiqh.
القانون المقارن (al-qanun al-muqaran): Comparative law, a field that studies different legal systems.

The 1938 Hague Congress of Comparative Law recognized Islamic Sharia as a living, evolving, and independent legal source—an external validation of the Hanafi school's claim that fiqh can address modern legal challenges.

International Recognition of Islamic Jurisprudence
Reports of international conferences affirming Islamic law's merits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the key decisions of international legal conferences regarding Islamic jurisprudence and explain their significance for the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Islamic law is internationally recognized as a living, independent, and flexible legal system.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's work presents decisions from two major international legal conferences—the 1938 Congress of Comparative Law and the 1948 International Bar Association—that explicitly affirm the value and adaptability of Islamic law. It also highlights praise from the renowned jurist Abd al-Razzaq al-Sanhuri. As a mufti or student of Hanafi fiqh, understanding these external acknowledgments helps you articulate the relevance of Islamic law in modern legal discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three pieces of evidence cited
Locate the 1938 Congress decision, the 1948 International Bar Association decision, and the quotation from Abd al-Razzaq al-Sanhuri. Note the year and organization for each.
These are external validations from non-Muslim legal bodies.
2
Extract the precise wording of the 1938 decision
The 1938 Congress declared that Islamic Sharia is (1) a source of general legislation, (2) living and capable of evolution, and (3) independent, not borrowed from others.
This three-part statement directly rebuts claims of stagnation or derivative origin.
3
Extract the 1948 decision and its emphasis on flexibility
The 1948 International Bar Association resolved, based on a proposal from the Comparative Legislation Committee, that because of the 'flexibility and importance of muslim law,' its comparative study should be adopted and encouraged.
The term 'flexibility' (murunah) is key; in Hanafi usul it connects to istihsan and 'urf.
4
Analyze how flexibility operates in Hanafi fiqh
Reflect on Hanafi tools like istihsan (juristic preference) and 'urf (custom) that allow the school to adapt rulings without abandoning foundational principles. This is the internal mechanism behind 'flexibility'.
This step bridges external recognition with internal methodological strengths.
5
Synthesize the significance for a mufti today
A mufti can cite these conference decisions to demonstrate that Islamic law is not only historically valid but globally respected as a living legal system. This supports arguments for the application of Islamic law in contemporary contexts.
This step prepares you to use this passage in real fatwa work.

/٢٢ من شهر آب عام / ١٩٤٨/م وقد اشتركت فيه ثلاث وخمسون دولة مختلف الأمم والأقطار، وأرسلت كل دولة من الأساتذة والمحامين لديها من يمثلها فيه، وقد كان ممثل سورية فيه زميلنا الأستاذ في كلية الحقوق السورية الدكتور مأمون الكزبري الذي حاضر في هذا المؤتمر عن الملكية العقارية في الشرع الإسلامي فأثبت في محاضرته أنَّ النظرية الإسلامية في الملكية هي أعدل النظريات القديمة والحديثة، إذ اعتبرت الملكية حقاً نسبياً ليس ذا سلطان مطلق مثلما عليه بعض التشريعات. الوضعية القانونية كالتشريع الروماني القديم والإنكليزي الحالي، وليس مغلولاً كما تريده بعض التشريعات الاجتماعية الحديثة اليوم.
وإن هذا المؤتمر الأخير، بناء على اقتراح من لجنة التشريع المقارن فيه، وعطفاً على ما كان قرَّره مؤتمر القانون المقارن السابق سنة / ١٩٣٧/م في مدينة لاهاي نفسها بشأن التشريع الإسلامي - مما ذكرناه آنفاً ـ قد اتخذ القرار التالي الذي ترجمته: نظراً لما في التشريع الإسلامي من مرونة، وماله من شأن هام يجـ على جمعية المحامين الدولية أن تتبنى الدراسة المقارنة لهذا التشريع، وتشجع عليها (١).
۸/۲۲ - وهنا يحسن أن نشيد بالكلمة المأثورة عن الأستاذ العلامة الحقوقي الكبير عبد الرزاق السنهوري من مقال منشور في مجلة القضاء العراقية في العدد الأول من السنة الثانية مايس / ١٩٣٦) ومنقول في العددين / ٦ - ٧/ للسنة الأولى من مجلة نقابة المحامين بدمشق، إذ قال في صدر بحثه عن صلاح الشريعة الإسلامية للخلود في ميدان التطبيق المدني :
(۱) إن نص اقتراح لجنة التشريع المقارن في هذا المؤتمر باللغة الإنكليزية التي صيغ بها، كما وافانا به زميلنا الدكتور مأمون الكزبري عضو المؤتمر المذكور وأقرته جمعيته العامة هو كما يلي:
Because of the recognized flexibility and importance of muslim law the comparative study by the international bar association must be adopted and encouraged.
۳۰۸
First appendix to Chapter Twenty-Two: Some decisions of international legal conferences and testimony of legal authorities regarding the merits of Islamic jurisprudence. 6/22 - Indeed, today's erudite in the science of law from European figures have come to recognize that Islamic legislation is among the foremost valid sources to meet the needs of modern legislation. This is expressed by the decision of the Congress of Comparative Law held in The Hague in Jumada al-Akhira 1356 AH = August 1938 AD, when Al-Azhar was invited to it, and it was represented by two delegates from senior scholars who presented there on criminal responsibility and civil responsibility in Islam and on the denial of any alleged relationship between Roman law and Islamic Sharia. This congress recorded thereafter its historic decision important for European legislators. It included: 1- Considering Islamic Sharia as a source of general legislation. 2- That it is living and capable of evolution. 3- That it is a law standing on its own, not taken from others. (1) 7/22 - When our book was printed in its third edition, news reached us of the decision of the International Bar Association congress held in The Hague in Europe between 15 August 1948 AD, in which fifty-three countries of different nations and regions participated, and each country sent professors and lawyers to represent it. Syria's representative was our colleague Professor Dr. Ma'mun al-Kuzbari from the Syrian Faculty of Law, who lectured at this congress on real property ownership in Islamic law and proved in his lecture that the Islamic theory of ownership is the most just of ancient and modern theories, as it considered ownership a relative right without absolute authority as in some positive legal legislations like ancient Roman and current English legislation, and not shackled as desired by some modern social legislations today. And this last congress, based on a proposal from its Comparative Legislation Committee, and following what the previous Congress of Comparative Law had decided in 1937 in the same city of The Hague regarding Islamic legislation - which we mentioned earlier - adopted the following decision, the translation of which is: 'Considering the flexibility of Islamic legislation and its important standing, the International Bar Association must adopt and encourage the comparative study of this legislation.' (1) 8/22 - Here it is appropriate to praise the well-known statement of the great legal scholar Abd al-Razzaq al-Sanhuri from an article published in the Iraqi Judiciary Magazine in the first issue of the second year (May 1936) and quoted in issues 6-7 of the first year of the Damascus Bar Association Magazine, as he said in the opening of his research on the suitability of Islamic Sharia for perpetuity in the field of civil application: (1) The text of the proposal of the Comparative Legislation Committee in this congress in English, as formulated, as our colleague Dr. Ma'mun al-Kuzbari, member of the aforementioned congress, informed us and its general assembly approved, is as follows: Because of the recognized flexibility and importance of muslim law the comparative study by the international bar association must be adopted and encouraged. 308
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a supplementary chapter presenting decisions from international legal conferences that acknowledge the merits of Islamic fiqh. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, cites three key events: the 1938 Congress of Comparative Law in The Hague, the 1948 International Bar Association congress in The Hague, and an article by Abd al-Razzaq al-Sanhuri. The 1938 congress declared Islamic Sharia as a source of general legislation, alive and capable of evolution, and independent. The 1948 congress, based on a proposal from its Comparative Legislation Committee, resolved that because of the flexibility and importance of Islamic law, the International Bar Association should adopt and encourage its comparative study. Al-Sanhuri's article is quoted to further emphasize the suitability of Islamic law for modern application. The passage serves as evidence that Islamic jurisprudence is not a relic but a dynamic and recognized legal system.

Key Terms
التشريع الإسلامي (al-tashriʿ al-islami): Islamic legislation; refers to the entire body of Islamic law derived from the Quran, Sunnah, and jurisprudence.
القانون المقارن (al-qanun al-muqaran): Comparative law; the study of differences and similarities between legal systems.
مرونة (murunah): Flexibility; the quality of Islamic law that allows it to adapt to changing circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did the International Congress of Comparative Law (1938) affirm about Islamic Sharia?
A) It is derived from Roman law.
B) It is a living and independent source of legislation.
C) It is only applicable to Muslims.
D) It is outdated and needs reform.

Key Takeaway
Islamic jurisprudence has been recognized by international legal bodies as a flexible, independent, and living legal system, reinforcing its relevance for contemporary fatwa and legislation.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Islamic Law's Flexibility: International Recognition
Demonstrating international recognition of Islamic law's adaptability and potential for modern legal theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Islamic law's inherent flexibility has been recognized by international legal bodies and used to argue for its relevance in modern codification.

Core Idea
Islamic law's flexibility is not a modern claim but an internationally recognized fact, forming the basis for comparative legal study.

This passage from al-Zarqa’s work on the modern codification of fiqh presents historical evidence that Islamic law was acknowledged by Western jurists and international conferences as a dynamic system capable of contributing to contemporary legal theory. The author cites specific conferences and scholars to demonstrate that the seeds of modern legal doctrines like abuse of right and force majeure are already present in Islamic jurisprudence, waiting only to be formulated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the evidence
Locate the specific conferences and resolutions mentioned in the passage. These serve as objective recognition of Islamic law's flexibility.
Key events: 1937 and 1948 conferences in The Hague.
2
Analyze the claim of flexibility
Understand what 'flexibility' means in this context: the ability of Islamic law to adapt to changing circumstances and to provide principles that align with modern legal theories.
The passage contrasts Islamic law's relative ownership with absolute Roman or English concepts.
3
Examine Sanhuri's argument
Al-Sanhuri identifies four modern legal theories and claims their foundations exist in Islamic law, needing only proper formulation.
List the four theories: abuse of right, exceptional circumstances, assumption of risk, strict liability.
4
Connect to codification
Why is this historical recognition important? It supports the project of deriving legal codes from Islamic jurisprudence, as al-Zarqa advocates throughout his book.
The final quote from a French jurist emphasizes the duty of Syrian legislation to use Islamic law.

۳۰۹
Chapter Four: The Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh
From 22 of August 1948, fifty-three states of different nations and regions participated, and each state sent professors and lawyers to represent it. Syria's representative was our colleague, Professor at the Syrian Faculty of Law, Dr. Ma'mun al-Kuzbari, who lectured at this conference on real estate ownership in Islamic law. He demonstrated in his lecture that the Islamic theory of ownership is the most just of ancient and modern theories, as it considered ownership a relative right, not an absolute authority as in some positive legal legislations like ancient Roman law and current English law, nor shackled as desired by some modern social legislations today.
And this recent conference, based on a proposal from its Comparative Legislation Committee, and following up on the previous Comparative Law Conference in 1937 in the same city of The Hague regarding Islamic legislation – which we mentioned earlier – adopted the following resolution, the translation of which is: 'In view of the flexibility of Islamic legislation and its important significance, the International Bar Association should adopt and encourage the comparative study of this legislation.' (1)
22/8 - Here it is appropriate to acclaim the famous words of the great legal scholar Abd al-Razzaq al-Sanhuri from an article published in the Iraqi Judiciary Magazine in the first issue of the second year (May 1936) and quoted in issues 6-7 of the first year of the Damascus Bar Association Magazine. He said at the beginning of his research on the suitability of Islamic law for eternity in the field of civil application:
(1) The text of the proposal of the Comparative Legislation Committee at this conference in the English language in which it was drafted, as conveyed to us by our colleague Dr. Ma'mun al-Kuzbari, member of the said conference, and approved by its general assembly, is as follows:
'Because of the recognized flexibility and importance of Muslim law the comparative study by the international bar association must be adopted and encouraged.'
308

First Appendix to Section (22): Resolutions of International Conferences on Fiqh
I do not wish to limit myself to the testimony of fair-minded jurists from Western scholars, such as the German jurist Kohler, the Italian professor Del Vecchio, the American dean Wigmore, and many others who testify to the flexibility and capacity for development inherent in Islamic law, and place it alongside Roman law and English law as one of the three basic legal systems that have dominated and continue to dominate the world. Professor Lambert, the renowned French jurist, pointed out in the International Congress of Comparative Law held in The Hague in 1932 to this great appreciation for Islamic law that has begun to prevail among European and American jurists in the present age.
But I refer to the law itself to prove the validity of what I have stated:
In this law there are elements that, if handled by the hand of drafting and well formulated, could produce theories and principles no less in sophistication, comprehensiveness, and alignment with evolution than the most significant legal theories we receive today from modern Western jurisprudence.
I present four examples, which I am forced to limit due to lack of space: Any observer of Western fiqh knows that among its most recent theories in the twentieth century are the theory of abuse of right, the theory of 'exceptional circumstances', the theory of 'assumption of risk', and 'strict liability'. Each of these four theories has a foundation in Islamic law that requires only formulation and construction.' Then Mr. al-Sanhuri elaborated at length on the foundations of these four modern theories in Islamic law.
22/9 - On this occasion, we quote here a great and beautiful statement about the duty of Syrian lands in their awaited new legislation by the great French professor Donnedieu de Vabres, a prominent figure in criminal law scholars in the present age and professor at the Faculty of Law of the University of Paris, mentioned in his introduction...
309
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a historical and testimonial segment from al-Zarqa's work, demonstrating the international recognition of Islamic law's flexibility and relevance for modern codification. The author begins by recounting the 1948 International Bar Association conference where Dr. Ma'mun al-Kuzbari presented on Islamic property law, showing that Islamic ownership theory is balanced—neither absolute nor excessively restricted. The conference resolved to encourage comparative study of Muslim law due to its flexibility and importance. Next, al-Zarqa quotes Abd al-Razzaq al-Sanhuri, a leading Arab jurist, who argues that Islamic law contains the seeds of modern legal theories like abuse of right, force majeure, risk assumption, and strict liability. Sanhuri insists these theories need only proper formulation to match or surpass Western jurisprudence. Finally, the author references Donnedieu de Vabres on Syria's duty in legislation. This passage is not a legal ruling but a meta-juristic argument for the vitality of Islamic law in contemporary legal systems. It serves as an introduction to the idea that Islamic fiqh can be a source for modern codification, a key theme of the book. No specific rasm rule or qawida is presented; rather, it is a philosophical and historical justification for engaging with Islamic legal heritage.

Key Terms
التقنين المعاصر (al-taqnīn al-muʿāṣir): Modern codification of laws in contemporary nation-states
الملكية العقارية (al-milkiyya al-ʿaqāriyya): Real estate ownership, specifically the legal theory of property in Islamic law
التعسف في استعمال الحق (al-taʿassuf fī istiʿmāl al-ḥaqq): Abuse of right, a modern legal theory prohibiting exercising a right solely to harm others

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the International Bar Association resolve regarding Muslim law in 1948?
A) To adopt Muslim law as the primary legal system for all member states.
B) To encourage the comparative study of Muslim law due to its flexibility and importance.
C) To reject Muslim law as outdated and incompatible with modern legal systems.
D) To translate the Quran into all languages for legal purposes.

Key Takeaway
Islamic law's flexibility has been recognized internationally, and its principles can be formulated into modern legal theories, making it relevant for contemporary codification.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Western Jurists' Acknowledgment of Islamic Law's Flexibility: A Case Study in Legal Reform Argumentation
Appendix presenting Western praise for Islamic law's modernity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct an argument using Western juristic testimony to support the claim that Hanafi fiqh contains foundations for modern legal theories.

Core Idea
Western jurists rank Islamic law with Roman and English law; its principles can be codified into modern theories.

This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm presents evidence from Western scholars that Islamic law is flexible and capable of development. It argues that four modern legal theories—abuse of right, unforeseen circumstances, assumption of risk, and strict liability—have roots in Sharia. The author quotes a French professor urging Syria to integrate its Islamic heritage into new legislation. This lesson examines how such testimony can be used in legal reform arguments within the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Western jurists cited
Recognize that the author lists specific scholars (Kohler, Delvechie, Wigmore, Lambert) who praised Islamic law's flexibility and placed it alongside Roman and English law.
This establishes external credibility for Islamic law's modernity.
2
Note the four modern theories
Understand that the passage claims the theories of abuse of right, unforeseen circumstances, assumption of risk, and strict liability have foundations in Sharia that need only proper formulation.
These theories are well-known in Western jurisprudence but the author asserts their Islamic roots.
3
Analyze the quote from Donnerieu de Vares
Extract his argument that Syria must not neglect its Islamic legislative heritage when modernizing after war, even while adopting foreign models.
This directly applies the general claim to a specific national context.
4
Connect to Hanafi fiqh
Recognize that the author implies these four theories can be derived from Hanafi usul and furu, such as the maxim 'La darar wa la dirar' for abuse of right, and rules on hardship for unforeseen circumstances.
This bridges the Western testimony to concrete Hanafi principles.
5
Evaluate the argumentative strategy
Assess the strength of using Western testimony to legitimize Islamic law in contemporary legal reform. Consider potential weaknesses: is it merely an appeal to authority?
This develops critical thinking about the rhetoric of legal reform.

٢٢/ ٩ - وبهذه المناسبة ننقل هنا عبارة جليلة جميلة عن واجب البلاد السورية في تشريعها الجديد المنتظر للأستاذ الفرنسي الكبير (دونديو دوفابر Donnerieu de Vares من أعلام علماء الحقوق الجنائية في العصر الحاضر، والأستاذ في كلية الحقوق من جامعة باريز ذكرها في مقدمته التي كتبها على رسالة زميلنا الدكتور أنور إبراهيم باشا التي عنوانها «المسؤولية الجزائية في الشريعة الإسلامية بنظر الاجتهاد الحنفي.
«De la responsabilité pénale en Droit Islamique d'aprés la Doctrine Hanafite»
وهي الرسالة التي نال بها شهادة الدكتوراه في الحقوق من الجامعة المذكورة، سنة / ١٩٤٤/ خلال الحرب العالمية الثانية الماضية، إذ يقول الأستاذ دوفابر ما ترجمته:
عندما تضع الحرب أوزارها في يوم السلم القريب ستجد سورية نفسها أمام ضرورة ملجئة إلى أن تستبدل بالتشريعات المتنوعة السائدة في بلادها، وخاصة منها المجموعة القانونية العثمانية لسنة / ١٨٥٨/م، تشريعاً موحداً ملائماً للعصر .
يجب وسوف تلجأ بلا ريب كما فعلت سابقاً، إلى نماذج أجنبية. ولكنها أن لا تهمل ماضيها التشريعي المجيد المنشيء للحضارة الرفيعة، والمكون من تراثها الإسلامي.
وإن مؤلف هذه الرسالة يساهم في إظهار صورة هذا الماضي بارزة» (۱).
هذا ولا أجد ختاماً أختم به هذه المقدمة أجمل وأمثل القيم المأثورة إذ يقول :
(۱) النص الفرنسي لعبارة الأستاذ دوفابر» هذه هو كما يلي: 
au Jour prochain de la paix, la Syrie se verra dans l'obligation urgente de substituer aux législa- tions variées qui régnent sur son territoire, et, notemment au Code ottoman de 1858, un droit unifié et modernisé.
Comme précédemment, elle aura recours, sand doute, à des exemples étrangers.

L'auteur de ce livre aura, heureusement, contribué à mettre ce précédent en relief.
First Appendix to Chapter (22): Resolutions of International Conferences on Fiqh
I do not wish to limit myself to the testimony of impartial jurists from Western scholars, such as the German jurist Kohler, the Italian professor Delvechie, the American dean Wigmore, and many others who testify to the flexibility and capacity for development inherent in Islamic Sharia; and they place it alongside Roman law and English law as one of the three basic legal systems that have dominated and still dominate the world. Professor Lambert, the renowned French jurist, pointed out in the International Congress of Comparative Law held in The Hague in 1932 to this great appreciation for Islamic Sharia that has begun to prevail among European and American jurists in the present age.
But I return to Sharia itself to prove the correctness of what I have stated:
In this Sharia there are elements that, if taken by the hand of drafting and well formulated, could produce theories and principles no less in sophistication, comprehensiveness, and adaptability to development than the most important legal theories we receive today from modern Western jurisprudence.
I will give four examples that I was forced to limit due to lack of space: every informed person in Western jurisprudence knows that among its most recent theories in the twentieth century are the theory of abuse of right, the theory of 'unforeseen circumstances,' the theory of assumption of risk, and 'liability without fault.' Each of these four theories has a foundation in Islamic Sharia that needs only formulation and construction. Then Professor Sanhouri elaborated at length the foundations of these four modern theories in Islamic Sharia.
22/9 - On this occasion, we quote here a magnificent, beautiful statement about the duty of the Syrian lands in their awaited new legislation by the great French professor (Donnerieu de Vares), one of the leading criminal law scholars of the present age and professor at the Faculty of Law of the University of Paris, which he mentioned in his introduction to the thesis of our colleague Dr. Anwar Ibrahim Pasha, entitled 'Criminal Responsibility in Islamic Sharia according to Hanafi Jurisprudence.'
'De la responsabilité pénale en Droit Islamique d'aprés la Doctrine Hanafite'
This is the thesis by which he obtained his doctorate in law from the aforementioned university in 1944 during the past World War II. Professor Dovaber said, in translation:
'When the war ceases in the near day of peace, Syria will find itself under an urgent necessity to replace the diverse legislations prevailing in its land, especially the Ottoman legal code of 1858, with a unified legislation suitable for the age.
It will undoubtedly resort, as it did before, to foreign models. But it must not neglect its glorious legislative past, creator of a high civilization, and composed of its Islamic heritage.
The author of this thesis contributes to bringing out the image of this past in a prominent way.'
(1) The French text of Professor Dovaber's statement is as follows:
au Jour prochain de la paix, la Syrie se verra dans l'obligation urgente de substituer aux législations variées qui règnent sur son territoire, et, notemment au Code ottoman de 1858, un droit unifié et modernisé.
Comme précédemment, elle aura recours, sand doute, à des exemples étrangers.
Mais elle ne saurait négliger le précédent illustre, et hautement civilisateur, que constitue sa tradition islamique.
L'auteur de ce livre aura, heureusement, contribué à mettre ce précédent en relief.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is an appendix to Chapter 22 of al-Zarqa's work, presenting evidence from Western scholars about the merits of Islamic law. The author first lists prominent Western jurists who acknowledge the flexibility and evolutionary capacity of Sharia, ranking it alongside Roman and English law. He then asserts that Islamic law contains raw materials that, if properly codified, can yield legal theories as advanced as those of modern Western jurisprudence. He mentions four such theories—abuse of right, unforeseen circumstances (force majeure), assumption of risk, and strict liability—and notes that each has a basis in Sharia. To illustrate practical application, he quotes French criminal law scholar Donnerieu de Vares, who urged Syria to draw on its Islamic legal heritage when modernizing its legislation after World War II. The passage thus serves to legitimize the use of Islamic fiqh in contemporary legal reform and underscores the relevance of classical Hanafi jurisprudence to modern codification.

Key Terms
نظرية التعسف في استعمال الحق (nazariyyat al-ta'assuf fi isti'mal al-haqq): The legal theory that a right may not be exercised in a way that harms others without legitimate interest; abuse of right.
نظرية الظروف الطارئة (nazariyyat al-zuruf al-tari'a): The theory of unforeseen circumstances that excuse non-performance of contractual obligations; akin to force majeure or hardship.
نظرية تحمل التبعة (nazariyyat tahammul al-tabi'a): The theory of assumption of risk, where a person bears the consequences of lawful but hazardous activities.
مسؤولية عدم التمييز (mas'uliyyat 'adam al-tamyiz): Strict liability or liability without fault, where a person is held responsible for harm regardless of intent or negligence.

Siyasa Shar'iyya: Justice as Part of Sharia
Ibn Qayyim's argument that just political policies are part of Sharia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Ibn Qayyim's argument that just political policies are part of Sharia and apply it to evaluate whether a given governance practice is from Sharia.

Core Idea
Just governance is not separate from Sharia; it is an integral part of it.

This appendix presents a profound legal philosophy passage from Ibn Qayyim. He argues that the purpose of Sharia is justice, and any just policy, even if not explicitly mentioned in primary texts, is part of Sharia. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for understanding the scope of Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the teleological foundation
Recognize that Allah sent messengers and books to establish justice (al-qist), which is the foundation of creation.
This sets the purpose of Sharia as justice.
2
Understand Ibn Qayyim's claim
Ibn Qayyim asserts that whenever signs of truth and justice appear by any means, that is from Allah's Sharia and His pleasure.
This expands the scope of Sharia beyond explicit texts.
3
Recognize the problem of narrow interpretations
Some scholars narrowed Sharia, claiming it is insufficient for governance, leading rulers to create laws outside Sharia.
This causes harm and corruption.
4
Grasp the solution
Ibn Qayyim insists that just political policies are part of Sharia, not separate. The term 'politics' is merely conventional.
If it is just, it is from Sharia.
5
Note the terminological point
The use of 'political laws' (qawanin siyasiyya) among earlier jurists shows that statutory enactments were considered within the Sharia framework.
This supports the continuity of Islamic legal development.

First appendix to chapter (22): Resolutions of international conferences on fiqh. Indeed, Allah sent His messengers and revealed His books so that people may uphold justice, which is the justice upon which the heavens and earth are established. So if the signs of truth appear and its evidences are established by any means, then that is from Allah's law and religion, and His pleasure and command (1). By such mature minds and pure understandings, Islamic fiqh rose to its high station and its hidden treasures emerged from the folds of Sharia, and perhaps this fiqh renaissance will soon return. (1) Ibn Qayyim mentioned this at the end of his valuable book 'I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in 'an Rabb al-'Alamin' (3/543), during his discussion of al-siyasa al-shar'iyya and what it requires of creating rulings for people according to needs and benefit, and what those newly introduced political rulings may be beneficial and in accordance with the spirit of Islamic Sharia or harmful and contrary. He, may Allah have mercy on him, brought up very precious words on this occasion about the principle and reasons for innovating what he called 'political laws' – i.e., rulings enacted by the commands of rulers based on considerations of al-siyasa al-shar'iyya – and explained how a group of those who claimed knowledge of Sharia narrowed its scope out of ignorance, blocked for themselves sound methods from the Sharia methods for establishing justice and organizing the interests and rights of people, and made Sharia as if it is insufficient for the interests of people, until rulers saw that people's affairs cannot be set right except by something additional to what these people understood from a narrow understanding of the vast Sharia, so they introduced political laws to organize the interests of the world, and among these laws were sound and corrupt. So from the shortcoming of the former and the innovations of the latter in their political arrangements emerged great evil and widespread corruption, etc. Then he, may Allah have mercy on him, said: We do not say that just political rule contradicts the complete Sharia; rather, it is part of its parts and a chapter of its chapters. Calling it 'politics' is a matter of terminology; otherwise, if it is just, it is from the Sharia. Then he gave examples of the actions of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and his companions, and their judgments among people by political policy (al-siyasa al-shar'iyya), so let it be examined. He repeated most of this research in his book 'al-Turuq al-Hukmiyya' (pp. 12-15). Ibn Qayyim's expression 'political laws' indicates that the application of the term 'laws' to mean temporal legislative systems was in use among the jurists before Ibn Qayyim, who died in 751 AH.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This appendix opens with a profound legal philosophy passage from Ibn Qayyim. The author begins by quoting a principle: Allah sent messengers and books to establish justice (al-qist) – the very justice by which heavens and earth stand. This sets the teleological foundation for Sharia: justice is its essence. Ibn Qayyim then extends this: whenever signs of truth and proofs of justice appear by any means, that is from Allah's law (shar' Allah) and His pleasure. This is a powerful argument for the legitimacy of just political enactments (siyasa shar'iyya). The passage warns against a narrow understanding of Sharia that excludes just governance. Those who claim to be scholars but restrict Sharia cause harm; rulers then create laws outside Sharia due to this perceived deficiency. Ibn Qayyim insists that just politics is not separate from Sharia – it is a part and chapter of it. The term 'politics' is merely conventional; if it is just, it is Sharia. This refutes both those who secularize law and those who rigidly confine Sharia to ritual matters. The author notes that Ibn Qayyim's use of 'political laws' (qawanin siyasiyya) shows that the term 'law' (qanun) was already used by jurists before his time, indicating an established tradition of engaging with statutory enactments.

Key Terms
السياسة الشرعية (al-siyasa al-shar'iyya): Governance based on Sharia principles, including enactments by rulers to establish justice and organize public interests, even if not explicitly detailed in primary texts.
القوانين السياسية (al-qawanin al-siyasiyya): Laws enacted by rulers based on considerations of siyasa shar'iyya, which can be beneficial or harmful depending on their conformity to Sharia spirit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Just governance is an integral part of Sharia; it is not separate or secular.

Two Methods of Codifying Islamic Fiqh: Single-Madhhab vs. Eclectic
Definition and distinction of two codification methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'codification of Islamic fiqh' and 'codification from fiqh' and apply the appropriate method to a given state legislative scenario.

Core Idea
Codification of fiqh confines to one madhhab; codification from fiqh draws from all madhhabs and early authorities.

This appendix introduces the modern concept of codification (taqnin) as applied to Islamic fiqh. The author distinguishes two approaches: one that stays within a single madhhab, and one that eclectically selects from multiple schools and early jurists. Understanding this distinction is essential for any mufti or legal reformer involved in state legislation today.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general definition of codification
Read the first paragraph: codification means collecting, arranging, and drafting legal rules into numbered articles, then enacting them as state law binding on judges. This is the modern legislative technique.
This definition is universal; the specific application to Islamic fiqh follows.
2
Identify the first type: 'codification of Islamic fiqh'
This method takes rulings from a single madhhab only. If multiple opinions exist within that madhhab on one issue, only one is selected. The state's judiciary applies only that madhhab.
Example: A Hanafi-majority country codifying Hanafi fiqh for its courts.
3
Identify the second type: 'codification from fiqh'
This broader method draws from all considered madhhabs (the four schools) and also from the opinions of early authorities who did not leave complete madhhabs (e.g., al-Layth ibn Sa'd, al-Awza'i). It allows eclectic selection.
Note the Arabic term: al-taqnin min al-fiqh, meaning 'codification from fiqh' not 'of fiqh'.
4
Note the missing continuation
The passage breaks off at the point where the author would explain what happens when no ruling is found in any of these sources. Be aware that the full discussion likely includes a hierarchy or fallback mechanism.
Review the next page if available; this is an incomplete chunk.

تقنين الفقه الإسلامي والتقنين منه
٢٢/ ١٠ - مقدمة :
يقصد بالتقنين بوجه عام جمع الأحكام والقواعد التشريعية المتعلقة بمجال من مجالات العلاقات الاجتماعية، وتبويبها وترتيبها، وصياغتها بعبارات آمرة موجزة واضحة في بنود تسمى (مواد) ذات أرقام متسلسلة. ثم إصدارها في صورة قانون أو نظام تفرضه الدولة، ويلتزم القضاة بتطبيقه بين الناس .
ونقصد بتقنين الفقه الإسلامي، تطبيق طريقة التقنين الآنف الذكر على الأحكام الفقهية المأخوذة من مذهب واحد.

Second appendix to chapter 22: Codification of Islamic fiqh and codification from fiqh. 22/10 - Introduction: Codification generally means collecting legal rulings and rules related to a field of social relations, arranging them, organizing them, and drafting them in imperative, concise, clear phrases in clauses called 'articles' with serial numbers. Then issuing them in the form of a law or system imposed by the state, and judges are obligated to apply it among people. And by codification of Islamic fiqh we mean applying the aforementioned method of codification to fiqh rulings taken from a single madhhab. If in a single issue there are multiple opinions within the madhhab, only one is chosen. That is, codification of fiqh aims to codify the rulings of a single madhhab in transactions if the state in some Islamic countries wants its judiciary to operate on only one madhhab. As for codification from fiqh, we mean that the state derives its codifications in various subjects from Islamic fiqh in its general sense, i.e., from all considered madhhabs, and from the opinions of the Companions, Followers, and later mujtahids whose opinions have been transmitted in books of ikhtilaf al-fuqaha' and who did not have complete madhhabs recorded in all chapters of fiqh and its transactions, such as al-Layth ibn Sa'd, al-Awza'i, Ibn Shubrama, and Ibn Abi Layla. Then if in all fiqh regarding the subject to be codified...
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This second appendix defines the concept of codification (taqnin) in the context of Islamic fiqh. The author first gives the general modern meaning: collecting, organizing, and drafting laws into articles, to be enforced by the state. Then he distinguishes two approaches within Islamic fiqh: (1) Codification of Islamic fiqh (taqnin al-fiqh al-Islami): this takes rulings from a single madhhab; if there are multiple opinions within that madhhab on an issue, only one is chosen. This is suitable for countries that want to apply one madhhab exclusively. (2) Codification from fiqh (al-taqnin min al-fiqh): this is a broader approach where the state derives its codes from all recognized madhhabs (madhahib mu'tabara) and also from the opinions of early authorities like companions, followers, and independent mujtahids who did not establish complete madhhabs (e.g., al-Layth ibn Sa'd, al-Awza'i, Ibn Shubrama, Ibn Abi Layla). The text breaks off at the point where the author presumably explains the next step if no ruling is found in any of these sources. This distinction is crucial for understanding modern legislative reform in Muslim countries: the choice between strict adherence to one school versus eclectic selection from multiple schools.

Key Terms
التقنين (al-taqnin): The process of collecting, arranging, and drafting legal rulings into authoritative articles (mawadd) with serial numbers, issued as state law.
تقنين الفقه الإسلامي (taqnin al-fiqh al-Islami): Codification restricted to rulings of a single Islamic madhhab.
التقنين من الفقه (al-taqnin min al-fiqh): Codification drawing from all recognized madhhabs and early authorities not confined to a single school.
الليث بن سعد (al-Layth ibn Sa'd): A prominent early jurist (d. 175/791) from Egypt whose opinions are recorded but did not form a separate madhhab.
الأوزاعي (al-Awza'i): A Syrian jurist (d. 157/774) whose school was followed in Syria but later faded, though his opinions are preserved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between 'codification of Islamic fiqh' and 'codification from fiqh'?
A) Codification of fiqh uses only Hanafi rulings; codification from fiqh uses all schools.
B) Codification of fiqh is limited to one madhhab; codification from fiqh draws from multiple madhhabs and early authorities.
C) Codification of fiqh is for transactions only; codification from fiqh is for all subjects.
D) Codification of fiqh requires new ijtihad; codification from fiqh merely compiles existing rulings.

Key Takeaway
Codification of fiqh can be confined to a single madhhab or can eclectically draw from multiple schools; the choice shapes the legal system's flexibility and inclusiveness.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

8 min
Codification of Islamic Fiqh: Definition and Two Approaches
Defines two approaches to codifying Islamic fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between taqnīn al-fiqh and al-taqnīn min al-fiqh and explain the methodology of selection in codification.

Core Idea
Codification from all schools is broader and more flexible than codifying a single school.

This passage introduces the concept of codification (taqnīn) in Islamic law and presents two distinct approaches: taqnīn al-fiqh, which restricts to one madhhab, and al-taqnīn min al-fiqh, which draws from all recognized sources. Understanding this distinction is crucial for modern legal reform in Muslim societies.

Guided Path
1
Define codification generally
Read the first paragraph and state the general meaning of taqnīn: collecting, classifying, and formulating legal rules into numbered articles issued as binding state law.
2
Identify the two approaches
Find where the author distinguishes between taqnīn al-fiqh and al-taqnīn min al-fiqh. Note the key difference: single madhhab vs. all madhhabs plus independent mujtahids.
The phrases 'تقنين الفقه' and 'التقنين من الفقه' are introduced.
3
Understand the selection methodology
When multiple opinions exist, the chosen opinion is the most suitable based on evidential strength, ease of application, and alignment with maqasid. This selection is a collective ijtihad.
The author emphasizes that this is not arbitrary but requires temporal insight.
4
Analyze the role of collective ijtihad
Note that the selection is rarely entrusted to one person; it is a collective effort of trustworthy experts. This is a key feature of codification as opposed to individual fatwa.

الملحق الثاني بالفصل الثاني والعشرين
تقنين الفقه الإسلامي والتقنين منه
٢٢/ ١٠ - مقدمة :
يقصد بالتقنين بوجه عام جمع الأحكام والقواعد التشريعية المتعلقة بمجال من مجالات العلاقات الاجتماعية، وتبويبها وترتيبها، وصياغتها بعبارات آمرة موجزة واضحة في بنود تسمى (مواد) ذات أرقام متسلسلة. ثم إصدارها في صورة قانون أو نظام تفرضه الدولة، ويلتزم القضاة بتطبيقه بين الناس .
ونقصد بتقنين الفقه الإسلامي، تطبيق طريقة التقنين الآنف الذكر على الأحكام الفقهية المأخوذة من مذهب واحد.
وإذا كان في المسألة الواحدة أقوال متعدّدة ضمن المذهب، يختار واحد منها فقط. أي ان تقنين الفقه يراد به تقنين أحكام المذهب الواحد في المعاملات إذا أرادت الدولة في بعض الأقطار الإسلامية أن يجري قضاؤها على مذهب واحد فقط . أما التقنين من الفقه فنريد به أن تستمد الدولة تقنيناتها في مختلف الموضوعات من الفقه الإسلامي بمفهومه العام، أي من جميع المذاهب المعتبرة، ومن آراء فقهاء الصحابة والتابعين ومن بعدهم من المجتهدين الذين نُقِلَت آراؤهم في كتب اختلاف الفقهاء، ولم تدوّن لهم مذاهب كاملة في جميع أبواب الفقه ومعاملاته كالليث بن سعد والأوزاعي وابن شبرمة وابن أبي ليلى. ثم إذا لم يوجد في جميع الفقه في الموضوع المراد تقنينه رأي سابق لأنَّ الموضوع مستمد من قضايا العصر المستجدة أو كان في الموضوع رأي فقهي سابق، ولكن المصلحة الزمنية (بميزانها الشرعي) تتطلب اجتهاداً جديداً بخلافه، يلجأ عندئذ إلى تخريج الأحكام المراد تقنينها في الموضوع على قواعد الفقه الإسلامي العامة وأصول الفقه، ومقاصد الشريعة، والمصالح المرسلة.
وفي تقنين الفقه أو التقنين منه، عندما تتعدد الآراء والأقوال الفقهية في المسألة الواحدة، يختار منها للتقنين ما هو الأصلح بحسب قوة الدليل الشرعي، ويُسْر التطبيق، والقُرب من مقاصد الشريعة وعدالتها. وهذا الاختيار عمل اجتهادي يتطلب فوق المعرفة الشرعية بصيرة زمنية بأحوال الناس العملية وأنواع المشكلات التي يصادفونها والمخالفات التي يقعون فيها. وغالباً ما يعهد بهذا العمل الاجتهادي إلى مجموعة من الخبراء الثقات، وقلما يترك الرأي لشخص واحد فهو اجتهاد جماعة وليس اجتهاد فرد.
والمحصلة النهائية للتقنين هي أنه يثبت في كل مسألة حكماً فقهياً واحداً واضح النص واجب التطبيق على القاضي والمتقاضي. وينحصر اجتهاد القاضي حينئذ في فهم هذا النص وتطبيقه على وقائع القضايا.
١١/٢٢ - أهمية التقنين :
إن من بديهيات قواعد النظام اليوم في العالم أن المواطن المكلف، أي الفرد في الدولة له الحق كل الحق أن يعرف مسبقاً حكم النظام الذي يسري عليه في الأفعال والتصرفات والعقود وسائر الحوادث التي يمكن أن تقع منه أو عليه لكي يعرف كيف يطيع النظام، ويعرف ما هي تبعاته إذا عصاه؟
ومرد ذلك إلى مبدأ هو من بديهيات أصول الشرائع الإلهية المصدر أو الوضعية على حد سواء، هو مبدأ ( علنية النظام). فكل نظام يراد تطبيقه على المكلفين يجب أن يكون معلناً إليهم بجميع قواعده وأحكامه قبل.
Appendix 2 to Chapter 22: Codification of Islamic Fiqh and Codification From It. 22/10 - Introduction: Codification generally means collecting legislative rulings and rules related to a field of social relations, classifying and arranging them, and formulating them in commanding, concise, clear phrases into items called (articles) with sequential numbers. Then issuing them in the form of a law or system imposed by the state, and judges are obliged to apply it among people. And by codification of Islamic fiqh we mean applying the aforementioned codification method to the fiqh rulings taken from a single madhhab. If there are multiple opinions within the madhhab on a single issue, only one is selected. That is, the codification of fiqh intends the codification of the rulings of a single madhhab in transactions if the state in some Islamic countries wants its judiciary to proceed according to only one madhhab. As for codification from fiqh, we mean that the state derives its codifications in various subjects from Islamic fiqh in its general sense, i.e., from all recognized schools, and from the opinions of the jurists of the Companions, the Followers, and later mujtahids whose opinions have been transmitted in books on the differences of jurists, but who did not compile complete schools in all chapters of fiqh and its transactions, such as al-Layth ibn Sa'd, al-Awza'i, Ibn Shubruma, and Ibn Abi Layla. Then if in all fiqh on the subject to be codified there is no previous opinion because the subject is derived from new contemporary issues, or there is a previous fiqh opinion but temporal interest (by its shari' balance) requires a new ijtihad contrary to it, then one resorts to deriving the rulings intended for codification on the subject based on the general principles of Islamic fiqh, usul al-fiqh, maqasid al-shari'ah, and masalih mursalah. And in codification of fiqh or codification from it, when multiple fiqh opinions and views exist on a single issue, the one chosen for codification is the most suitable according to the strength of the shari' evidence, ease of application, and nearness to the purposes of shari'ah and its justice. This selection is an ijtihad work requiring, beyond shari' knowledge, temporal insight into the practical conditions of people, the types of problems they encounter, and the violations they fall into. Usually, this ijtihad work is entrusted to a group of trustworthy experts, and it is rarely left to one person; so it is collective ijtihad, not individual ijtihad. The final outcome of codification is that it establishes for each issue a single clear fiqh ruling obligatory for application by the judge and the litigant. The judge's ijtihad is then confined to understanding this text and applying it to the facts of cases. 22/11 - The Importance of Codification: It is among the self-evident principles of the system today in the world that the responsible citizen, i.e., the individual in the state, has the full right to know in advance the ruling of the system that applies to his actions, transactions, contracts, and all incidents that may occur by him or against him, so that he knows how to obey the system and knows what consequences he faces if he disobeys it. This is due to a principle that is among the self-evident principles of legal sources, whether divine or positive, which is the principle of (publicity of the law). Every system intended to be applied to the responsible individuals must be announced to them with all its rules and rulings beforehand.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work is a legal philosophy analysis of codification (taqnin) in the context of Islamic law. The author begins by defining codification generally as the process of collecting, classifying, and formulating legal rules into numbered articles issued as binding state law. He then distinguishes two approaches: 'taqnin al-fiqh' (codification of fiqh) — which means codifying the rulings of a single madhhab exclusively, suitable when a state chooses one school for its judiciary; and 'taqnin min al-fiqh' (codification from fiqh) — a more comprehensive method drawing from all recognized schools and even individual mujtahids without complete madhhabs, and when no existing opinion covers a new issue, rulings are derived from general fiqh principles, usul, maqasid, and masalih mursalah. The selection among multiple opinions is guided by evidential strength, ease of application, and alignment with shari'a objectives — a collective ijtihad requiring temporal awareness. The final product fixes a single clear ruling for each issue, limiting the judge's role to applying the text. The importance section argues the necessity of publicity: citizens must know the law beforehand to obey it — a foundational principle shared by divine and positive legal systems.

Key Terms
تقنين (taqnīn): جمع الأحكام والقواعد التشريعية في بنود مرقمة وإصدارها كقانون ملزم
تقنين الفقه (taqnīn al-fiqh): تقنين أحكام مذهب واحد فقط
التقنين من الفقه (al-taqnīn min al-fiqh): استمداد التقنينات من جميع المذاهب المعتبرة وآراء المجتهدين
اجتهاد جماعة (ijtihād jamā‘ah): الاختيار الفقهي للتقنين يعهد به إلى خبراء ثقات وليس لشخص واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
ما الفرق بين 'تقنين الفقه' و'التقنين من الفقه' حسب النص؟
أ) تقنين الفقه يشمل جميع المذاهب، والتقنين من الفقه يقتصر على مذهب واحد.
ب) تقنين الفقه يقتصر على مذهب واحد، والتقنين من الفقه يستند لجميع المذاهب وآراء المجتهدين.
ج) لا فرق بينهما.
د) تقنين الفقه خاص بالعبادات، والتقنين من الفقه خاص بالمعاملات.

Key Takeaway
Islamic codification can be single-madhhab (taqnīn al-fiqh) or multi-madhhab (taqnīn min al-fiqh); the latter is more flexible but requires collective ijtihad and adherence to evidential and maṣlaḥa criteria.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

The Importance of Codification: Publicity and Certainty
Explains the importance of codification through the principle of publicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue for the necessity of codification based on the principle of 'alaniyyat al-niẓām (publicity of the law) and explain its role in limiting judicial ijtihad.

Core Idea
A law must be publicly announced before it can be binding on the people.

This passage argues that codification is essential because it fulfills the fundamental legal principle of publicity: citizens must know the law in advance to obey it. This principle applies in both divine and positive legal systems. Codification also creates legal certainty by providing a single clear ruling for each case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
The author states that it is a self-evident principle that citizens have the right to know the law beforehand.
2
Understand the basis: publicity of the law
This right derives from the principle of 'alaniyyat al-niẓām: any system must be publicly announced before application.
The author notes this is common to both divine and positive laws.
3
Connect to codification
Codification makes the law clear, accessible, and fixed, enabling citizens to know their obligations and consequences.
4
Examine the effect on judicial ijtihad
Codification limits the judge's role to interpreting and applying the codified text, rather than engaging in independent ijtihad on each case.
