Codifying Fiqh: Methodology and Principles
Methodology of codifying fiqh rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi methodology for codifying fiqh rulings, including the triggers for codification, selection criteria among multiple opinions, and the resulting binding effect on judges and litigants.

Core Idea
Codification selects one ruling per issue by collective ijtihad based on strongest evidence and ease of application.

The codification (taqnīn) of Islamic jurisprudence is a modern project that transforms the multi-opinion nature of fiqh into a unified, binding legal code. This passage outlines the Hanafi-inspired methodology: when new circumstances arise or existing opinions need revision, scholars derive rulings using the general rules of fiqh, usul, maqasid, and masalih mursalah. When multiple opinions exist, the selection is not arbitrary but follows specific criteria. The final code limits the judge's ijtihad to interpreting and applying the codified text, not re-evaluating the underlying evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the need for codification
Recognize the trigger: a new contemporary issue (qadiyya mustajidda) or a prior opinion that conflicts with current maslaha in its Shariah balance. The passage states that 'temporal interest' may require a new ijtihad contrary to the old view.
This step requires awareness of changing circumstances and maqasid.
2
Derive rulings from foundational sources
Using the general qawaid fiqhiyya, usul al-fiqh, maqasid al-shariah, and masalih mursalah to produce the ruling intended for codification. This is a process of takhrij (derivation) from the broader framework.
The derivation is not from scratch but from the established Hanafi legal tradition.
3
Select among multiple opinions
When multiple fiqh opinions exist on the same issue, choose the one that combines: 1) strength of Shariah evidence (quwwat al-dalil), 2) ease of application (yusr al-tatbiq), and 3) closeness to maqasid and justice (qurb min maqasid al-shariah wa adalatiha).
This is an ijtihadi (not merely preferential) selection requiring temporal insight.
4
Entrust selection to collective ijtihad
The passage emphasizes that such selection is rarely done by one scholar; it is collective ijtihad (ijtihad jama'i) performed by a group of trustworthy experts (thiqat). This ensures reliability and breadth of consideration.
Contrast with individual ijtihad in fatwa.
5
Apply the final outcome
The codified ruling becomes a single, clear text (nass wadih) that is wajib (obligatory) upon both the judge (qadi) and the litigant (mutqadi). The judge's ijtihad is then confined to understanding that text and applying it to the specific facts of cases.
This final binding effect replaces the typical pluralism of fatwa with a uniform code.

Source Text

الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه
رأي سابق لأنَّ الموضوع مستمد من قضايا العصر المستجدة أو كان في الموضوع رأي فقهي سابق، ولكن المصلحة الزمنية (بميزانها الشرعي) تتطلب اجتهاداً جديداً بخلافه، يلجأ عندئذ إلى تخريج الأحكام المراد تقنينها في الموضوع على قواعد الفقه الإسلامي العامة وأصول الفقه، ومقاصد الشريعة، والمصالح المرسلة
وفي تقنين الفقه أو التقنين منه، عندما تتعدد الآراء والأقوال الفقهية في المسألة الواحدة، يختار منها للتقنين ما هو الأصلح بحسب قوة الدليل الشرعي، ويُسْر التطبيق، والقُرب من مقاصد الشريعة وعدالتها وهذا الاختيار عمل اجتهادي يتطلب فوق المعرفة الشرعية بصيرة زمنية بأحوال الناس العملية وأنواع المشكلات التي يصادفونها والمخالفات التي يقعون فيها. وغالباً ما يعهد بهذا العمل الاجتهادي إلى مجموعة من الخبراء الثقات، وقلما يترك الرأي شخص واحد فهو اجتهاد جماعة وليس اجتهاد فرد.
والمحصلة النهائية للتقنين هي أنه يثبت في كل مسألة حكماً فقهياً واحداً واضح النص واجب التطبيق على القاضي والمتقاضي. وينحصر اجتهاد القاضي حينئذ في فهم هذا النص وتطبيقه على وقائع القضايا.
Chapter Four: Deriving Contemporary Codification from Jurisprudence. There is a prior opinion because the subject is derived from emerging contemporary issues or there was a prior juristic opinion, but temporal interest (by its Shariah balance) requires a new ijtihad contrary to it. At that point, one resorts to deriving the rulings intended for codification on the subject from the general rules of Islamic jurisprudence, principles of fiqh, objectives of Shariah, and unrestricted interests. In codifying fiqh or codifying from it, when multiple juristic opinions and statements appear on a single issue, the one most suitable is chosen for codification according to the strength of the Shariah evidence, ease of application, and proximity to the objectives of Shariah and its justice. This selection is an ijtihadi work requiring, beyond Shariah knowledge, temporal insight into the practical conditions of people, the types of problems they encounter, and the violations they commit. Often this ijtihadi work is entrusted to a group of reliable experts; it is rarely left to one person, so it is collective ijtihad, not individual ijtihad. The final outcome of codification is that it establishes for each issue a single fiqh ruling, clear in text, obligatory for the judge and the litigant to apply. The judge's ijtihad then is confined to understanding this text and applying it to the facts of cases.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage discusses the methodology of codifying Islamic jurisprudence (taqnīn al-fiqh). The author explains that when new issues arise or existing opinions need revision due to changed circumstances (with proper Shariah balance), one derives rulings using general fiqh rules, usul al-fiqh, maqasid al-shariah, and masalih mursalah. When multiple opinions exist, the selection for codification prioritizes: strength of evidence, ease of application, and alignment with maqasid and justice. This selection is a collective ijtihad performed by a group of trustworthy experts, not an individual. The final product is a single, clear, binding ruling for each issue, limiting the judge's ijtihad to interpretation and application of that text.

Key Terms
التقنين (taqnīn): The process of codifying fiqh rulings into a unified legal code with binding authority.
الاجتهاد الجماعي (al-ijtihād al-jamā'ī): Collective ijtihad performed by a group of qualified scholars, as opposed to individual ijtihad.
المصلحة الزمنية (al-maslaḥa al-zamaniyya): Temporal public interest as assessed through the Shariah balance, which can justify a new ijtihad contrary to a previous opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary basis for selecting among multiple fiqh opinions for codification?
A) The opinion of the most senior scholar in the committee.
B) The opinion that is easiest for judges to apply.
C) The opinion that combines strength of evidence, ease of application, and proximity to Shariah objectives and justice.
D) The most recently issued fatwa.

Key Takeaway
Codification of fiqh is an ijtihad-based process that resolves plural opinions into a single binding ruling, guided by evidence strength, practicality, and maqasid.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner

The Principle of Publicity in Law and Its Implications
Explains the necessity of legal publicity and ignorance maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the principle of publicity in Islamic law, identify its role as a condition for valid obligation (taklīf), and apply the maxim that ignorance of the law after proper announcement does not excuse non-compliance.

Core Idea
Public announcement of laws is essential; after it, ignorance is no excuse.

This passage establishes a foundational principle: a legal system must be publicly known before it can be enforced. Without publicity, the obligation to obey is impossible and thus invalid by Sharia and reason. The text then derives the maxim that ignorance of the law after proper announcement is not an excuse, and discusses historical and modern methods of achieving publicity. It also argues for a single highest court to ensure uniform interpretation, reinforcing the predictability of law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Locate the term 'علنية النظام' (ʿalaniyyat al-niẓām) and state its meaning: that laws must be publicly announced before enforcement.
This principle is axiomatic in both divine and positive legal systems.
2
Understand the rationale
Analyze why publicity is necessary: because an obligation (taklīf) requires the obligated person to have knowledge of what is required. Obligating someone to follow a hidden law is 'تكليف بما لا يطاق' (an impossible burden), prohibited by both Sharia and reason.
This links to the Hanafi usul principle that taklīf presupposes knowledge.
3
Derive the legal maxim
From the necessity of publicity, the text deduces the maxim: 'الجهل بالقانون ليس عذراً' (ignorance of the law is no excuse). After proper announcement, failure to know is negligence.
The justification: the person had the means to know (by himself or by asking scholars).
4
Examine historical and modern methods
Note examples of publicity: carving on monuments, clay tablets, criers in ancient times; official gazettes in modern states. Each method fulfills the same function of making law accessible.
The state is responsible for every letter published in the gazette.
5
Connect to judicial unity
Understand the argument for a single court of cassation: to maintain uniform interpretation (ijtihād muwaḥḥad). This ensures the law is predictable, supporting the principle of publicity.
Change of ijtihād is allowed exceptionally, but then it becomes the new uniform interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أهمية التقنين :
إن من بديهيات قواعد النظام اليوم في العالم أن المواطن المكلف، أي الفرد في الدولة له الحق كل الحق أن يعرف مسبقاً حكم النظام الذي يسري عليه في الأفعال والتصرفات والعقود وسائر الحوادث التي يمكن أن تقع منه أو عليه لكي يعرف كيف يطيع النظام، ويعرف ما هي تبعاته إذا عصاه؟
ومرد ذلك إلى مبدأ هو من بديهيات أصول الشرائع الإلهية المصدر أو الوضعية على حد سواء، هو مبدأ ( علنية النظام). فكل نظام يراد تطبيقه على المكلفين يجب أن يكون معلناً إليهم بجميع قواعده وأحكامه قبل سريانهم عليهم ونفاذه فيهم، لأنّ علم المكلف بالنظام الذي تطلب منه طاعته شرط لصحة التكليف وإن تكليفه بطاعة نظام غير معلن، بل هو في ضمير الحاكم، هو تكليف بما لا يطاق، وهو ممتنع شرعاً وعقلاً.
وعلنية النظام هي طريق علم المكلف به فيمكن بعدها الحكم على المكلف بمقتضاه، سواء أعلم به بعد ذلك أو جهله، لأن بالإعلان أصبح قادراً على معرفة أحكامه جملة وتفصيلاً إما بنفسه أو بالسؤال من أهل العلم. ومن ثم قامت في عالم التشريع القاعدة الشهيرة: «أن الجهل بالقانون ليس عذراً للمكلف يمنع تطبيقه عليه، لأنه بعد كونه معلناً للجمهور يعتبر عدم اطلاع بعض المكلفين عليه تقصيراً منهم فلا يبقى جهلهم عذراً لهم. فالمهم أن لا يفاجأ المكلف بمسؤولية بمقتضى نظام لم يعرفه، ولم يكن له سبيل إلى معرفته، لأنه غير معلن.
وقد كانت العلنية في الشرائع قديماً تحقق بالوسائل الممكنة إذ ذاك، كالنقش على النصب (المسلات)، أو على ألواح من الخزف تعرض في الأماكن العامة، أو إرسال منادين ينادون ويعلنون للناس ما يصدر عن الحكام أصحاب السلطة من أوامر ونواه وترتيبات إلزامية.
واليوم تتحقق العلنية بالنشر في جريدة رسمية تصدرها الدولة، وتعتبر مسؤولة عن كل حرف ينشر فيها .
ومن ثم أيضاً كان من المقرر في النظم القضائية، مهما تعددت المحاكم في الدولة الواحدة أن لا تتعدد محكمة التمييز (محكمة النقض والإبرام)، بل يجب أن تكون واحدة ليكون الاجتهاد القضائي الملزم واحداً كوحدة النص أمام المكلف، حتى يستطيع أن يعرف مسبقاً ما سيطبق على أعماله وتصرفاته وهذا لا ينافي إمكان تبدل اجتهاد محكمة التمييز، فإن حالة التبدل تقبل بصورة استثنائية للضرورة، ثم نبقى أمام اجتهاد واحد في تفسير النص الواحد أو القياس عليه أو ما لانصَّ فيه من الحوادث، إذ يحل الرأي
The Importance of Codification: It is among the axioms of today's world system that the obligated citizen, i.e., the individual in the state, has every right to know in advance the ruling of the system that applies to him in actions, transactions, contracts, and all incidents that may occur by him or against him, so that he knows how to obey the system and knows what consequences are if he disobeys it. This is due to a principle that is among the axioms of the foundations of divine or positive legal systems alike: the principle of 'publicity of the system.' Every system intended to be applied to the obligated must be announced to them with all its rules and rulings before its enforcement and implementation among them, because the obligated person's knowledge of the system from which obedience is required is a condition for the validity of the legal obligation. And obligating him to obey a system that is not announced, but is in the conscience of the ruler, is an obligation of what is impossible, and it is prohibited by Sharia and reason. Publicity of the system is the way for the obligated person to know it; thereby he can be judged according to it, whether he knows it afterward or is ignorant of it, because by announcement he becomes able to know its rulings in general and in detail, either by himself or by asking the people of knowledge. Hence the famous rule arose in the legislative world: 'Ignorance of the law is not an excuse for the obligated person that prevents its application against him, because after it is announced to the public, the failure of some obligated persons to become informed is a negligence on their part, so their ignorance does not remain an excuse for them.' The important thing is that the obligated person should not be surprised by a responsibility under a system he did not know and had no way to know, because it was not announced. In ancient laws, publicity was achieved by possible means at that time, such as carving on monuments (obelisks), or on clay tablets displayed in public places, or sending criers who called out and announced to the people what the ruler-possessors of authority issued of commands, prohibitions, and binding arrangements. Today, publicity is achieved by publication in an official gazette issued by the state, which is responsible for every letter published therein. Furthermore, it was also established in judicial systems, no matter how many courts exist in a single state, that the court of cassation (court of cassation and annulment) should not multiply; rather, it must be one so that the binding judicial ijtihad is one, like the unity of the text before the obligated person, so that he can know in advance what will be applied to his actions and transactions. This does not contradict the possibility of change in the ijtihad of the court of cassation; for the state of change is accepted exceptionally out of necessity, then we remain before one ijtihad in interpreting the single text or analogizing from it or regarding incidents that have no text, where opinion takes [the place of analogy].
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage explains the importance of codification from the perspective of the principle of publicity (علنية النظام). The author argues that an obligated person must know the law before it is applied; otherwise the obligation is impossible and invalid both by Sharia and reason. Publicity enables knowledge; after proper announcement, ignorance of the law is not an excuse because the person had the means to know. The passage then gives historical examples of publicity (carving, clay tablets, criers) and modern methods (official gazette). Finally, it argues for a single highest court (court of cassation) to ensure uniform interpretation, which also supports the principle of publicity by making law predictable.

Key Terms
علنية النظام (ʿalaniyyat al-niẓām): The principle that legal rules must be publicly announced before they can be enforced.
الجهل بالقانون (al-jahl bi-l-qānūn): Ignorance of the law; after proper publicity, it does not excuse non-compliance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must a legal system be publicly announced before it can be enforced?
A) To make the law available for scholarly criticism.
B) Because a valid obligation requires the obligated person to have the means to know the law.
C) To allow citizens to vote on the law.
D) Because Islamic courts require written documentation.

Key Takeaway
Public announcement of the law is a prerequisite for valid obligation; after it, ignorance is not an excuse.

The Principle of Publicity of Law and Its Consequences
Explaining the principle of publicity and its implications for codification and judicial practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of publicity of law (علنية النظام) to determine when ignorance of a legal ruling is excused and to justify the requirement of unified judicial interpretation in Islamic legal systems.

Core Idea
A law must be publicly announced to be binding; ignorance after announcement is no excuse, and unified interpretation preserves certainty.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa explores a foundational legal principle: that law must be public to validly obligate. From this, it derives the rule that ignorance of the law is no excuse, and it argues for a single supreme court to ensure consistent interpretation. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and how it applies to modern fatwa and judiciary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
The author states that knowledge of the law is a condition for valid obligation. Why would a secret law be invalid?
Focus on the concept of taklif bima la yutaq.
2
Trace the consequence
After public announcement, ignorance is considered negligence. What is the famous rule derived?
State the qawida in Arabic and English.
3
Examine the historical modes of publicity
How was law publicized in ancient times? How is it done today?
Note the evolution from inscriptions to official gazettes.
4
Connect to judicial organization
Why must there be a single supreme court? How does this relate to the principle?
Emphasize the need for unified interpretation to maintain predictability.
5
Contrast early Islam and settled fiqh
Why were early judges mujtahids? Why is that no longer permissible?
Understand the historical context and the crystallization of fiqh.

سريانه عليهم ونفاذه فيهم، لأنّ علم المكلف بالنظام الذي تطلب منه طاعته شرط لصحة التكليف وإن تكليفه بطاعة نظام غير معلن، بل هو في ضمير الحاكم، هو تكليف بما لا يطاق، وهو ممتنع شرعاً وعقلاً.

وعلنية النظام هي طريق علم المكلف به فيمكن بعدها الحكم على المكلف بمقتضاه، سواء أعلم به بعد ذلك أو جهله، لأن بالإعلان أصبح قادراً على معرفة أحكامه جملة وتفصيلاً إما بنفسه أو بالسؤال من أهل العلم. ومن ثم قامت في عالم التشريع القاعدة الشهيرة: «أن الجهل بالقانون ليس عذراً للمكلف يمنع تطبيقه عليه، لأنه بعد كونه معلناً للجمهور يعتبر عدم اطلاع بعض المكلفين عليه تقصيراً منهم فلا يبقى جهلهم عذراً لهم. فالمهم أن لا يفاجأ المكلف بمسؤولية بمقتضى نظام لم يعرفه، ولم يكن له سبيل إلى معرفته، لأنه غير معلن.

ومن ثم أيضاً كان من المقرر في النظم القضائية، مهما تعددت المحاكم في الدولة الواحدة أن لا تتعدد محكمة التمييز (محكمة النقض والإبرام)، بل يجب أن تكون واحدة ليكون الاجتهاد القضائي الملزم واحداً كوحدة النص أمام المكلف، حتى يستطيع أن يعرف مسبقاً ما سيطبق على أعماله وتصرفاته وهذا لا ينافي إمكان تبدل اجتهاد محكمة التمييز، فإن حالة التبدل تقبل بصورة استثنائية للضرورة، ثم نبقى أمام اجتهاد واحد في تفسير النص الواحد أو القياس عليه أو ما لانصَّ فيه من الحوادث، إذ يحل الرأي الاجتهادي الجديد محل القديم، ولا نكون بصورة دائمة في دوامة أمام آراء اجتهادية متعددة يتخيّر القاضي بينها، فلا يعرف المكلف مصيره فيها.
:
وفي صدر الإسلام كان القضاة مجتهدين، بحكم الضرورة على خلاف الأصل، إذ لم تكن قد تكوّنت وتبلورت آراء ومذاهب اجتهادية في فهم نصوص الشريعة في الكتاب والسنة ليمكن الاختيار من بينها للقضاء، فكان ذلك حالة استثنائية (كتبدل اجتهاد محكمة التمييز اليوم تقبل ولا تستمر بصورة دائمة حرصاً على مبدأ علنية النظام.
وإذا كان القضاة في صدر الإسلام مجتهدين، فقد كان ذلك حالة لابد منها ولا محيص عنها قبل أن تستقر قواعد الفقه وأصوله ومذاهبه. إذ كان الصحابة ومن تلقى عنهم من التابعين وتابعيهم متفرقين في الأمصار، وعند كل منهم علم من سنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وتفسيره للقرآن ما ليس عند الآخر، كما قاله مالك رضي الله عنه للرشيد. أما بعد ذلك وقد استقر الفقه على قواعده ومذاهبه فلا يجوز أن يبقى القاضي حراً يختار للحكم في كل قضية ما يراه هو حقاً وعدلاً، ولو كان هو من العلم والفقه في رتبة الاجتهاد، بل يجب تقييده بنصوص معلنة للملأ لا يخرج عنها. وينحصر اجتهاده عندئذ في فهم النص وتفسيره وفقاً لقواعد الفهم والتفسير في أصول الفقه، كما سلفت الإشارة إليه، حتى إن الرأي الاجتهادي للقاضي في فهم النص أيضاً أن يكون معروفاً مسبقاً للمكلفين ما أمكن إلى ذلك من سبيل. ومن ثم كان اليوم في عالم القانون والنظم القضائية أن رأي محكمة التمييز (محكمة النقض) وحدها في تفسير النص القانوني هو الاجتهاد القضائي الملزم ليكون الاجتهاد القضائي (كالنص القانوني) رأياً تفسيرياً واحداً معروفاً لدى المكلفين .
وأما الظن بأنَّ القاضي يجب شرعاً أن يترك لاجتهاده يقضي بحسبه وأنه لا يجوز تقييده بقول شرعي واحد يلزم بالقضاء به، فهذا ليس له سند
Second appendix to Chapter (22) Codification of Fiqh and Codification from It
 Its applicability and enforcement upon them, because the knowledge of the legally responsible person (mukallaf) of the system from which obedience is demanded is a condition for the validity of the obligation. Indeed, obligating him to obey an unannounced system—rather it is in the conscience of the ruler—is an obligation of what is impossible, and that is prohibited by Shari'ah and reason.
•
The publicity of the system is the way for the mukallaf to know it, so after it he can be judged according to it, whether he actually knows it or is ignorant of it, because by the announcement he becomes capable of knowing its rulings in general and in detail, either by himself or by asking the people of knowledge. Hence, in the world of legislation, the famous rule arose: 'Ignorance of the law is not an excuse for the mukallaf preventing its application upon him, because after it is announced to the public, the lack of knowledge of some mukallaf of it is considered negligence on their part, so their ignorance remains no excuse for them. The important thing is that the mukallaf should not be surprised by a responsibility under a system he did not know, and had no way to know, because it is not announced.'
Publicity in ancient laws was achieved by the means possible then, such as engraving on monuments (obelisks), or on clay tablets displayed in public places, or sending criers to call out and announce to the people what the rulers in authority issued of commands, prohibitions, and binding arrangements.
Today, publicity is achieved by publication in an official gazette issued by the state, which is responsible for every letter published in it.
Hence also it was established in judicial systems, however many courts there are in a single state, that the court of cassation (court of appeal and finality) should not be multiple; rather it must be one, so that binding judicial ijtihad is one, like the unity of the text before the mukallaf, so that he can know in advance what will be applied to his actions and transactions. This does not negate the possibility of the change of ijtihad of the court of cassation, for the case of change is accepted exceptionally out of necessity, then we remain before a single ijtihad in interpreting a single text or analogizing to it or regarding incidents not explicitly mentioned, as the new ijtihadi opinion replaces the old, and we are not permanently in a whirlpool before multiple ijtihadi opinions from which the judge chooses, so the mukallaf does not know his fate in them.
:
In early Islam, judges were mujtahids, due to necessity contrary to the principle, because ijtihadi opinions and schools had not yet been formed and crystallized in understanding the texts of Shari'ah from the Book and Sunnah to enable choice among them for judgment. So that was an exceptional case (like the change of ijtihad of the court of cassation today is accepted, but it does not continue permanently out of concern for the principle of publicity of the system).
And if the judges in early Islam were mujtahids, that was an inevitable and unavoidable situation before the rules of fiqh, its principles and schools became settled. For the Companions and those who received from them of the Followers and their successors were scattered in the provinces, and each of them had knowledge of the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah ﷺ and his interpretation of the Qur'an that others did not have, as Malik رضي الله عنه said to al-Rashid. But after that, when fiqh was settled on its rules and schools, it is not permissible for the judge to remain free, choosing for judgment in every case what he himself sees as truth and justice, even if he is of the rank of ijtihad in knowledge and fiqh. Rather, he must be restricted to announced texts from which he does not deviate. His ijtihad then is confined to understanding the text and interpreting it according to the rules of understanding and interpretation in Usul al-Fiqh, as previously indicated, such that even the ijtihadi opinion of the judge in understanding the text should be known in advance to the mukallaf as much as possible. Hence, today in the world of law and judicial systems, the opinion of the court of cassation (the court of cassation) alone in interpreting the legal text is the binding judicial ijtihad, so that judicial ijtihad (like the legal text) is a single interpretative opinion known to the mukallaf.
As for the assumption that the judge must, according to Shari'ah, be left to his own ijtihad and judge accordingly, and that he cannot be restricted to a single Shari'ah opinion that he is obliged to judge by, this has no support.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work discusses the principle of publicity of law (علنية النظام) and its implications for codification and judicial practice. The author starts by establishing that knowledge of the law is a condition for valid obligation. Obliging someone to follow a secret, unpublished law is impossible (تكليف بما لا يطاق), forbidden by both Shari'ah and reason. Publicity ensures that the mukallaf can learn the law, so after promulgation, he is bound regardless of actual knowledge. This leads to the well-known rule: 'Ignorance of the law is no excuse.' The author then explains historical methods of promulgation (engravings, public displays, criers) and modern ones (official gazette). The passage then connects this principle to judicial organization: to maintain a single, predictable standard, there must be one supreme court that provides binding, uniform interpretation. The unity of judicial ijtihad parallels the unity of the legal text. Changes in interpretation are exceptional and limited. The author contrasts this with early Islam, where judges were necessarily mujtahids due to the undeveloped state of fiqh. But once fiqh crystallized, judges must be bound by codified, public texts, and their ijtihad is only in understanding the text according to established usul. Finally, the author rejects the notion that a judge must be free in ijtihad—this has no basis in Shari'ah. The overarching message is that codification and unified interpretation uphold the principle of publicity and justice.

Key Terms
علنية النظام (alaniyyat al-nizam): The state of a legal rule being publicly announced and accessible to the people, so that it can be known.
التكليف بما لا يطاق (al-taklif bima la yutaq): Obligating a person to do something impossible, which is prohibited in Shari'ah.
الاجتهاد القضائي الملزم (al-ijtihad al-qada'i al-mulzim): The binding judicial interpretation established by a single supreme court, which judges must follow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impermissible to obligate a mukallaf to follow a law that is not publicly announced?
A) Because the law might be unjust.
B) Because it is an obligation of what is impossible (تكليف بما لا يطاق).
C) Because the mukallaf cannot read.
D) Because the judge must decide each case individually.

Key Takeaway
Publicity of law is a condition for valid obligation; after announcement, ignorance is no excuse, and unified judicial interpretation is necessary to maintain this principle.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
The Legal Basis for Codification: Judge as Deputy of the Imam
Explains legitimacy and methodology of codifying fiqh for unified judiciary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi legal basis for binding judges to codified rulings based on the principle of agency (wakalah).

Core Idea
The judge is the Imam's deputy; thus bound by the Imam's chosen rulings, not personal ijtihad.

This passage establishes the foundational principle: judicial authority originates from the Imam (ruler). The judge, as his deputy, must follow the rulings prescribed by the Imam, just as an agent follows the principal. Codification is therefore legally valid and binding.

Guided Path
1
Trace the origin of judicial authority
Understand that judicial authority (al-qada) originally belonged to the Prophet ﷺ, then transferred to the Imam (caliph) after him.
The Imam may delegate it to judges.
2
Identify the relationship: judge = deputy (na'ib) of the Imam
The judge is legally a representative of the Imam; therefore he is bound by the Imam's instructions.
The text cites Kitab al-Qada for this principle.
3
Apply the agency principle
An agent must follow the restrictions set by the principal. If the Imam selects a valid madhhab opinion and orders judges to apply it, the judge cannot deviate using his own ijtihad.
This is a rational (aqli) derivation from the nature of agency.
4
Conclude the binding nature of codification
Codification is the process of the Imam/public authority selecting opinions and legislating them. It is legally valid and binding on judges.
This ends judicial chaos and ensures uniformity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملحق ثاني بالفصل (۲۲) تقنين الفقه والتقنين منه
متين من الفقه وإن المصلحة توجب خلافه وتدعو إلى التمسك بعلنية الأحكام كما أسلفنا.
ونصوص الفقهاء في كتاب القضاء صريحة بأن القضاء يتخصص بالزمان وبالمكان وبنوع القضايا (۱) ، وبالرأي المذهبي. فلو نصب قاض ليقضي بالمذهب الشافعي في أصح الأقوال فيه ليس له أن يقضي بسواه ولو خالف مذهبه هو . ذلك لأنَّ القضاء في الأصل شرعاً من حق الإمام الخليفة بعد أن كان لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في حياته .
وإن للإمام أن ينيب عنه في القضاء إذا كثرت الأعباء العامة عليه. فالقاضي شرعاً نائب الإمام أي وكيله والوكيل يتقيَّد بما يقيده به الموكل، لأنَّ الإرادة الأصلية للمنوب عنه، فإذا رأى صاحب السلطة الشرعية في النظام اختيار رأي شرعي معتبر يلزم به الناس نظاماً معلناً يطيعونه ويطبقونه، ويلزم نائبه القاضي أن يقضي به، فهذا صحيح شرعاً، وملزم للقاضي، وليس له تجاوزه والقضاء بسواه اجتهاداً منه .
وهذه المشكلة كانت قد بَرَزَت وحصل الانتباه إليها في أوائل العصر العباسي، ورفعها عبد الله بن المقفع إلى الخليفة المنصور في رسالة سماها (رسالة (الصحابة شرح له فيها أنه في البصرة يقضى بحل الأنكحة والأموال في حادثة، ويقضى في الكوفة بحرمتها لاختلاف اجتهاد القضاة. وأشار عليه بأن يوحد ما يقضى به بين الناس، وذلك بأن يأمر تلك الأقضية بجمع وآراء الفقهاء المتعارضة في مجموعة وترفع إليه في كتاب، ويرفع معها ما يحتج به كل قوم من سنة أو قياس، ثم ينظر هو فيها ويختار في كل قضية ما يلهمه الله أنه الصواب أو الأصوب، فيأمر بالعمل به وينهى عن القضاء بخلافه، فتصبح تلك الأحكام المختلطة الصواب بالخطأ، حكماً واحداً
صواباً. ويكون اجتماع السير قربة لاجتماع الأمر برأي أمير المؤمنين وعلى لسانه» (تنظر رسالة الصحابة لابن المقفع في جمهرة رسائل العرب» للأستاذ أحمد زكي صفوة (ج ٣ الرسالة/ ٢٦).
ولكل بلد إسلامي أن يقنن في إطار المذهب السائد فيه حيث ائتلفه أهله وأنسوا به، كما فعلت الدولة العثمانية في تقنين المجلة من المذهب الحنفي. وله أيضاً أن يختار أحكام المجموعة الفقهية القضائية من المذاهب المعتبرة بحسب ما يراه أكثر ملاءمة لحاجة البلد وتطورها في كل عصر .
مختلف:
فالمهم إنما هو توحيد الحكم القضائي من بين الآراء الفقهية المتعددة بصورة إلزامية للقضاة والمتقاضين في الدولة الواحدة، وتحقيق علنيته بأحسن الوسائل وأوثقها كما توجبه أصول التشريع، وذلك للقضاء على فوضى القضاء، وإن كان الأفضل عدم التقيد بمذهب واحد، لأنَّ في كل مذهب بعض مزايا وحلول أفضل نتيجة لأنه ليس أحد من أئمة هذه المذاهب وأتباعهم معصوماً ومحتماً أن يكون عنده الصواب وعند غيره الخطأ، أو أن يكون عنده أحسن الفهوم الاحتمالية جميعاً لنصوص القرآن والسنة النبوية والقواعد المستنبطة منهما. فعند كل منهم ما هو فاضل ومفضول، وما يضيق عنه مذهب من حاجات الزمن قد يتسع لحله مذهب آخر. فالعصبية المذهبية في فقه الشريعة بلية وغشاوة حاجبة للرؤية يجب أن تذهب إلى غير رجعة لكي تظهر في ميدان التطبيق غزارة الشريعة وفقهها، وقدرتهما على تلبية حاجات العصور، بالإضافة إلى مصلحة توحيد الحكم القضائي وعلنيته المسبقة ويبقى الاجتهاد مطلقاً للمفتي المؤهل لا للقاضي وينحصر اجتهاد القاضي بفهم النص المقنن فقط تحت رقابة محكمة التمييز توحيداً للاجتهاد الملزم
وانطلاقاً بالنظر والرؤية السديدة من هذه الزاوية الصحيحة القضاء سوياً واضحاً أميناً متمشياً مع المصلحة الشرعية والعملية .
يصبح
طريق
Second appendix to Chapter (22) Codification of fiqh and its codification: solid fiqh but interest (maslaha) requires otherwise and calls for adherence to the publicity of rulings as we stated. The texts of the jurists in the book of judiciary (Kitab al-Qada) explicitly state that judicial office is specialized by time, place, type of cases, and by madhhab opinion. If a judge is appointed to judge according to the Shafi'i school on its most correct opinion, he may not judge otherwise even if it contradicts his own madhhab. That is because judicial authority in origin, legally, belongs to the Imam (caliph) after it was for the Messenger of God (PBUH) during his life. And the Imam may delegate in judgment when public burdens increase upon him. So the judge legally is the deputy of the Imam, i.e., his agent, and the agent is bound by what the principal restricts him with, because the original will belongs to the one delegated. So if the holder of legal authority in the system sees fit to choose a valid legal opinion and imposes it upon the people as a public system that they obey and apply, and obliges his deputy the judge to judge by it, this is legally valid, binding upon the judge, and he may not exceed it and judge by something else through his own ijtihad. This problem had emerged and attention was drawn to it in the early Abbasid era, and 'Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa' raised it to the Caliph al-Mansur in a letter called (Risalah of the Companions) explaining that in Basra marriages and properties are judged permissible in an incident, and in Kufa they are judged forbidden due to differing ijtihad of judges. He suggested unifying what is judged among the people by ordering that those judicial opinions and conflicting juristic views be collected in a compilation and submitted to him in a book, along with each group's evidence from Sunnah or qiyas. Then he (the Caliph) would look into them and choose for each case what God inspires him as correct or most correct, then order to act by it and prohibit judging contrary to it, so that those mixed opinions—some correct, some erroneous—become one correct ruling. And the unification of conduct becomes a good deed because it unifies the matter by the opinion of the Commander of the Faithful and on his tongue. (See Risalah al-Sahabah by Ibn al-Muqaffa' in Jamharah Rasa'il al-Arab by Ahmad Zaki Safwah, vol. 3, letter 26). And every Islamic country may codify within the framework of the predominant madhhab where the people are familiar and accustomed to it, as the Ottoman state did in codifying the Majallah from the Hanafi school. It may also choose rulings of the judicial fiqh corpus from the authoritative madhahib according to what it deems more suitable for the country's need and development in every era. The important thing is to unify the judicial ruling among the multiple fiqh opinions in a binding manner for judges and litigants in one state, and to achieve its publicity through the best and most reliable means as required by the principles of legislation, in order to eliminate judicial chaos. However, it is better not to be restricted to one madhhab, because in every madhhab there are some advantages and better solutions since none of the imams of these madhahib and their followers are infallible; it is not necessarily that one has the truth and others have error, or that one has the best possible understandings of all the texts of the Qur'an, the Prophetic Sunnah, and the derived rules. Each of them has what is excellent and what is less excellent; what one madhhab cannot accommodate of the needs of time may be broad enough for another madhhab to solve. Hence, sectarian fanaticism in the fiqh of Shari'ah is a calamity and a veil obstructing vision that must disappear forever so that the richness of Shari'ah and its fiqh may appear in the field of application, and their ability to fulfill the needs of the times, in addition to the benefit of unifying the judicial ruling and its prior publicity. Ijtihad remains absolute for the qualified mufti, not for the judge; the judge's ijtihad is limited to understanding the codified text only, under the supervision of the Court of Cassation, to unify binding ijtihad. By looking and sound vision from this correct angle, the path of judiciary becomes straight, clear, honest, consistent with legal and practical interest.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical discussion on the codification (taqnin) of Islamic fiqh for judicial application. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by reaffirming that despite the solidity of fiqh texts, the principle of public interest (maslaha) may require codification to ensure consistency and publicity of rulings. He then provides the classical Hanafi legal basis: the judge is a deputy (na'ib) of the Imam, and as an agent, he is bound by the restrictions set by the principal. Therefore, if the ruler chooses a valid madhhab opinion and orders judges to follow it, it is binding, and the judge cannot deviate using his own ijtihad. This is supported by quotes from fiqh works on judiciary (Kitab al-Qada). He cites the historical example of Ibn al-Muqaffa' advising Caliph al-Mansur to unify judicial rulings to eliminate chaos. The author then states that each country may either codify within its predominant madhhab (as the Ottomans did with Hanafi Majallah) or choose from various madhahib as needed. Importantly, he argues against rigid adherence to one madhhab, advocating selection of the best from each school, while maintaining that the qadi's ijtihad is limited to understanding the codified text, with independent ijtihad reserved for qualified muftis. The overarching goal is to achieve unity and publicity of judicial rulings, ending judicial anarchy.

Key Terms
تقنين (taqnin): The process of codifying Islamic fiqh into statutory legal codes for application by courts.
القضاء (al-qada): Judicial authority and function, originally vested in the Prophet and then the Imam, delegated to judges.
الوكيل (al-wakil): Agent; the judge is considered the Imam's agent (wakil) in judicial matters.
الاجتهاد (ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning; in this text, it is restricted for judges in codified systems but remains open for muftis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a judge bound to follow the madhhab specified by the ruler?
A) Because the judge must follow his own personal madhhab
B) Because the judge is a deputy of the Imam and bound by the Imam's instructions
C) Because the judge has no right to ijtihad under any circumstances
D) Because the madhhab chosen is always the most correct

Key Takeaway
Codification is legally valid because the judge, as the Imam's deputy, must follow the ruler's prescribed rulings.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Methodologies of Codification: Single Madhhab vs. Multi-Madhhab Selection
Explains legitimacy and methodology of codifying fiqh for unified judiciary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between codifying within one madhhab and selecting from multiple madhahib, and evaluate the advantages of each approach.

Core Idea
Codification can follow one madhhab or select from many; the latter avoids fanaticism and meets diverse needs.

The passage presents two options for codifying fiqh: either within the framework of the predominant madhhab (like the Ottoman Majallah from the Hanafi school) or by selecting the best rulings from all authoritative madhahib. The author argues against rigid adherence to one school, advocating flexibility to harness the richness of the Shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first approach: single madhhab codification
Recognize that an Islamic country may codify within the predominant madhhab where the people are familiar and comfortable. Example: Ottoman Majallah from Hanafi school.
This approach provides stability and continuity.
2
Identify the second approach: multi-madhhab selection
A country may choose rulings from different madhahib according to what is most suitable for its needs and development in each era.
This allows for more tailored solutions.
3
Understand the rationale for flexibility
The author argues that no madhhab has a monopoly on truth; each has strengths and weaknesses. Fanaticism restricts the Shari'ah's ability to address new needs.
The goal is the public interest (maslaha) and practicality.
4
Evaluate both approaches
Consider the trade-offs: uniformity vs. flexibility, ease of application vs. legal comprehensiveness.
The choice depends on the country's circumstances.

Second appendix to Chapter (22) Codification of fiqh and its codification: solid fiqh but interest (maslaha) requires otherwise and calls for adherence to the publicity of rulings as we stated. The texts of the jurists in the book of judiciary (Kitab al-Qada) explicitly state that judicial office is specialized by time, place, type of cases, and by madhhab opinion. And the Imam may delegate in judgment when public burdens increase upon him. So the judge legally is the deputy of the Imam, i.e., his agent, and the agent is bound by what the principal restricts him with, because the original will belongs to the one delegated. So if the holder of legal authority in the system sees fit to choose a valid legal opinion and imposes it upon the people as a public system that they obey and apply, and obliges his deputy the judge to judge by it, this is legally valid, binding upon the judge, and he may not exceed it and judge by something else through his own ijtihad. This problem had emerged and attention was drawn to it in the early Abbasid era, and 'Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa' raised it to the Caliph al-Mansur in a letter called (Risalah of the Companions) explaining that in Basra marriages and properties are judged permissible in an incident, and in Kufa they are judged forbidden due to differing ijtihad of judges. He suggested unifying what is judged among the people by ordering that those judicial opinions and conflicting juristic views be collected in a compilation and submitted to him in a book, along with each group's evidence from Sunnah or qiyas. Then he (the Caliph) would look into them and choose for each case what God inspires him as correct or most correct, then order to act by it and prohibit judging contrary to it, so that those mixed opinions—some correct, some erroneous—become one correct ruling. And the unification of conduct becomes a good deed because it unifies the matter by the opinion of the Commander of the Faithful and on his tongue. (See Risalah al-Sahabah by Ibn al-Muqaffa' in Jamharah Rasa'il al-Arab by Ahmad Zaki Safwah, vol. 3, letter 26). And every Islamic country may codify within the framework of the predominant madhhab where the people are familiar and accustomed to it, as the Ottoman state did in codifying the Majallah from the Hanafi school. It may also choose rulings of the judicial fiqh corpus from the authoritative madhahib according to what it deems more suitable for the country's need and development in every era. The important thing is to unify the judicial ruling among the multiple fiqh opinions in a binding manner for judges and litigants in one state, and to achieve its publicity through the best and most reliable means as required by the principles of legislation, in order to eliminate judicial chaos. However, it is better not to be restricted to one madhhab, because in every madhhab there are some advantages and better solutions since none of the imams of these madhahib and their followers are infallible; it is not necessarily that one has the truth and others have error, or that one has the best possible understandings of all the texts of the Qur'an, the Prophetic Sunnah, and the derived rules. Each of them has what is excellent and what is less excellent; what one madhhab cannot accommodate of the needs of time may be broad enough for another madhhab to solve. Hence, sectarian fanaticism in the fiqh of Shari'ah is a calamity and a veil obstructing vision that must disappear forever so that the richness of Shari'ah and its fiqh may appear in the field of application, and their ability to fulfill the needs of the times, in addition to the benefit of unifying the judicial ruling and its prior publicity. Ijtihad remains absolute for the qualified mufti, not for the judge; the judge's ijtihad is limited to understanding the codified text only, under the supervision of the Court of Cassation, to unify binding ijtihad. By looking and sound vision from this correct angle, the path of judiciary becomes straight, clear, honest, consistent with legal and practical interest.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical discussion on the codification (taqnin) of Islamic fiqh for judicial application. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by reaffirming that despite the solidity of fiqh texts, the principle of public interest (maslaha) may require codification to ensure consistency and publicity of rulings. He then provides the classical Hanafi legal basis: the judge is a deputy (na'ib) of the Imam, and as an agent, he is bound by the restrictions set by the principal. Therefore, if the ruler chooses a valid madhhab opinion and orders judges to follow it, it is binding, and the judge cannot deviate using his own ijtihad. This is supported by quotes from fiqh works on judiciary (Kitab al-Qada). He cites the historical example of Ibn al-Muqaffa' advising Caliph al-Mansur to unify judicial rulings to eliminate chaos. The author then states that each country may either codify within its predominant madhhab (as the Ottomans did with Hanafi Majallah) or choose from various madhahib as needed. Importantly, he argues against rigid adherence to one madhhab, advocating selection of the best from each school, while maintaining that the qadi's ijtihad is limited to understanding the codified text, with independent ijtihad reserved for qualified muftis. The overarching goal is to achieve unity and publicity of judicial rulings, ending judicial anarchy.

Key Terms
المذهب (madhhab): A school of Islamic law; the text discusses selecting from multiple madhahib for codification.
التلفيق (talfiq): Combining rulings from different schools in a way that may produce contradictory results; the text implicitly cautions against it but permits careful selection.
المصلحة (maslaha): Public interest; a principle that justifies choosing rulings that best serve the community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is it better not to be restricted to one madhhab in codification?
A) Because a single madhhab cannot cover all areas of fiqh
B) Because each madhhab has advantages and no school is infallible
C) Because the Hanafi school is outdated
D) Because it is easier to apply multiple schools

Key Takeaway
Codification can be done within one madhhab or by selecting from multiple; the latter is often preferable to serve public interest.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Limits of Judicial Ijtihad and the Role of the Mufti
Explains legitimacy and methodology of codifying fiqh for unified judiciary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between a judge's limited ijtihad (understanding codified text) and a mufti's absolute ijtihad, and the role of the Court of Cassation.

Core Idea
In a codified system, judges interpret the code; muftis remain free to explore the full fiqh.

The passage clearly distinguishes the roles: the qualified mufti retains absolute ijtihad (within the framework of Shari'ah), while the judge's ijtihad is confined to understanding the codified text. This separation ensures consistency in the judiciary while allowing scholarly development through fatwa. The Court of Cassation supervises to unify interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actors: judge (qadi) and mufti
The text states: 'يبقى الاجتهاد مطلقاً للمفتي المؤهل لا للقاضي وينحصر اجتهاد القاضي بفهم النص المقنن فقط' – ijtihad remains absolute for the qualified mufti, not for the judge; the judge's ijtihad is limited to understanding the codified text.
This is a clear division of roles.
2
Understand the scope of judicial ijtihad
The judge's task is to interpret the codified law, not to derive new rulings from primary sources. This prevents arbitrary decisions.
The judge may need ijtihad in terms of interpreting ambiguous text.
3
Understand the scope of mufti's ijtihad
The mufti, being qualified, can engage in full ijtihad, drawing from the Quran, Sunnah, and principles of the madhhab. His fatwas are not binding on the court but guide individuals.
The mufti is not bound by the code; he can offer opinions based on other valid views.
4
Recognize the role of Court of Cassation
The text says 'تحت رقابة محكمة التمييز توحيداً للاجتهاد الملزم' – under the supervision of the Court of Cassation to unify binding ijtihad.
This ensures consistency even in interpretation of the code.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملحق ثاني بالفصل (۲۲) تقنين الفقه والتقنين منه
متين من الفقه وإن المصلحة توجب خلافه وتدعو إلى التمسك بعلنية الأحكام كما أسلفنا.
ونصوص الفقهاء في كتاب القضاء صريحة بأن القضاء يتخصص بالزمان وبالمكان وبنوع القضايا (۱) ، وبالرأي المذهبي. فلو نصب قاض ليقضي بالمذهب الشافعي في أصح الأقوال فيه ليس له أن يقضي بسواه ولو خالف مذهبه هو . ذلك لأنَّ القضاء في الأصل شرعاً من حق الإمام الخليفة بعد أن كان لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في حياته .
وإن للإمام أن ينيب عنه في القضاء إذا كثرت الأعباء العامة عليه. فالقاضي شرعاً نائب الإمام أي وكيله والوكيل يتقيَّد بما يقيده به الموكل، لأنَّ الإرادة الأصلية للمنوب عنه، فإذا رأى صاحب السلطة الشرعية في النظام اختيار رأي شرعي معتبر يلزم به الناس نظاماً معلناً يطيعونه ويطبقونه، ويلزم نائبه القاضي أن يقضي به، فهذا صحيح شرعاً، وملزم للقاضي، وليس له تجاوزه والقضاء بسواه اجتهاداً منه .
وهذه المشكلة كانت قد بَرَزَت وحصل الانتباه إليها في أوائل العصر العباسي، ورفعها عبد الله بن المقفع إلى الخليفة المنصور في رسالة سماها (رسالة (الصحابة شرح له فيها أنه في البصرة يقضى بحل الأنكحة والأموال في حادثة، ويقضى في الكوفة بحرمتها لاختلاف اجتهاد القضاة. وأشار عليه بأن يوحد ما يقضى به بين الناس، وذلك بأن يأمر تلك الأقضية بجمع وآراء الفقهاء المتعارضة في مجموعة وترفع إليه في كتاب، ويرفع معها ما يحتج به كل قوم من سنة أو قياس، ثم ينظر هو فيها ويختار في كل قضية ما يلهمه الله أنه الصواب أو الأصوب، فيأمر بالعمل به وينهى عن القضاء بخلافه، فتصبح تلك الأحكام المختلطة الصواب بالخطأ، حكماً واحداً
صواباً. ويكون اجتماع السير قربة لاجتماع الأمر برأي أمير المؤمنين وعلى لسانه» (تنظر رسالة الصحابة لابن المقفع في جمهرة رسائل العرب» للأستاذ أحمد زكي صفوة (ج ٣ الرسالة/ ٢٦).
ولكل بلد إسلامي أن يقنن في إطار المذهب السائد فيه حيث ائتلفه أهله وأنسوا به، كما فعلت الدولة العثمانية في تقنين المجلة من المذهب الحنفي. وله أيضاً أن يختار أحكام المجموعة الفقهية القضائية من المذاهب المعتبرة بحسب ما يراه أكثر ملاءمة لحاجة البلد وتطورها في كل عصر .
مختلف:
فالمهم إنما هو توحيد الحكم القضائي من بين الآراء الفقهية المتعددة بصورة إلزامية للقضاة والمتقاضين في الدولة الواحدة، وتحقيق علنيته بأحسن الوسائل وأوثقها كما توجبه أصول التشريع، وذلك للقضاء على فوضى القضاء، وإن كان الأفضل عدم التقيد بمذهب واحد، لأنَّ في كل مذهب بعض مزايا وحلول أفضل نتيجة لأنه ليس أحد من أئمة هذه المذاهب وأتباعهم معصوماً ومحتماً أن يكون عنده الصواب وعند غيره الخطأ، أو أن يكون عنده أحسن الفهوم الاحتمالية جميعاً لنصوص القرآن والسنة النبوية والقواعد المستنبطة منهما. فعند كل منهم ما هو فاضل ومفضول، وما يضيق عنه مذهب من حاجات الزمن قد يتسع لحله مذهب آخر. فالعصبية المذهبية في فقه الشريعة بلية وغشاوة حاجبة للرؤية يجب أن تذهب إلى غير رجعة لكي تظهر في ميدان التطبيق غزارة الشريعة وفقهها، وقدرتهما على تلبية حاجات العصور، بالإضافة إلى مصلحة توحيد الحكم القضائي وعلنيته المسبقة ويبقى الاجتهاد مطلقاً للمفتي المؤهل لا للقاضي وينحصر اجتهاد القاضي بفهم النص المقنن فقط تحت رقابة محكمة التمييز توحيداً للاجتهاد الملزم
وانطلاقاً بالنظر والرؤية السديدة من هذه الزاوية الصحيحة القضاء سوياً واضحاً أميناً متمشياً مع المصلحة الشرعية والعملية .

Second appendix to Chapter (22) Codification of fiqh and its codification: solid fiqh but interest (maslaha) requires otherwise and calls for adherence to the publicity of rulings as we stated. The texts of the jurists in the book of judiciary (Kitab al-Qada) explicitly state that judicial office is specialized by time, place, type of cases, and by madhhab opinion. If a judge is appointed to judge according to the Shafi'i school on its most correct opinion, he may not judge otherwise even if it contradicts his own madhhab. That is because judicial authority in origin, legally, belongs to the Imam (caliph) after it was for the Messenger of God (PBUH) during his life. And the Imam may delegate in judgment when public burdens increase upon him. So the judge legally is the deputy of the Imam, i.e., his agent, and the agent is bound by what the principal restricts him with, because the original will belongs to the one delegated. Key Terms
القاضي (al-qadi): Judge; in a codified system, his ijtihad is limited to understanding the codified text.
المفتي (al-mufti): A qualified scholar who issues fatwas; in a codified system, he retains full ijtihad.
الاجتهاد المقيد (al-ijtihad al-muqayyad): Limited ijtihad, such as understanding and applying a codified text.
محكمة التمييز (mahkamat al-tamyiz): Court of Cassation; highest court to unify interpretation of law.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Codification of Fiqh: Balancing Judicial Uniformity and Scholarly Diversity
Discussion on the necessity and benefits of codifying fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the advantages of codifying fiqh, explain the Hanafi view on taqnin within a madhhab, and evaluate the critique of rigid sectarianism.

Core Idea
Codification unifies judicial rulings without freezing ijtihad, allowing flexibility across madhhabs.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa’s work on fiqh methodology discusses the necessity and benefits of codifying Islamic jurisprudence. It addresses the permissibility of adopting rulings from different madhhabs and critiques sectarianism, while emphasizing that codification serves to unify judicial practice and make fiqh accessible. The passage also distinguishes the roles of the mufti (who retains absolute ijtihad) and the judge (who applies the codified text).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem of Judicial Chaos
The passage begins by stating the need to unify judicial rulings among multiple opinions to prevent chaos.
The problem arises due to the non-codified nature of fiqh.
2
Understand the Permissibility of Taqnin within a Madhhab
The author states that each country may codify within the prevailing madhhab or select from different madhhabs as needed.
Reference to Ottoman Majalla as Hanafi example.
3
Critique of Sectarianism
The passage argues that no madhhab is infallible and advocates flexibility to use better solutions from other schools.
'العصبية المذهبية' is a calamity.
4
Historical Precedent: Abu Yusuf's Policy
Abu Yusuf unified judiciary under Hanafi school as a forerunner to codification when fiqh was not codified.
This shows early recognition of need for uniformity.
5
List the Advantages of Codification
Dr. Zuhayli's two main advantages: ease of access and regulation/selection of best opinions.
These outweigh drawbacks.

الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه
صواباً. ويكون اجتماع السير قربة لاجتماع الأمر برأي أمير المؤمنين وعلى لسانه» (تنظر رسالة الصحابة لابن المقفع في جمهرة رسائل العرب» للأستاذ أحمد زكي صفوة (ج ٣ الرسالة/ ٢٦).
ولكل بلد إسلامي أن يقنن في إطار المذهب السائد فيه حيث ائتلفه أهله وأنسوا به، كما فعلت الدولة العثمانية في تقنين المجلة من المذهب الحنفي. وله أيضاً أن يختار أحكام المجموعة الفقهية القضائية من المذاهب المعتبرة بحسب ما يراه أكثر ملاءمة لحاجة البلد وتطورها في كل عصر .

ملحق ثاني بالفصل (۲۲) تقنين الفقه والتقنين منه
ومن ثم يتبيَّن لنا أنه إذا صح ما يقوله مؤرخو نشأة المذاهب الفقهية، وانتشارها من أن أبا يوسف القاضي صاحب أبي حنيفة حينما جعله الرشيد رئيساً للقضاء، وربط به القضاء كان لا ينصب قاضياً إلا من أتباع مذهب أبي حنيفة، فإنَّ عمله هذا يدل على بصيرة نافذة وإدراك عميق لمصلحة القضاء والمتقاضين، لأنه كان الطريق الوحيد لتوحيد الحكم القضائي الواجب عندما لا يكون الفقه مقنناً.

١٣/٢٢
- من مزايا التقنين من الفقه :
يرى جمهور الفقهاء والمعاصرين أنَّ مزايا التقنين وموجباته تفوق كثيراً ما يراه البعض من محاذيره (۱) . ويقول الأستاذ الدكتور وهبة الزحيلي معدداً مزايا التقنين ما خلاصته أن منها سهولة مراجعة الأحكام.
فمن المعروف أنَّ كتبنا الفقهية معروضة بأسلوب يختلف عن أسلوب العصر، وهي تغص باختلاف الآراء في القضية الواحدة. وهذا يجعل غير المتخصص (وهم أكثر الناس) في حَرَج وحَيْرة حين يريدون الأخذ بحكم فقهي . فإذا ما قُننت أحكام الفقه بعبارة سهلة مألوفة، ورتبت ترتيباً ميسراً فعندئذ يسهل على القاضي والفقيه والمحامي والمثقف العادي معرفة أحكام الشريعة في العقود والمعاملات.. وربما يزهد الكثيرون في مراجعة أحكام الشريعة التطبيقية لصعوبة الرجوع إلى كتب الفقهاء مفضلين الأخذ بالقوانين الوضعية الغربية المذللة الطريق في مراجعها وموسوعاتها .
وإلى جانب هذا التسهيل، فإنَّ تقنين الفقه يؤدي إلى ضبط الأحكام الشرعية واعتماد الرأي الأصلح من الآراء المختلفة بين المذاهب، بل وفي المذهب الواحد .
«فماذا يفعل القاضي أمام هذا الحشد الهائل من اختلاف الفقهاء؟
(۱) نقل الدكتور عبد البر في كتابه تقنين الفقه ص ٥٦ - ٥٩ أقوال عدد من الفقهاء المحدثين في ذلك.
Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh. It is correct. And the gathering of conduct is a near act for the gathering of the matter by the opinion of the Commander of the Faithful and by his tongue (see Ibn al-Muqaffa's 'Risalah al-Sahabah' in 'Jumhurat Rasa'il al-Arab' by Ahmad Zaki Safwah, vol. 3, letter 26). And every Islamic country may codify within the framework of the prevailing madhhab where its people are familiar and comfortable with it, as the Ottoman state did in the codification of the Majalla from the Hanafi madhhab. It may also choose the rulings of the judicial fiqh collection from the recognized madhhabs according to what it deems more suitable for the country's need and development in every era. Different: The important thing is to unify the judicial ruling among the multiple juristic opinions in a binding manner for judges and litigants in one state, and to achieve its publicity by the best and most reliable means as required by the principles of legislation, in order to eliminate judicial chaos. Although it is better not to be bound to one madhhab, because in every madhhab there are some advantages and better solutions, as none of the imams of these madhhabs and their followers is infallible or necessarily has the correct view while others err, nor does any one have all the best interpretive understandings of the texts of the Quran and the prophetic Sunnah and the deduced rules. For each of them has what is excellent and what is less excellent, and what a madhhab cannot accommodate from the needs of the times may be solved by another madhhab. So sectarianism in the fiqh of Shari'ah is a calamity and a blinding cover that must disappear permanently so that the abundance of Shari'ah and its fiqh may appear in the field of application, and their ability to meet the needs of the times, in addition to the interest of unifying the judicial ruling and its prior publicity. Ijtihad remains absolute for the qualified mufti, not for the judge, and the judge's ijtihad is confined to understanding the codified text only, under the supervision of the Court of Cassation, to unify binding ijtihad. And by starting with proper consideration and insight from this correct angle, the path of the judiciary becomes sound, clear, honest, consistent with the legal and practical interest. Second appendix to section (22): Codification of Fiqh and Codification from It. Hence it becomes clear to us that if what the historians of the emergence and spread of the juristic madhhabs say is true—that Abu Yusuf the judge, companion of Abu Hanifah, when al-Rashid made him chief of the judiciary and linked the judiciary to him, he would not appoint a judge except from the followers of the Hanafi madhhab—then this action of his indicates penetrating insight and deep understanding of the interest of the judiciary and litigants, because it was the only way to unify the necessary judicial ruling when fiqh was not codified. - 13/22 - From the advantages of codification from fiqh: The majority of jurists and contemporaries hold that the advantages and necessities of codification far outweigh what some see as its drawbacks (1). Professor Dr. Wahbah al-Zuhayli, enumerating the advantages of codification, says that its summary includes ease of reviewing rulings. It is well known that our fiqh books are presented in a style different from the style of the age, and they are filled with different opinions on the same issue. This makes the non-specialist (who are most people) in difficulty and confusion when they want to adopt a juristic ruling. If the rulings of fiqh are codified in an easy, familiar expression and arranged in an accessible order, then it becomes easy for the judge, jurist, lawyer, and ordinary educated person to know the Shari'ah rulings on contracts and transactions... And perhaps many people become averse to reviewing the applied Shari'ah rulings due to the difficulty of referring to the books of jurists, preferring to adopt Western positive laws that have smoothed the path in their references and encyclopedias. Alongside this facilitation, codification of fiqh leads to regulating the Shari'ah rulings and adopting the most appropriate opinion from among the different opinions between madhhabs, and even within the same madhhab. 'So what should the judge do before this vast crowd of jurists' differences?' (1) Dr. Abd al-Barr in his book 'Taqnin al-Fiqh' pp. 56-59 quotes the statements of a number of contemporary jurists on this.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage discusses the principles of codification (taqnin) of fiqh and its advantages. It begins by noting that every Islamic country may codify according to its prevailing madhhab, citing the Ottoman Majalla as an example. The author emphasizes the need to unify judicial rulings to prevent chaos, and argues that while adhering to one madhhab is not ideal since each has merits, codification serves the purpose of uniformity. The passage then relates that Abu Yusuf, as chief judge under Harun al-Rashid, appointed only Hanafi judges to achieve unity—a foresighted move when fiqh was not codified. Finally, it lists advantages of codification as enumerated by Dr. Wahba al-Zuhayli: ease of reference by presenting fiqh in a modern, accessible style; regulation of rulings; and adoption of the most suitable opinion from among the various madhhabs or even within one madhhab. The overall argument is that codification, despite some perceived drawbacks, is a practical necessity for modern judicial systems and does not preclude ijtihad for qualified muftis.

Key Terms
تقنين (taqnin): The process of codifying Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh) into statutory legal codes, as done with the Ottoman Majalla.
مذهب (madhhab): A school of Islamic jurisprudence; here, the prevailing school in a region (e.g., Hanafi in Ottoman lands).
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning; the passage reserves absolute ijtihad for the mufti, while the judge's role is limited to interpreting the codified text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is codification of fiqh beneficial despite potential drawbacks?
A) It makes fiqh accessible and unifies judicial rulings.
B) It eliminates all differences of opinion.
C) It prevents ijtihad entirely.
D) It forces all countries to follow one madhhab.

Key Takeaway
Codification of fiqh is a practical necessity for modern judiciaries, facilitating access, uniformity, and the selection of optimal rulings without stifling scholarly ijtihad.

Abu Yusuf's Judicial Policy: A Historical Precedent for Codification
Explains Abu Yusuf's judicial policy as a form of codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Abu Yusuf's policy of appointing only Hanafi judges was a de facto codification and argue for its necessity in unifying judicial practice.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf unified the judiciary by appointing Hanafi judges, showing codification's benefits centuries ago.

The passage opens with a historical observation: when Imam Abu Yusuf was appointed chief judge, he restricted judgeships to followers of the Hanafi school. This was not sectarianism but a practical step toward judicial uniformity, a precursor to modern codification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical event
Locate the description of Abu Yusuf's policy under Harun al-Rashid: he would not appoint any judge except from the followers of Abu Hanifa.
The passage treats this as a well-known fact from historians of the emergence of madhahib.
2
Understand the rationale
The passage states that this was the only way to unify judicial rulings when fiqh was not codified. Consider why uniformity in the judiciary is essential.
3
Relate to the concept of taqnin
Taqnin means codifying fiqh into a statutory code. Abu Yusuf's policy is a functional equivalent: it ensured that all judges applied the same school's rulings, creating predictability and consistency.
4
Evaluate the insight
The author calls this 'penetrating insight and deep awareness of the interest of the judiciary and litigants.' Why is this considered forward-thinking?
Consider that without codification, the only way to achieve uniformity was to unify the appointing authority behind one school.

Fiqh, Chapter Four: Derivation of contemporary codification from
Is it not obligatory then, in an age of speed and complexity of transactions and the multitude of cases presented before courts... is it not in the interest to facilitate the issuance of judgments? Indeed, the ijtihad required from the judge is the sound application of the codified Sharia ruling to complex facts. As for ijtihad in the broader sense, we leave it to those who select rulings at the time of codification. From the book Taqnin al-Fiqh al-Islami, Muassasat al-Risala, 1408 AH / 1987 CE, pp. 27-28, abridged and slightly adapted.)

Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy argument for the codification (taqnin) of Islamic fiqh. The author begins by noting that Abu Yusuf's policy of appointing only Hanafi judges when he was chief justice under Harun al-Rashid was a form of de facto codification, showing foresight in unifying judicial practice. The main thesis is that the merits of codification far outweigh its drawbacks. Citing Wahba al-Zuhayli, the author lists advantages: ease of reviewing rulings (since classical fiqh books are voluminous and full of differing opinions), accessibility for non-specialists (judges, lawyers, ordinary educated people), and the ability to select the most appropriate opinion from among different schools or within a single school. The passage argues that the sheer volume of juristic differences leaves judges perplexed, and in a modern era of speed and complex transactions, codification facilitates justice. The required ijtihad for a judge is the sound application of codified rulings to facts, while broader ijtihad is reserved for those who select rulings at the time of codification. This reflects the Hanafi tradition of prioritizing practical uniformity in the judiciary.

Key Terms
تقنين (taqnin): The codification of Islamic fiqh into a statutory legal code with simplified language and organized structure.
مذاهب فقهية (madhaahib fiqhiyyah): Schools of Islamic jurisprudence; the passage refers to the historical emergence of different schools and their spread.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author consider Abu Yusuf's policy of appointing only Hanafi judges as evidence for the merits of codification?
A) Because Abu Yusuf wanted to exclude other schools entirely.
B) Because it was the only way to unify judicial rulings in the absence of codified law.
C) Because Abu Yusuf was afraid of differing opinions.
D) Because Abu Yusuf was ordered by al-Rashid to do so.

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's policy was a pioneering form of codification, proving that unity in judicial practice was valued in early Hanafi tradition.

The Merits of Codifying Fiqh: Ease, Accessibility, and Uniformity
Lists the merits of codification of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain three major benefits of codifying fiqh as argued by Wahba al-Zuhayli and justify why codification outweighs its perceived drawbacks.

Core Idea
Codification makes fiqh accessible, selects the best opinions, and ends judicial confusion.

The second part of the passage, citing Professor Wahba al-Zuhayli, enumerates the practical advantages of codification: easing reference for non-specialists, enabling the adoption of the most appropriate ruling, and providing clear guidance for judges overwhelmed by juristic disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main benefits stated
Read the passage carefully and list the specific advantages mentioned: ease of reviewing rulings, facilitating access for non-specialists, ability to choose the most suitable opinion from among schools or within a single school, and solving the problem of judges facing conflicting opinions.
The benefits are interconnected; note the problem each solves.
2
Explain the problem: classical fiqh books
The passage describes classical fiqh books as having a style different from the contemporary age and 'full of differing opinions' on a single issue. Understand why this creates hardship for non-specialists and even judges.
'Non-specialist' includes judges, lawyers, and ordinary educated people.
3
Explain how codification solves each
Codification simplifies language, organizes rulings systematically, and selects one authoritative opinion per issue. This removes the burden of sifting through multiple views, making rulings accessible and predictable.
The selection of 'the most appropriate opinion' is a task for the codifiers, not the judge.
4
Relate to contemporary needs
The passage connects the need for codification to 'an age of speed and complexity of transactions and the multitude of cases.' Consider how modern legal systems require quick and consistent resolution.

The author begins by noting that Abu Yusuf's policy of appointing only Hanafi judges when he was chief justice under Harun al-Rashid was a form of de facto codification, showing foresight in unifying judicial practice. The main thesis is that the merits of codification far outweigh its drawbacks. Citing Wahba al-Zuhayli, the author lists advantages: ease of reviewing rulings (since classical fiqh books are voluminous and full of differing opinions), accessibility for non-specialists (judges, lawyers, ordinary educated people), and the ability to select the most appropriate opinion from among different schools or within a single school. The passage argues that the sheer volume of juristic differences leaves judges perplexed, and in a modern era of speed and complex transactions, codification facilitates justice. The required ijtihad for a judge is the sound application of codified rulings to facts, while broader ijtihad is reserved for those who select rulings at the time of codification. This reflects the Hanafi tradition of prioritizing practical uniformity in the judiciary.

Key Terms
تقنين (taqnin): The codification of Islamic fiqh into a statutory legal code with simplified language and organized structure.
مذاهب فقهية (madhaahib fiqhiyyah): Schools of Islamic jurisprudence; the passage refers to the historical emergence of different schools and their spread.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one of the main reasons for codifying fiqh?
A) To replace Sharia with secular law.
B) To make it easier for non-specialists to access fiqh rulings.
C) To eliminate all different juristic opinions.
D) To restrict ijtihad entirely.

Key Takeaway
The benefits of codification—ease of reference, selection of best opinions, and judicial clarity—make it a necessary tool for preserving and applying fiqh in modern contexts.

Ta'zir as a Basis for Modern Criminal Law
Argument for deriving modern penal codes from Sharia via ta'zir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ta'zir to evaluate the permissibility of modern penal code provisions for non-had crimes.

Core Idea
Ta'zir allows the state to codify punishments for non-had crimes based on public interest and circumstance.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa develops a key argument for the compatibility of modern criminal codes with Islamic Sharia. He identifies the foundation in the principle of ta'zir, a discretionary punishment classically recognized in Hanafi fiqh. As a mufti, you must understand how this principle bridges classical jurisprudence and contemporary legislation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of ta'zir
Determine which crimes fall under ta'zir: all crimes except the five hudud (zina, theft, qadhf, shurb al-khamr, hirabah) and qisas. Modern penal codes often include these as well, but their punishments are hadd, not ta'zir.
Hanafi: ta'zir cannot exceed the minimum hadd punishment for comparable offenses (e.g., flogging less than 80 lashes for drinking wine).
2
Apply the qa'idah of ta'zir
The qa'idah states that ta'zir is discretionary, left to the authorities based on the crime and circumstances. This flexibility allows codification of punishments like imprisonment or fines for crimes like fraud or bribery.
The qa'idah is a sughra (minor) maxim, governed by higher maxims like 'public interest' (maslahah).
3
Check for exceptions (istithna')
Ta'zir does not apply to hudud and qisas, which have fixed punishments from texts. Ensure the modern code does not alter these. Also, ta'zir cannot violate core Sharia principles (e.g., cruel or excessive punishments).
Istithna' block in qawida_panel lists hudud and qisas as exceptions.
4
Verify the principle of legality
The modern principle 'no crime without pre-announced text' is acceptable in Sharia because it aligns with the requirement of i'lan al-ahkam (public notification). Confirm that the code is published before enforcement.
Al-Zarqa explicitly accepts this principle as compatible.
5
Formulate the fatwa
State that the penal code provisions are permissible as ta'zir, provided they stay within Sharia limits (e.g., no exceeding hadd, no violating rights). The mufta bihi position is that such codification is commendable (mustahsan).
Cite the mufta bihi from fatwa_hierarchy.

Is it not obligatory then, while we are in an age of speed and complexity of transactions, and the multitude of cases presented to the courts... is it not in the public interest to facilitate the issuance of judgment? Indeed, the ijtihad required of the judge is the sound application of the codified legal ruling to complex facts. As for ijtihad in the broader sense, we leave it to those who select rulings at the time of codification. Appendix III to Chapter Twenty-Two
Unification of the Criminal System in Arab Countries
on the Basis of Islamic Sharia (1)
In my estimation, the criminal system represented today by the penal codes in the Arab countries can derive its provisions (except a few of them) from Islamic Sharia and its jurisprudence represented by the views of jurists from the various schools of jurisprudence.
14/22 - Ta'zir: Apart from the five fixed punishments (hudud): zina, theft, slander, drinking wine, and hirabah (i.e., highway robbery), and some cases of qisas, we can judge that what the penal codes contain is legally acceptable on the basis of the principle of ta'zir, which is a punishment not textually specified, but rather left to the discretion of the temporal authorities for each crime, according to it, and according to the circumstances of time and place. Rather, it can be said more than that: The provisions of the penal codes can be considered a legally commendable organization for the application of the principle of ta'zir. That is because the well-known legal principle that there is no crime or punishment except by a specific or general text announced before the act intended to be criminalized is a legally acceptable principle because it is based on the principle of the necessity of the publicity of the system before its application, a principle that is agreed upon.
(1) The Secretary-General of the Arab Organization for Social Defense against Crime requested from me, in a letter dated 27/12/1977, an idea and a vision on this topic, which I present here with slight modification.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy argument from Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a prominent Hanafi jurist. He addresses the challenge of modern codification of Islamic law. The author argues that modern penal codes in Arab countries can be largely derived from Islamic Sharia, particularly through the principle of ta'zir. Ta'zir is a discretionary punishment not fixed by a specific text; it is left to the authority's judgment based on the crime and circumstances. This adaptability allows the state to codify penal laws for crimes not covered by hudud or qisas. The author also defends the modern legal principle of 'nullum crimen, nulla poena sine lege' (no crime without law) as compatible with Sharia because it ensures prior announcement of the rule, which is a recognized principle in Islamic jurisprudence. The footnote indicates the article was written in response to a request from the Arab Organization for Social Defense.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): A discretionary punishment not fixed by a specific text, left to the authority's estimation for crimes that do not have fixed penalties.
قاعدة التعزير (qa'idat al-ta'zir): The legal maxim that ta'zir is a discretionary punishment left to the authorities, permissible as a basis for codifying non-had criminal laws.
لا جريمة ولا عقوبة إلا بنص (la jarima wa la 'uquba illa bi-nass): The principle that no act is a crime and no punishment is applicable except by virtue of a pre-announced legal text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary basis for accepting modern penal codes as permissible under Islamic law?
A) They are entirely derived from Quranic texts.
B) They fall under the principle of ta'zir, which allows discretionary punishment for non-had crimes.
C) They are based on ijma of classical jurists.
D) They are permitted only by emergency necessity.

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir provides a legitimate Sharia basis for modern criminal legislation, allowing the state to codify punishments for non-had crimes according to time and circumstance.
